Method of Learning
beginner
4 min
Linking by Condition (al-Jam' bil-Sharṭ)
Sanusi explains the third type with the example of will requiring knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third type of link, linking by condition, where a known relationship (conditioning) between attributes in the witnessed is applied to the absent to deduce a necessary attribute.

Core Idea
Linking by condition uses a conditional relationship to deduce an attribute that is prerequisite for another.

The third type is linking by condition. This method relies on a conditional relation: attribute A is a condition for attribute B in the witnessed world. If B is present in the absent (God), then A must also be present. Sanusi gives the example of will and knowledge: in the witnessed, will (irāda) requires intention (qaṣd), and intention requires knowledge. Since God is described as willing (murīd), He must have knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Pay attention to the logical structure: if B then A, because A is a condition for B.
The conditional is 'intention is conditioned by knowledge'.
2
Identify the condition
Determine which attribute is the condition (sharṭ) and which is the conditioned (mashrūṭ).
Knowledge (ʿilm) is the condition; intention (qaṣd) is the conditioned.
3
Apply the analogy
Since God has will (irāda), and will implies intention, and intention requires knowledge, God must have knowledge.
This is a chain of conditionals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مطلب : في الجوامع الأربعةِ في قياس الغائب على الشاهدِ ] قالوا : والجوامع أربعة (١) : جمع بالحقيقة : كقولهم : العالِمُ شاهداً : مَنْ لهُ العلم ، أو ذو العلم ، والبارى عالم ، فله علم ، وهذهِ عمده مَنْ ينفي الأحوال والجمع بالدليل : كقولهم : الإحكام شاهداً : دليل في العقل على أنَّ لفاعلِهِ علماً بهِ ، والبارى تعالى مُحكِمٌ مُتقِنُ لأفعالِهِ ، فدل على أنَّ له علماً والجمع بالشرط : كقولهم: البارى تعالى مريد ، وكل مريد قاصد لفعله ، والقصد مشروط بالعلم ، فالبارى تعالى له علم ، وإلا لثبتَ المشروط بدونِ الشرط . والجمعُ بالعلَّة - وهو عمدةُ مَنْ يثبتُ الأحوال - : كقولهم : العلم والعالمية متلازمانِ ، والعالميَّةُ مرتبةٌ على العلم ، وقد ساعدتُم على إثباتِ العالميَّةِ غائباً ، فيلزمُ مِنْ إثبات العالمية العلم ؛ فإنَّ التلازم ثابتُ بينَهما مِنَ الجانبين ، فلو صح وجود عالميَّةٍ ولا علم .. لصح ثبوت علم ولا عالمية ، ولا يقولونَ بهِ (1) الجوامع : جمع جامع ؛ وهو الأمر الذي يكون سبباً في انسحاب حكم المقيس عليه على المقيس ، فالجامع غير الحكم . حامدي » ( ص ۲۲۱ ) ، وانظر » تحرير هذه القسمة في ) شرح الإرشاد ) للعلامة المقترح ( ص ١٥٩ ) . ٣١٤
Topic: On the four universals (jawāmi') in the analogical reasoning from the absent to the witnessed. They said: The universals are four: (1) Linking by essence (al-jam' bil-ḥaqīqa): as they say: The knower in the witnessed world is one who has knowledge, or possesses knowledge, and the Creator is knowing, so He has knowledge. This is the mainstay of those who deny states (aḥwāl). (2) Linking by evidence (al-jam' bil-dalīl): as they say: The perfection of craftsmanship in the witnessed world is an intellectual evidence that its agent has knowledge of it, and the Creator, exalted, is perfect in His actions, so it indicates that He has knowledge. (3) Linking by condition (al-jam' bil-sharṭ): as they say: The Creator, exalted, is willing, and every willing one intends his action, and intention is conditioned by knowledge, so the Creator, exalted, has knowledge; otherwise the conditioned would exist without the condition. (4) Linking by cause (al-jam' bil-'illa) – and this is the mainstay of those who affirm states: as they say: Knowledge and knowership (al-'ālimiyya) are mutually entailing, and knowership is dependent on knowledge, and you have agreed to affirm knowership in the absent, so from the affirmation of knowership follows knowledge; for the mutual entailment is established between them from both sides. If a knowership without knowledge were valid, then a knowledge without knowership would be valid, and they do not say that. (1) al-jawāmi': plural of jāmi'; it is the thing that is the reason for the extension of the ruling of the analogized-from to the analogized-to; so the jāmi' is not the ruling. Ḥāmidī (p. 221), and see the analysis of this division in Sharḥ al-Irshād by al-Muqtraḥ (p. 159).
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is a methodological classification. The author presents the four types of 'jāmi'' (link or common factor) used in qiyās al-ghā'ib 'alā al-shāhid, i.e., reasoning from the observed world (the witnessed) to the unseen divine realm (the absent). Each type is defined with an example related to the divine attribute of knowledge. The first type is linking by essence (al-jam' bil-ḥaqīqa): it asserts a common essential attribute, e.g., both the human knower and God are described as 'knowing', so God must have knowledge. This is the method of those who deny the ontological reality of states (aḥwāl). The second type is linking by evidence (al-jam' bil-dalīl): the perfection of craftsmanship in the observed world indicates that its agent has knowledge; God's perfect creation similarly indicates knowledge. The third type is linking by condition (al-jam' bil-sharṭ): every willing agent intends his action, and intention requires knowledge; since God is willing, He must have knowledge, otherwise a conditioned thing would exist without its condition, which is impossible. The fourth type is linking by cause (al-jam' bil-'illa): it posits mutual entailment between two attributes, here knowledge and 'knowership' (al-'ālimiyya). Since the opponent agrees that God has knowership (the attribute of being knowing), and knowership necessarily entails knowledge due to their mutual entailment, God must have knowledge. This is the method of those who affirm states (aḥwāl). The footnote explains the term 'jāmi'' and provides references.

Key Terms
الجمع بالشرط (al-jam' bil-sharṭ)
الشرط (al-sharṭ)
المشروط (al-mashrūṭ)
القصد (al-qaṣd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the conditional relationship is from the witnessed to the absent directly without logical necessity.
Correction: The argument uses a universal conditional: all willing beings intend, and all intending requires knowledge. This is a necessary relation, not an empirical generalization.

Checkpoint
In linking by condition, what is the condition that must be present for intention to exist?
A) Will
B) Power
C) Knowledge
D) Life

Key Takeaway
Linking by condition deduces a necessary attribute based on a conditional relationship observed in the witnessed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that intention (qasd) requires knowledge. Do you truly intend your actions with knowledge of their consequences before God? Or do you often act without reflection? How can you cultivate deliberate intention in your daily life, starting today?
?
God's will is perfectly linked to knowledge. Have you ever acted out of will without seeking knowledge of what is pleasing to Allah? What does that reveal about your own relationship with your Creator? Commit to one practical step to align your will with knowledge.
?
The conditional link is necessary: the conditioned cannot exist without its condition. Examine your own faith: Is your intention (qasd) to obey Allah conditioned on seeking knowledge? Or do you sometimes assume you know without learning? What will you change?

Method of Learning
beginner
5 min
Linking by Cause (al-Jam' bil-'Illa)
Sanusi explains the fourth type using mutual entailment between knowledge and knowership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth type of link, linking by cause, which relies on mutual entailment between two attributes to deduce one from the other.

Core Idea
Linking by cause uses the mutual entailment of two attributes to infer knowledge from knowership.

The fourth type is linking by cause, described as the mainstay of those who affirm states (aḥwāl). Sanusi presents an example involving knowledge (ʿilm) and knowership (ʿālimiyya). The two are claimed to be mutually entailing: wherever there is knowership, there is knowledge, and vice versa. Since the opponent already affirms knowership for God, knowledge follows necessarily. This method is used by those who believe that states are real ontological entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that this type is longer and mentions mutual entailment (talāzum).
The key term is 'mutual entailment' (talāzum).
2
Understand the logic
The argument: knowledge and knowership entail each other. The opponent already accepts knowership in God. Therefore, knowledge must be affirmed.
This is a symmetrical relationship.
3
Identify the link
The link (jāmi') here is the mutual entailment relationship itself, applied analogically from the witnessed to the absent.
It is a causal link in the sense of necessary connection.
4
Note the theological position
This method is associated with those who affirm states (aḥwāl). Reflect on why: it requires intermediate concepts like 'knowership' as a state.
States are extra-mental realities for this group.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مطلب : في الجوامع الأربعةِ في قياس الغائب على الشاهدِ ] قالوا : والجوامع أربعة (١) : جمع بالحقيقة : كقولهم : العالِمُ شاهداً : مَنْ لهُ العلم ، أو ذو العلم ، والبارى عالم ، فله علم ، وهذهِ عمده مَنْ ينفي الأحوال والجمع بالدليل : كقولهم : الإحكام شاهداً : دليل في العقل على أنَّ لفاعلِهِ علماً بهِ ، والبارى تعالى مُحكِمٌ مُتقِنُ لأفعالِهِ ، فدل على أنَّ له علماً والجمع بالشرط : كقولهم: البارى تعالى مريد ، وكل مريد قاصد لفعله ، والقصد مشروط بالعلم ، فالبارى تعالى له علم ، وإلا لثبتَ المشروط بدونِ الشرط . والجمعُ بالعلَّة - وهو عمدةُ مَنْ يثبتُ الأحوال - : كقولهم : العلم والعالمية متلازمانِ ، والعالميَّةُ مرتبةٌ على العلم ، وقد ساعدتُم على إثباتِ العالميَّةِ غائباً ، فيلزمُ مِنْ إثبات العالمية العلم ؛ فإنَّ التلازم ثابتُ بينَهما مِنَ الجانبين ، فلو صح وجود عالميَّةٍ ولا علم .. لصح ثبوت علم ولا عالمية ، ولا يقولونَ بهِ (1) الجوامع : جمع جامع ؛ وهو الأمر الذي يكون سبباً في انسحاب حكم المقيس عليه على المقيس ، فالجامع غير الحكم . حامدي » ( ص ۲۲۱ ) ، وانظر » تحرير هذه القسمة في ) شرح الإرشاد ) للعلامة المقترح ( ص ١٥٩ ) . ٣١٤
Topic: On the four universals (jawāmi') in the analogical reasoning from the absent to the witnessed. They said: The universals are four: (1) Linking by essence (al-jam' bil-ḥaqīqa): as they say: The knower in the witnessed world is one who has knowledge, or possesses knowledge, and the Creator is knowing, so He has knowledge. This is the mainstay of those who deny states (aḥwāl). (2) Linking by evidence (al-jam' bil-dalīl): as they say: The perfection of craftsmanship in the witnessed world is an intellectual evidence that its agent has knowledge of it, and the Creator, exalted, is perfect in His actions, so it indicates that He has knowledge. (3) Linking by condition (al-jam' bil-sharṭ): as they say: The Creator, exalted, is willing, and every willing one intends his action, and intention is conditioned by knowledge, so the Creator, exalted, has knowledge; otherwise the conditioned would exist without the condition. (4) Linking by cause (al-jam' bil-'illa) – and this is the mainstay of those who affirm states: as they say: Knowledge and knowership (al-'ālimiyya) are mutually entailing, and knowership is dependent on knowledge, and you have agreed to affirm knowership in the absent, so from the affirmation of knowership follows knowledge; for the mutual entailment is established between them from both sides. If a knowership without knowledge were valid, then a knowledge without knowership would be valid, and they do not say that. (1) al-jawāmi': plural of jāmi'; it is the thing that is the reason for the extension of the ruling of the analogized-from to the analogized-to; so the jāmi' is not the ruling. Commentary
This passage is a methodological classification. The author presents the four types of 'jāmi'' (link or common factor) used in qiyās al-ghā'ib 'alā al-shāhid, i.e., reasoning from the observed world (the witnessed) to the unseen divine realm (the absent). Each type is defined with an example related to the divine attribute of knowledge. The first type is linking by essence (al-jam' bil-ḥaqīqa): it asserts a common essential attribute, e.g., both the human knower and God are described as 'knowing', so God must have knowledge. This is the method of those who deny the ontological reality of states (aḥwāl). The second type is linking by evidence (al-jam' bil-dalīl): the perfection of craftsmanship in the observed world indicates that its agent has knowledge; God's perfect creation similarly indicates knowledge. The third type is linking by condition (al-jam' bil-sharṭ): every willing agent intends his action, and intention requires knowledge; since God is willing, He must have knowledge, otherwise a conditioned thing would exist without its condition, which is impossible. The fourth type is linking by cause (al-jam' bil-'illa): it posits mutual entailment between two attributes, here knowledge and 'knowership' (al-'ālimiyya). Since the opponent agrees that God has knowership (the attribute of being knowing), and knowership necessarily entails knowledge due to their mutual entailment, God must have knowledge. This is the method of those who affirm states (aḥwāl). The footnote explains the term 'jāmi'' and provides references.

Key Terms
الجمع بالعلة (al-jam' bil-'illa)
التلازم (al-talāzum)
العالمية (al-'ālimiyya)
الأحوال (al-aḥwāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that linking by cause involves a causal relationship in the ordinary sense (like efficient causation).
Correction: Here 'cause' means 'the reason for making the analogy valid', specifically through mutual entailment (talāzum), not temporal causation.

Checkpoint
Which group primarily uses linking by cause according to the passage?
A) Those who deny states
B) Those who affirm states
C) The Mu'tazila
D) The Jahmiyya

Key Takeaway
Linking by cause deduces a necessary attribute through mutual entailment, often used by those who affirm states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi argues that if you affirm God's knowership, you must also affirm His knowledge. Do you truly believe that God knows every detail of your life, including your private thoughts and hidden intentions? How would your actions today change if you lived with absolute certainty of that knowledge? Commit to one specific change this week.
?
The mutual entailment means that denying God's knowledge would be as absurd as denying His knowership. Have you ever acted as though God does not see or know what you do? Reflect on a moment when you behaved as if you could hide something from Him. What would it take for you to live consistently with the truth that He knows all?
?
If the logical link between God's knowership and His knowledge is so tight, then knowing that He is the All-Knowing should produce awe and accountability in you. Does it? Or have you become comfortable with the concept? What is one practical step you can take this week to make the reality of God's knowledge more present in your daily choices?

Method of Learning
intermediate
3 min
The General Conceptual Objection Refuted
Sanusi refutes the general conceptual objection to the proof of prophetic knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the weakness of an inductive claim that denies all knowledge beyond one's own list, and how Sanusi refutes it.

Core Idea
A sweeping inductive claim cannot disprove what lies beyond its scope.

The objector claims that his own investigation shows no human ever had knowledge beyond what he listed. This seems like a strong empirical argument, but Sanusi points out its logical flaw. He then prepares to introduce a counterexample from the Sufi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the objector's claim in Arabic and English. Notice the sweeping nature of his induction.
Claim: he knows that no human from Adam to the last has knowledge beyond his list.
2
Examine the refutation
Sanusi calls the claim obviously fallacious. Then he considers a weaker version: the objector only speaks from his own limited sample. That doesn't prove anything for all humans.
The weaker version is not a strong argument.
3
Transition to the Sufi counterexample
The refutation ends by noting that this claim is opposed by the Sufi claim. This sets up the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الاعتراضُ الجُمْليُّ المعنوي على هذا الدليل ادعى في استقرائِهِ أنَّهُ يعلمُ أنَّهُ لا علم عندَ أحدٍ مِنَ البشرِ مِنْ آدَمَ إِلى آخرِ مميّز يُوجَدُ مِنَ البشرِ سوى ما ذكرَهُ .. فلا يخفى سقوط هذهِ الدعوى ، وإنِ ادَّعى أنَّ هذا هو الذي وجدَهُ فيمَنِ استقرأَهُ مِنَ البشر .. فلا يفيدُ أنَّ الحاصل لجميع البشرِ ليس إلا ذلكَ ويعارضه ما تدَّعيه الصوفية : مِنْ أنَّ الرياضة بعد تصحيح العقيدة ، وإحكام الفرائضِ ، وتناول الحلال ، بالخلوة والعزلة والصومِ ، ودوام الذكرِ على طهارةِ الظاهر والباطن ، وصدق الافتقارِ إلى الله تعالى بتركِ الدعوى، والتبرّي مِنَ الحولِ والقُوَّةِ ظاهراً وباطناً . . سبب - بمشيئة الله تعالى - للزيادة في المعارفِ ؛ كما قال تعالى : ﴿ وَالَّذِينَ جَهَدُوا فِينَا لَنَهْدِيَنَّهُمْ سُبُلَنَا ﴾ [العنكبوت : ٦٩] ، وقال تعالى : ( أُولَيكَ كَتَبَ فِي قُلُوبِهِمُ الْإِيمَانَ وَأَيَّدَهُم بِرُوحٍ مِنْهُ ) [المجادلة : ٢٢]. ويُعبّرونَ عن ذلك بالروح والنور وعين السرّ ؛ وهو مرآة تجليات وكشوف الأمورٍ بخَلْقِ علوم لا سبيل للاطلاع عليها بالاستدلال ولا بطرق الاعتبار ، بل بمحض إنعام وإلهام ؛ بخلقِ علوم لم تجرِ العادة بخلقها ، ولا يعرفُها إلا أهلها ، ولا يعرفها غيرهم كما لا يعرفُ الأكمَهُ حقائق الألوانِ ، ولا سبيل إلى تعريفها بالقول للغيرِ ، بل بإشارة العارفِ للعارفِ ، كما قيل : [ من الطويل] ( و تشيرُ فَأَدْرِي مَا تَقُولُ بِطَرْفِهَا وَأُطْرقُ طَرْفِي عِنْدَ ذَاكَ فَتَفْهَمُ )
Then the general conceptual objection to this proof: he claimed in his induction that he knows that there is no knowledge with any human from Adam to the last discerning human that exists among humans except what he mentioned. So the fallaciousness of this claim is not hidden. And if he claims that this is what he found in those he examined among humans, then it does not prove that the result for all humans is only that. And it is opposed by what the Sufis claim: that spiritual discipline after correcting belief, perfecting obligations, consuming what is lawful, through seclusion and isolation and fasting, constant remembrance with purity of outward and inward, truthfulness of neediness towards Allah by abandoning pretension, and disavowing power and strength outwardly and inwardly, is a cause, by the will of Allah, for increase in gnoses; as Allah said: 'And those who strive in Us, We will surely guide them to Our ways' [29:69], and He said: 'Those are the ones in whose hearts He has inscribed faith and supported them with a spirit from Him' [58:22]. And they express that as the spirit, the light, and the eye of the secret; and it is a mirror of self-disclosures and uncoverings of matters by the creation of knowledges for which there is no way to access through reasoning nor by methods of reflection, but solely by favor and inspiration; by the creation of knowledges whose creation is not customary, and none knows them but their people, and others do not know them just as the blind does not know the realities of colors, and there is no way to define them by speech for others, but only by the allusion of the knower to the knower, as it is said in the long meter: 'She signals and I understand what she says with her eye, and I lower my gaze then she understands.'
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objector makes a sweeping inductive claim that no human has ever had any knowledge beyond what he listed. Sanusi points out that this claim is obviously false. If the objector only claims that he personally found no such knowledge, that does not prove that all humans are limited to that. Then Sanusi presents the Sufi claim as a counterexample: through spiritual discipline after proper belief and practice, one can attain knowledge beyond ordinary reasoning. He supports this with Quranic verses. The Sufis describe this as a direct creation of knowledge by Allah, not accessible through deduction. They compare it to colors which the blind cannot perceive. Such knowledge can only be transmitted by allusion between knowers. A poetic example is given.

Key Terms
الاعتراض الجملي
الاستقراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw in the objector's claim according to Sanusi?
A) His induction is too limited
B) He did not study the Sufis
C) The claim is obviously fallacious and even if limited, does not cover all humans
D) He forgot to include himself

Key Takeaway
An inductive claim that denies all knowledge beyond one's own list is logically weak and cannot stand as a refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a truth because it didn't match your personal experience, assuming your limited exposure is exhaustive? Commit to actively investigating claims you find uncomfortable instead of dismissing them.
?
This lesson exposes how a seemingly strong inductive argument can be logically flawed. In what area of your own life—perhaps in your understanding of God's mercy, your view of others, or your religious practice—are you relying on weak evidence without realizing it? What concrete step will you take this week to seek stronger certainty?
?
The objector assumed he had the full picture. When you encounter a perspective that challenges your current beliefs, do you reflexively defend your position, or do you honestly ask, 'What if I am missing something?' Pray for the humility to admit ignorance and the courage to pursue truth wherever it leads.

Method of Learning

5 min
The Sufi Path to Gnosis
Sanusi presents the Sufi claim of spiritual knowledge as a counterexample to the objector's induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how spiritual discipline according to the Sufis leads to knowledge beyond ordinary reasoning, and why this is presented as a counterexample.

Core Idea
Spiritual discipline can unlock knowledge inaccessible to reason alone.

Having refuted the objector's induction, Sanusi now presents the Sufi claim as a direct counterexample. The Sufis assert that through specific spiritual practices, Allah grants direct knowledge that cannot be obtained through deduction or reflection. This claim is supported by Quranic verses and described as an illumination.

Guided Path
1
Read the Sufi claim
Read the Arabic and translation of the Sufi claim. Notice the detailed conditions for spiritual discipline.
Conditions: correct belief, perfecting obligations, lawful sustenance, seclusion, fasting, constant remembrance, sincerity, etc.
2
Examine the Quranic support
The passage cites two Quranic verses. Reflect on how they support the idea that striving leads to guidance and faith is inscribed in hearts.
Verse 29:69 and 58:22.
3
Understand the nature of this knowledge
Sanusi explains that this knowledge is created directly by Allah, not through reasoning. It is like colors to the blind—those without it cannot grasp it.
Communication between knowers is by allusion, not direct speech.

ثم الاعتراضُ الجُمْليُّ المعنوي على هذا الدليل ادعى في استقرائِهِ أنَّهُ يعلمُ أنَّهُ لا علم عندَ أحدٍ مِنَ البشرِ مِنْ آدَمَ إِلى آخرِ مميّز يُوجَدُ مِنَ البشرِ سوى ما ذكرَهُ .. فلا يخفى سقوط هذهِ الدعوى ، وإنِ ادَّعى أنَّ هذا هو الذي وجدَهُ فيمَنِ استقرأَهُ مِنَ البشر .. فلا يفيدُ أنَّ الحاصل لجميع البشرِ ليس إلا ذلكَ ويعارضه ما تدَّعيه الصوفية : مِنْ أنَّ الرياضة بعد تصحيح العقيدة ، وإحكام الفرائضِ ، وتناول الحلال ، بالخلوة والعزلة والصومِ ، ودوام الذكرِ على طهارةِ الظاهر والباطن ، وصدق الافتقارِ إلى الله تعالى بتركِ الدعوى، والتبرّي مِنَ الحولِ والقُوَّةِ ظاهراً وباطناً . . سبب - بمشيئة الله تعالى - للزيادة في المعارفِ ؛ كما قال تعالى : ﴿ وَالَّذِينَ جَهَدُوا فِينَا لَنَهْدِيَنَّهُمْ سُبُلَنَا ﴾ [العنكبوت : ٦٩] ، وقال تعالى : ( أُولَيكَ كَتَبَ فِي قُلُوبِهِمُ الْإِيمَانَ وَأَيَّدَهُم بِرُوحٍ مِنْهُ ) [المجادلة : ٢٢]. ويُعبّرونَ عن ذلك بالروح والنور وعين السرّ ؛ وهو مرآة تجليات وكشوف الأمورٍ بخَلْقِ علوم لا سبيل للاطلاع عليها بالاستدلال ولا بطرق الاعتبار ، بل بمحض إنعام وإلهام ؛ بخلقِ علوم لم تجرِ العادة بخلقها ، ولا يعرفُها إلا أهلها ، ولا يعرفها غيرهم كما لا يعرفُ الأكمَهُ حقائق الألوانِ ، ولا سبيل إلى تعريفها بالقول للغيرِ ، بل بإشارة العارفِ للعارفِ ، كما قيل : [ من الطويل] ( و تشيرُ فَأَدْرِي مَا تَقُولُ بِطَرْفِهَا وَأُطْرقُ طَرْفِي عِنْدَ ذَاكَ فَتَفْهَمُ )
Then the general conceptual objection to this proof: he claimed in his induction that he knows that there is no knowledge with any human from Adam to the last discerning human that exists among humans except what he mentioned. So the fallaciousness of this claim is not hidden. And if he claims that this is what he found in those he examined among humans, then it does not prove that the result for all humans is only that. And it is opposed by what the Sufis claim: that spiritual discipline after correcting belief, perfecting obligations, consuming what is lawful, through seclusion and isolation and fasting, constant remembrance with purity of outward and inward, truthfulness of neediness towards Allah by abandoning pretension, and disavowing power and strength outwardly and inwardly, is a cause, by the will of Allah, for increase in gnoses; as Allah said: 'And those who strive in Us, We will surely guide them to Our ways' [29:69], and He said: 'Those are the ones in whose hearts He has inscribed faith and supported them with a spirit from Him' [58:22]. And they express that as the spirit, the light, and the eye of the secret; and it is a mirror of self-disclosures and uncoverings of matters by the creation of knowledges for which there is no way to access through reasoning nor by methods of reflection, but solely by favor and inspiration; by the creation of knowledges whose creation is not customary, and none knows them but their people, and others do not know them just as the blind does not know the realities of colors, and there is no way to define them by speech for others, but only by the allusion of the knower to the knower, as it is said in the long meter: 'She signals and I understand what she says with her eye, and I lower my gaze then she understands.'
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objector makes a sweeping inductive claim that no human has ever had any knowledge beyond what he listed. Sanusi points out that this claim is obviously false. If the objector only claims that he personally found no such knowledge, that does not prove that all humans are limited to that. Then Sanusi presents the Sufi claim as a counterexample: through spiritual discipline after proper belief and practice, one can attain knowledge beyond ordinary reasoning. He supports this with Quranic verses. The Sufis describe this as a direct creation of knowledge by Allah, not accessible through deduction. They compare it to colors which the blind cannot perceive. Such knowledge can only be transmitted by allusion between knowers. A poetic example is given.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that spiritual experiences are not valid sources of knowledge or that they can be communicated plainly to everyone.
Correction: Sanusi argues that this knowledge is real but not accessible through ordinary reasoning, and can only be alluded to between those who have experienced it.

Checkpoint
According to the Sufi claim in the passage, what is the means of gaining this special knowledge?
A) Intensive reasoning and reflection
B) Spiritual discipline combined with Allah's will
C) Reading books of philosophy
D) Consulting the objector's induction

Key Takeaway
The Sufi claim demonstrates that there is knowledge beyond ordinary reason, attained through spiritual discipline and divine favor, thus refuting the objector's limited induction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says that spiritual discipline increases gnosis by Allah's will. Ask yourself: am I even striving in this path, or have I settled for mere intellectual belief? What specific step of the discipline—like seclusion, fasting, or constant dhikr—am I neglecting, and will I commit to it today?
?
The Sufis assert that this knowledge is only communicated by allusion between knowers. Do you have a living guide or teacher who can point you to that reality? If not, what is your plan to find one, knowing that true knowledge of Allah is not found in books alone?
?
Consider the blind man who cannot perceive colors. Is my heart blind to the realities that the Sufis describe? What attachment to this world or pride in my own intellect is keeping me from the openness and neediness required to receive divine inspiration?

Spiritual Knowledge as Direct Divine Illumination
Sanusi describes the means and nature of spiritual knowledge and defends it against the charge of incarnation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how spiritual knowledge (maʿārif) is acquired through divine illumination rather than rational inference, and why it does not imply incarnation.

Core Idea
Spiritual knowledge is a direct gift from God, not a product of reasoning.

Sanusi now turns to a topic that may seem unfamiliar: how the saints and prophets gain knowledge that goes beyond ordinary reasoning. He describes a path of purification that leads to divine illumination, producing sciences that cannot be reached by inference or reflection. But he also warns against misunderstanding this as a form of incarnation. Let's read his careful explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Notice how Sanusi lists practices, then describes the resulting knowledge, then clarifies a misunderstanding.
The passage is dense but rewards careful reading.
2
Identify the means of increase
Find the list of practices that lead to increase in spiritual knowledges. What are they? What Quranic verse does he cite?
These are not mere rituals but states of the heart.
3
Understand the nature of this knowledge
Sanusi says these sciences are created by God directly, not through istidlāl or iʿtibār. What do those terms mean? How does he illustrate their incommunicability?
He uses the metaphor of a blind man and colors.
4
Grasp the defense against incarnation
Sanusi denies that this knowledge implies ḥulūl (incarnation). What does he mean by 'inner insight' and 'divine gift'? How does the Quranic verse about the Prophet's vision support his point?
The charge of incarnation is a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالصَّوْمِ وَدَوَامِ الذِّكْرِ عَلَى طَهَارَةِ الظَّاهِرِ وَالْبَاطِنِ، وَصِدْقِ الافْتِقَارِ إِلَى اللهِ تَعَالَى بِتَرْكِ الدَّعْوَى، وَالتَّبَرِّي مِنَ الْحَوْلِ وَالْقُوَّةِ ظَاهِراً وَبَاطِناً، سَبَبٌ بِمَشِيئَةِ اللهِ تَعَالَى لِلزِّيَادَةِ فِي الْمَعَارِفِ، كَمَا قَالَ تَعَالَى: ﴿وَالَّذِينَ جَاهَدُوا فِينَا لَنَهْدِيَنَّهُمْ سُبُلَنَا﴾ [العنكبوت: 69]. وَيُعَبِّرُونَ عَنِ الرُّوحِ وَالنُّورِ بِعَيْنِ السِّرِّ، وَهُوَ مِرْآةٌ لِتَجَلِّيَاتٍ وَكُشُوفِ الْأُمُورِ بِخَلْقِ عُلُومٍ لَا سَبِيلَ لِلِاطِّلَاعِ عَلَيْهَا بِالِاسْتِدْلَالِ وَلَا بِطُرُقِ الِاعْتِبَارِ، بَلْ بِمَحْضِ إِنْعَامٍ وَإِلْهَامٍ، بِخَلْقِ عُلُومٍ لَمْ تَجْرِ الْعَادَةُ بِخَلْقِهَا، وَلَا يَعْرِفُهَا إِلَّا أَهْلُهَا، وَلَا يَعْرِفُهَا غَيْرُهُمْ كَمَا لَا يَعْرِفُ الْأَكْمَهُ حَقَائِقَ الْأَلْوَانِ، وَلَا سَبِيلَ إِلَى تَعْرِيفِهَا بِالْقَوْلِ لِلْغَيْرِ، بَلْ بِإِشَارَةِ الْعَارِفِ لِلْعَارِفِ، كَمَا قِيلَ: [مِنَ الطَّوِيلِ]
تَشِيرُ فَأَدْرِي مَا تَقُولُ بِطَرْفِهَا ۝ وَأُطْرِقُ طَرْفِي عِنْدَ ذَاكَ فَتَفْهَمُ
وَيُقَالُ: لَنْ يَفْهَمَ عَنْكَ إِلَّا مَنْ أَشْرَقَ فِيهِ مَا أَشْرَقَ فِيكَ. وَلَا يَعْنُونَ بِذَلِكَ حُلُولاً كَمَا يَفْهَمُهُ بَعْضُ الْمُلَبِّسِينَ، بَلْ يُرِيدُونَ تِلْكَ الْبَصِيرَةَ الْبَاطِنَةَ وَالْمَوْهِبَةَ الرَّبَّانِيَّةَ الَّتِي لَا رَيْبَ فِيهَا وَلَا شَكَّ، كَمَا وَصَفَ بِذَلِكَ نَبِيَّهُ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ فَقَالَ: ﴿مَا زَاغَ الْبَصَرُ وَمَا طَغَى﴾ [النجم: 17]، فَأَنَّى لَهُ الْجَزْمُ بِنَفْيِ جَمِيعِ مَا يَدَّعُونَهُ؟!
And fasting, and continuous remembrance in purity of the outward and inward, and truth of neediness towards Allah the Exalted by abandoning pretension, and disavowing power and strength outwardly and inwardly, is a cause - by the will of Allah the Exalted - for increase in the [spiritual] knowledges (al-maʿārif), as He said the Exalted: 'And those who strive for Us, We will surely guide them to Our ways' [al-ʿAnkabūt: 69]. And they express by the spirit and the light with the essence of the secret, and it is a mirror of manifestations and unveilings of matters by the creation of sciences (ʿulūm) for which there is no way to become acquainted with them by inference (al-istidlāl) nor by the methods of reflection (iʿtibār), but solely by bestowal and inspiration; by the creation of sciences whose creation is not customary, and none know them except their people, and others do not know them just as the blind man does not know the realities of colors, and there is no way to define them by speech to others, but only by a sign (ishārah) of the knower to the knower, as was said [al-Ṭawīl meter]: 'She signals and I know what she says with her eye / and I lower my gaze at that, so you understand.' And it is said: 'Only the one in whom has shone what has shone in you will understand you.' And they do not mean by that incarnation (ḥulūl) as some of those who are confused understand it, but they mean that inner insight (al-baṣīrah al-bāṭinah) and the divine gift (al-mawhibah al-rabbāniyyah) in which there is no doubt or uncertainty, as he described thereby his Prophet, peace be upon him, saying: 'The sight did not swerve, nor did it transgress' [al-Najm: 17]. So how can he have certainty to deny all that they claim?!
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage describes the nature and means of spiritual knowledge (maʿārif) according to the Sufis, and defends its possibility against wholesale denial.

The author lists practices—fasting, constant remembrance, sincerity, reliance on God—as means for increase in maʿārif, citing Quran 29:69. He then explains that Sufis refer to this knowledge as 'spirit' and 'light' by which hidden realities are unveiled. These sciences (ʿulūm) are created directly by God, not through rational inference (istidlāl) or reflective methods (iʿtibār), but purely by divine favor and inspiration. They are not customary, and only their possessors know them. Others cannot know them, just as a blind man cannot know colors. They cannot be conveyed by speech, only by a sign (ishārah) from one knower to another, illustrated by a poem. Then he quotes a saying: 'Only the one in whom has shone what has shone in you will understand you.'

The author clarifies that this does not mean literal incarnation (ḥulūl), as some confused people think, but rather the inner insight and divine gift that are beyond doubt. He supports this by the Prophet's vision in Quran 53:17. He concludes by challenging the denier: 'How can he be certain to deny all that they claim?' This shows the author's defense of the possibility of such knowledge, while rejecting misinterpretations.

Misconception: The Sufi claim about spiritual knowledge implies that God literally dwells in the person (incarnation/ḥulūl).
Correction: Sanusi explicitly denies this, saying they only mean an inner insight and divine gift, not a literal indwelling.

Objection to Mu'tazila: Specific Does Not Cause General
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila claim that sharing in the specific is a cause for sharing in the general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi rejects the Mu'tazila claim that sharing in a specific attribute is a cause for sharing in a general attribute, and you will correctly distinguish between a cause ('illa) and a necessary condition (malzum).

Core Idea
Sharing in the specific entails the general but does not cause it.

The Mu'tazila argued that if two things share a specific attribute, that sharing causes them to share a more general attribute. On the surface, this seems plausible. But Sanusi demonstrates that this would lead to impossible conclusions. In this passage, he offers counterexamples and clarifies the correct logical relationship: the specific is a necessary condition, not a cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
Read the opening where Sanusi states what the Mu'tazila said: sharing in the specific necessitates sharing in the general, and they consider it a cause.
The Mu'tazila define similars as sharing in the specific.
2
Understand the objection
Sanusi argues that if the specific were a cause for the general, then the general cannot exist without the specific. But we observe cases where the general exists without the specific.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Examine the counterexamples
Horse and human share animality (general) but not rationality or neighing (specific). Similarly, whiteness and blackness share colorness (general) but not their specific colors.
These examples disprove the Mu'tazila claim of causation.
4
Learn the correct relationship
Sanusi explains that the specific is a necessary condition (malzum) for the general, not a cause. The existence of the specific entails the general, but the general does not entail the specific.
This preserves the logical dependency without making it a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الإيجابُ للأخص في بابِ التماثل ممتنع ؛ لوجودِ الاشتراك فى الأعم مع انتفائِهِ في الأخص. هذا اعتراض على المعتزلة في قولهم : إنَّ الاشتراك في الأخص يُوجِبُ الاشتراك في الأعم ؛ أي: هو عِلَّةٌ له ، ولهذا قالوا : حقيقة المثلين : هما المشتركان في الأخصّ ، واشتراكهما في الأخصّ عِلَّةٌ لاشتراكهما في الأعم. وتقرير الاعتراض عليهم : أنَّ الاشتراك في الأخصّ لو كانَ مُوجِباً للاشتراك في الأعم ؛ أي : عِلَّةً له كما زعموا . . لَمَا وُجِدَ الاشتراك في الأعم بدونِ الاشتراكِ في الأخصّ ؛ لاستحالة وجودِ المعلول بدونِ علتِهِ ، لكن التالي باطل ، فالمقدَّمُ مثلُهُ. ودليل بطلانه : أنَّ الفرس والإنسان مشتركان في الأعم الذاتي وهو الحيوانية ، وليسا مشتركين في الأخصّ ؛ كالناطقية أو الصاهليةِ ، وكذا البياضُ والسواد يشتركان في اللونية وهي ذاتي أعم ، ولا يشتركان في الأخص وهو السوادية أو البياضية. وإنَّما الواجبُ أَنْ يُقالَ : الاشتراك في الأخص الذاتي ملزوم للاشتراك في الأعم الذاتي ، فيلزمُ مِنْ وجودِ الاشتراك في الأخص وجود الاشتراك في الأعم ؛ لاستحالة وجودِ الملزوم بدون لازمه ؛ كالاشتراك في الناطقيَّةِ التي هي للإنسان أخص ؛ فإنَّهُ يلزم منه الاشتراك في الأعم الذي هو الحيوانية ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ وجودِ الاشتراك في الأعم الذاتي - كالحيوانية للإنسان مثلاً ـ وجود الاشتراك في الأخص كالناطقيَّةِ لهُ ؛ إذْ لا يلزمُ مِنْ وجودِ اللازم وجود الملزومِ. والحاصلُ : أنَّ الذي ننكرُهُ عليهم جعلهم الاشتراك في الأخص عِلَّةً للاشتراك في الأعم ، أما كونه ملزوماً لهُ فمما لا شك فيهِ.
Then, making the specific (al-akhass) necessary in the case of likeness (al-tamathul) is impossible, because the general (al-a'am) is shared while the specific is absent. This is an objection against the Mu'tazila in their statement: 'Sharing in the specific necessitates sharing in the general'; i.e., it is a cause ('illa) for it. For this reason they said: The reality of two similars is that they share in the specific, and their sharing in the specific is a cause for their sharing in the general. The formulation of the objection against them: If sharing in the specific were necessitating sharing in the general, i.e., a cause for it as they claim, then sharing in the general would not be found without sharing in the specific, because it is impossible for the effect to exist without its cause. But the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false. The proof of its falsehood: The horse and the human share in the general essential (al-a'am al-dhati), which is animality (al-hayawaniyyah), but they do not share in the specific, such as rationality (al-natiqiyyah) or neighing (al-sahiliyyah). Similarly, whiteness and blackness share in colorness (al-lawniyyah), which is a general essential (dhati a'am), but they do not share in the specific, which is blackness (al-sawadiyyah) or whiteness (al-bayadiyyah). Rather, what must be said is: Sharing in the specific essential (al-akhass al-dhati) is a necessary condition (malzum) for sharing in the general essential, so the existence of sharing in the specific entails the existence of sharing in the general, because it is impossible for the necessary condition to exist without its necessary concomitant (lazim). For example, sharing in rationality, which is specific to the human, entails sharing in the general, which is animality. But the existence of sharing in the general essential — such as animality for the human, for instance — does not entail the existence of sharing in the specific, such as rationality for it, because the existence of the necessary concomitant does not necessarily entail the existence of its necessary condition. The conclusion: What we deny them is their making sharing in the specific a cause for sharing in the general. As for it being a necessary condition (malzum) for it, there is no doubt about that.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding a logical principle: the relationship between sharing in a specific attribute (al-akhass) and sharing in a general attribute (al-a'am). The Mu'tazila had claimed that if two things share a specific attribute (like rationality in humans), that sharing is a cause ('illa) for their sharing in a more general attribute (like animality). Sanusi objects to this characterization. He first states the objection: if sharing in the specific were truly a cause for sharing in the general, then it would be impossible to have sharing in the general without sharing in the specific, because an effect cannot exist without its cause. But we find counterexamples: a horse and a human share in the general attribute of animality but do not share in the specific attribute of rationality (neighing for the horse). Similarly, whiteness and blackness share in colorness but do not share in the specific attributes of whiteness or blackness. Therefore, the Mu'tazila claim is false. Sanusi then presents the correct position: the relationship is not one of cause and effect but of necessary condition (malzum) and necessary concomitant (lazim). Sharing in the specific is a malzum for sharing in the general, meaning that if the specific exists, the general necessarily exists (like rationality entails animality). However, the converse does not hold: the general can exist without the specific (animality does not entail rationality). Thus, the Mu'tazila were wrong to call the specific a cause; it is only a necessary condition.

Key Terms
إيجاب
الأخص
الأعم
التماثل
عِلَّة
ملزوم
لازم
ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sharing in a specific attribute is a cause ('illa) for sharing in a general attribute.
Correction: It is only a necessary condition (malzum); the general can exist without the specific, so it is not a cause.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the correct relationship between sharing in the specific (al-akhass) and sharing in the general (al-a'am)?
A) Sharing in the specific is a cause ('illa) for sharing in the general.
B) Sharing in the specific is a necessary condition (malzum) that entails sharing in the general.
C) Sharing in the specific and sharing in the general are mutually independent.
D) Sharing in the general is a cause for sharing in the specific.

Key Takeaway
Sharing in a specific attribute is a necessary condition for sharing in a general attribute, not a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief without examining whether it was based on a false causal assumption? Today, you learned that a necessary condition is not the same as a cause. Where in your understanding of Allah's attributes might you be confusing the two? Will you take time to re-examine that assumption?
?
The Mu'tazila made a seemingly subtle mistake, but it had major implications for their theology. Are you willing to invest the effort to think precisely about obscure logical matters in aqeedah, even when it feels tedious? What is at stake if you don't?
?
You have seen that sharing in the specific does not cause sharing in the general, but merely necessitates it. Think of a relationship in your own life (e.g., between your actions and their consequences) where you might be mistakenly treating a necessary condition as a cause. How can you correct that?

The Principle of Verbal Expressions Following Transmission, Meanings Following Reason
Sanusi states the proper relationship between verbal expressions and rational meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's methodological principle that the application of words is governed by transmitted usage while their interpretation is governed by rational certainty.

Core Idea
Words follow tradition; meanings follow reason.

Sanusi lays down a foundational rule for kalam methodology: how we use theological language is determined by revelation and tradition, but how we understand that language is determined by rational principles. This prevents both literalist errors and unchecked rational innovation. The passage establishes a balanced approach that respects both transmitted authority and intellectual rigor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once to see its structure. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning. Notice how Sanusi uses a concise, summary style.
The passage begins with 'وبالجملة' which signals a summary conclusion.
2
Identify the two key relationships
The passage presents two parallel relationships: (1) the application of verbal expressions follows transmitted usage (النقل), and (2) their meanings follow reason (العقل). Mark these contrasts in the Arabic text.
Sanusi uses the phrase 'مِنْ حيث' to indicate the aspect under which each follows its source.
3
Understand the consequence of ignoring reason
Sanusi warns that if we follow the literal meaning absolutely, rejecting rational certainties, we would fall into every error and disbelief. This shows the high stakes of this principle.
The phrase 'قواطع العقل' refers to rational proofs that yield certainty.
4
Reflect on how words have multiple aspects of indication
Sanusi notes that words have multiple possible meanings (وجوه دلالتها متكثرة). Mastery requires both lengthy practice with language and solid rational principles.
This explains why a purely literalist approach is insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة : فالإطلاقات اللفظية تابعة للنقل مِنْ حيث إطلاقها ، ومعانيها تابعة للعقلِ مِنْ حيثُ الحمل عليها ، فلا بدَّ مِنْ فهمها على ما يصح ، لا أنَّ الألفاظ متبوعة مطلقاً ، يُرفَضُ لظاهرها قواطع العقل ، وإلا لزم كلُّ ضلالٍ وكفر ، والألفاظ وجوه دلالتها متكثرة ، وإنَّما تنضبط بطولِ ممارستها مع إتقان القوانين العقليَّةِ
In sum: verbal usages follow transmitted usage with respect to their application, while their meanings follow reason with respect to their interpretation. So one must understand them in a sound manner, not that the words are followed absolutely, rejecting rational certainties for their apparent meaning; otherwise every error and disbelief would be necessary. And words have multiple aspects of indication, and they are only mastered through lengthy practice along with mastery of rational principles.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is a methodological principle. Sanusi establishes a twofold rule: the application of terms (i.e., which words to use) is based on transmitted usage (naql), but the interpretation of their meanings (ma'ani) is governed by reason (aql). One cannot simply take the literal meaning of words without considering rational certainties (qawati' al-aql). Doing so would lead to error and disbelief. Since words can have multiple possible meanings, their correct understanding requires both extensive practice in the language and solid rational principles. This principle is crucial in kalam to avoid anthropomorphic or literalist errors.

Key Terms
الإطلاقات اللفظية
النقل
العقل
قواطع العقل
ضلال
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The literal meaning of religious texts must be taken without any rational interpretation.
Correction: Sanusi teaches that while the application of words follows tradition, their meaning must be interpreted in light of rational certainties, because rejecting reason leads to error and disbelief.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what happens if one rejects rational certainties due to the apparent meaning of words?
A) It leads to correct understanding.
B) It leads to every error and disbelief.
C) It is acceptable if the words are from Quran.
D) It only leads to minor mistakes.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi establishes that the application of words is tied to revelation, but their interpretation is guided by reason, preventing both blind literalism and rational excess.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there any Islamic beliefs you hold for which you have never carefully examined the rational grounds, trusting only the literal wording of a text? How would Sanusi's principle challenge that comfort?
?
When you encounter a verse or hadith that seems to contradict clear logical or scientific truths, does your first instinct lean toward rejecting reason or finding a sound interpretation? What does this passage demand you change in your approach?
?
How much of your study time is devoted to mastering rational principles (logic, argumentation) compared to memorizing texts? If there is a large imbalance, what does Sanusi's call for 'lengthy practice along with mastery of rational principles' require you to do starting this week?

The Limits of Lengthy Discussions on Divine Attributes
Sanusi advises against excessive length in discussing God's speech and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi advises against prolonging debates on divine attributes after the truth is established, and the theological principle that the essence (kunh) of God's attributes is veiled from reason.

Core Idea
After truth is clear, lengthy debate on divine attributes yields little benefit — the essence is veiled.

Sanusi has just concluded his discussion on God's speech. Here he reflects on the nature of theological inquiry itself. He acknowledges the vastness of the topic but cautions against endless debate. The reason is profound: the true essence of God's attributes is beyond rational grasp. Any genuine knowledge would be intuitive (dhawqi) and inexpressible. This passage reminds us of the limits of reason and the importance of accepting revealed truths with humility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read Sanusi's concluding remark. Notice his tone — he is not dismissing theological inquiry but setting its proper limits.
Observe that he acknowledges the topic's importance but chooses brevity.
2
Identify the two key reasons
First, the truth has become clear from his previous discussion. Second, the essence of God's attributes is veiled from reason, making further benefit small.
The second reason is the deeper theological point.
3
Reflect on 'kunh' (كنه)
Consider the concept of 'kunh' — the true nature or essence. Why does this matter for kalam? It sets a boundary for rational inquiry.
4
Examine the investigator's statement
The quoted investigator says any knowledge of the essence would be intuitive (dhawqi) and inexpressible. What does this imply about the role of reason and language?
5
Connect to broader methodology
This passage shows the Ash'ari principle of stopping rational inquiry once the truth is clear, acknowledging the limits of the intellect.
It is a lesson in intellectual humility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أنَّ مسألةَ الكلام ذاتُ تشعَّب كثير وبحث مع المبتدعة منتشر شهيرٍ ، حتى قيلَ : إِنَّما سُمِّيَ فن أصول الدين بعلم الكلام لأجله ، وقد استبانَ الحقِّ بما ذكرناه في المسألة ، فرأينا الإعراض عن كثير مِنَ المباحثِ المذكورة فيها ؛ للمحافظةِ مِنَ التطويلِ ، بل لا كبير جدوى له ، ولهذا قال بعضُ المُحققينَ : الحقُّ أَنَّ التطويل في مسألة الكلام - بل وفي جميع صفاتِهِ تعالى - بعد ما يستبينُ الحقُّ في ذلكَ . قليلُ الجدوى ؛ لأنَّ كُنْهَ ذاتِهِ تعالى وكُنْه صفاتِهِ محجوبٌ عنِ العقل ، وعلى تقديرِ التوصل إلى شيءٍ مِنْ معرفة الذاتِ.. فهو ذوقي ، لا يمكن التعبير عنه ، والله سبحانَهُ أعلم
And know that the issue of speech is greatly ramified and the discussion with the innovators is widespread and well-known, such that it is said: 'The art of the principles of religion was named 'ilm al-kalam because of it.' And the truth has become clear by what we have mentioned regarding the issue, so we have chosen to turn away from many of the discussions mentioned therein, to avoid prolongation, indeed there is no great benefit in it. Hence some investigators said: 'The truth is that prolongation regarding the issue of speech—and indeed regarding all of His attributes, exalted is He—after the truth about that has become clear, is of little benefit, because the essence of His essence, exalted is He, and the essence of His attributes are veiled from reason, and assuming arriving at some knowledge of the essence, it is intuitive (dhawqi), not capable of expression.' And God, glorified and exalted, knows best.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is a concluding remark. Sanusi notes the vastness of the topic of God's speech and its association with the name 'ilm al-kalam. However, since the truth has been made clear in his discussion, he chooses to avoid prolongation, as he sees little benefit. He quotes an investigator who states that after the truth is established, lengthy debate on divine attributes is of little benefit because the true nature (kunh) of God's essence and attributes is veiled from reason. Any knowledge attained would be intuitive (dhawqi) and inexpressible. This underscores the limits of rational inquiry and the importance of accepting revealed truths with humility.

Key Terms
علم الكلام
مبتدعة
تطويل
جدوى
كنه
ذوقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that endless debate on divine attributes is always beneficial.
Correction: Sanusi argues it is not after the truth is clear, because the essence of God's attributes is veiled from reason.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi say prolonging discussion on God's speech is of little benefit?
A) Because the topic is too simple.
B) Because the essence of God and His attributes are veiled from reason.
C) Because the innovators have been refuted already.
D) Because it is forbidden to speak about God.

Key Takeaway
After the truth of divine attributes is established, further prolongation is of little benefit because the true essence of God and His attributes lies beyond rational grasp.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi says that after truth is clear, prolonging debate on divine attributes is of little benefit. Is there a theological question you have been wrestling with long after the answer was evident? What would it mean for you to accept that truth and move on?
?
The essence of God is veiled from reason, and any knowledge of it is intuitive and inexpressible. Have you ever felt a certainty in your heart about God that words could not capture? How does that experience challenge your reliance on rational arguments alone?
?
This passage implies that endless discussion can become a distraction from the real goal of faith. Is there an area of your life where you use intellectual debate to avoid personal surrender? What would it look like to turn away from that and let the truth settle in your heart?

The Priority of Reason Over Literal Words in Theology
Sanusi asserts that meanings are subordinate to reason, not words, and explains limited benefit of excessive elaboration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, in Islamic theology, rational principles take precedence over literal interpretations of words, and why extensive debate on divine attributes after establishing the truth is considered of limited benefit.

Core Idea
Reason, not literal words, is the final arbiter in theology.

In this passage, Sanusi lays down a foundational methodological principle: theological meanings must be interpreted in light of reason, not taken literally. He then explains that once the truth is clear, further lengthy debates on divine attributes are futile because the essence of God is beyond full rational grasp. This lesson explores that principle and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of Sanusi's argument. Notice his emphasis on rationality from the outset.
The author compresses a lot of meaning into a few lines.
2
Understand the subordination of meanings to reason
Sanusi argues that meanings (ma'ani) are subordinate to reason (al-'aql) in terms of predication. This means if a literal reading contradicts decisive rational proof, the literal meaning must be rejected—otherwise misguidance and disbelief follow.
This is the key methodological point of the passage.
3
Explore the multiplicity of word significations
Words have multiple aspects of signification, and only through long practice with mastery of rational laws can one correctly determine the intended meaning. This refutes any simplistic literalism.
Note the phrase 'القوانين العقلية' as essential tools.
4
Reflect on the limited benefit of lengthy debate
After the truth is clear, further prolongation (tatwil) is of little benefit because the inmost reality (kunh) of God's essence and attributes is veiled from reason; any deeper knowledge is by taste (dhawq) and inexpressible.
This pragmatic point counsels against unproductive theological speculation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومعانيها تابعة للعقلِ مِنْ حيثُ الحمل عليها ، فلا بدَّ مِنْ فهمها على ما يصح ، لا أنَّ الألفاظ متبوعة مطلقاً ، يُرفَضُ لظاهرها قواطع العقل ، وإلا لزم كلُّ ضلالٍ وكفر ، والألفاظ وجوه دلالتها متكثرة ، وإنَّما تنضبط بطولِ ممارستها مع إتقان القوانين العقليَّةِ واعلم : أنَّ مسألةَ الكلام ذاتُ تشعَّب كثير وبحث مع المبتدعة منتشر شهيرٍ ، حتى قيلَ : إِنَّما سُمِّيَ فن أصول الدين بعلم الكلام لأجله ، وقد استبانَ الحقِّ بما ذكرناه في المسألة ، فرأينا الإعراض عن كثير مِنَ المباحثِ المذكورة فيها ؛ للمحافظةِ مِنَ التطويلِ ، بل لا كبير جدوى له ، ولهذا قال بعضُ المُحققينَ : الحقُّ أَنَّ التطويل في مسألة الكلام - بل وفي جميع صفاتِهِ تعالى - بعد ما يستبينُ الحقُّ في ذلكَ . قليلُ الجدوى ؛ لأنَّ كُنْهَ ذاتِهِ تعالى وكُنْه صفاتِهِ محجوبٌ عنِ العقل ، وعلى تقديرِ التوصل إلى شيءٍ مِنْ معرفة الذاتِ.. فهو ذوقي ، لا يمكن التعبير عنه ، والله سبحانَهُ أعلم .
And their meanings are subordinate to reason in terms of predication, so they must be understood correctly, not that words are followed absolutely; the decisive proofs of reason reject their literal meaning, otherwise all misguidance and disbelief would follow. Words have multiple aspects of signification, and they are only controlled by long practice with mastery of rational laws. And know: that the issue of speech (kalam) is highly ramified, and debate with the innovators is widespread and famous, until it was said: 'Indeed, the art of usul al-din was named the science of kalam because of it.' And the truth has become clear with what we have mentioned on the issue, so we have chosen to turn away from many of the discussions mentioned in it, to avoid prolongation — indeed, there is no great benefit in it. For this reason, some investigators said: 'The truth is that lengthening discussion on the issue of speech — indeed on all of His attributes, may He be exalted — after the truth has become clear on that, is of little benefit; because the inmost reality of His essence and the inmost reality of His attributes is veiled from reason, and if one arrives at something of the knowledge of the essence, it is by taste (dhawq) and cannot be expressed.' And Allah, glorified is He, knows best.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is a methodological statement about the role of reason in understanding theological language. The author argues that meanings (ma'ani) are subordinate to reason (al-'aql) in terms of predication (haml), meaning that words must be interpreted in a way that is rationally sound, not taken literally if reason rejects them. Otherwise, blind literal adherence would lead to error and disbelief. Words have multiple possible meanings, and only through sustained study and mastery of rational principles can one correctly determine the intended meaning. The author then remarks that the topic of divine speech (kalam) is so central that the entire field of usul al-din is named after it. However, after establishing the correct position, he chooses to avoid lengthy debates because they are of little benefit. He cites a muhaqqiq (investigator) that once the truth is clear, further elaboration is futile because the essence and attributes of God are beyond full rational comprehension; any deeper knowledge is of the nature of spiritual taste (dhawq) and inexpressible. The author concludes by deferring to Allah's greater knowledge.

Key Terms
الكلام (al-kalam)
الألفاظ (al-alfaz)
القوانين العقلية (al-qawanin al-'aqliyya)
التطويل (al-tatwil)
كنه الذات (kunn al-dhat)
ذوقي (dhawqi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Theological terms must always be taken literally, and any rational objection is invalid.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that meanings are subordinate to reason; if a literal reading contradicts decisive rational proofs, the literal meaning must be rejected. Literalism without reason leads to error.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author gives for not engaging in lengthy discussion on divine attributes after the truth is clear?
A) Because it leads to disbelief
B) Because the essence and attributes are veiled from reason and deeper knowledge is inexpressible
C) Because the opponent is stubborn
D) Because it is forbidden

Key Takeaway
Meanings are subordinate to reason, and once the truth is clear, further debate on divine attributes is of little benefit because God's essence is veiled from reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a Quranic verse that seems to contradict your reason, do you force your reason to submit to a literal reading, or do you trust the method of scholars who interpret it rationally? What is at stake if you choose the wrong approach?
?
How much time have you spent arguing about theological minutiae that have already been settled by clear truth? Is your motive sincere devotion or intellectual pride? Would Sanusi say your efforts are of little benefit?
?
Have you ever tasted a spiritual reality that words cannot express? If not, are you content with mere debate, or are you seeking that deeper, dhawqi knowledge of Allah? What concrete step can you take today to pursue it?

Conclusion: Purpose of the Verse and the Doctrine of Kalām Nafsī
Sanusi concludes that the verse strengthens the proof of internal speech and that denying it is clearly false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of scriptural evidence in supporting the rational proof for internal speech (al-kalām al-nafsī) and the obligation to interpret divine speech literally.

Core Idea
The verse strengthens, but reason proves; denying internal speech is clearly false.

In this concluding passage, Sanusi clarifies why he cited the verse 'And Allah spoke to Musa' — not as the primary proof, but as a reinforcement. He emphasizes that the denial of internal speech is clearly false by both reason and revelation, and that once internal speech is established, all scriptural attributions of speech to Allah must be taken in their apparent sense. He warns against reinterpreting without valid cause, calling it innovation and violation of consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of citing the verse
Read the opening sentence: Sanusi states the verse is only for strengthening the proof of internal speech, not as the primary proof.
The primary proof is rational.
2
Understand the falsity of denying internal speech
Note that denying internal speech and restricting speech to letters and sounds is declared 'clearly false' by both reason and revelation.
This is a strong assertion.
3
Grasp the obligation to take apparent meaning
Once internal speech is established, any scriptural attribution of speech to Allah must be taken literally as referring to His eternal attribute.
4
Recognize the warning against innovation
Reinterpreting without cause is labeled 'innovation' (bidʿa) and a violation of the consensus of the Companions.
5
Reflect on the natural understanding of the verse
Sanusi notes that the immediate, natural understanding of 'And Allah spoke to Musa' is that He spoke to him directly, without metaphor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة : فنحن لم نذكر هذه الآية إلا على سبيل التقوية لإثباتِ الكلامِ النفسي القديمِ بسماع موسى عليه السلام له ، وإلا فإنكار الكلام النفسي وحصرُهُ في الحروفِ والأصوات . . واضحُ البطلان عقلاً ونقلاً ، وإذا ثبت الكلامُ النفسي ، ووُجِدَ في الكتاب أو السنَّةِ إِسنادُ الكلام إليه تعالى .. وجبَ اعتقاد ظاهرِهِ ، وأنَّ المراد كلامه القديم القائم بذاتِهِ، والتعرُّضُ لإخراج اللفظ عن ظاهره الصحيحِ مِنْ غيرِ مُوحِب .. بدعةٌ ومخالفة لإجماع الصحابة وتابعيهم بإحسان . ولا شك أنَّ المتبادر إلى الذهن لغةً وعرفاً مِنْ قولِهِ تعالى : ( وَكَلَّمَ الله موسى ﴾ [النساء : ١٦٤] مِنْ غيرِ نظر إلى التوكيد.. أَنَّهُ كَلَّمَهُ بغيرِ
In summary: We have only mentioned this verse by way of strengthening the proof of the eternal internal speech (al-kalām al-nafsī al-qadīm) by Musa's hearing it, peace be upon him. Otherwise, the denial of internal speech and its restriction to letters and sounds is clearly false by reason and revelation. And when internal speech is established, and the attribution of speech to Him Most High is found in the Book or the Sunnah, it becomes obligatory to believe in its apparent meaning, and that what is meant is His eternal speech subsisting in His essence. Attempting to remove the wording from its correct apparent meaning without a cause is an innovation and a violation of the consensus of the Companions and their followers in goodness. And there is no doubt that the immediate understanding of the mind, linguistically and conventionally, from His saying 'And Allah spoke to Musa' [4:164] without consideration of the emphasis, is that He spoke to him without...
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a concluding summary. The author clarifies the purpose of using the verse: it is not the primary proof for internal speech (al-kalām al-nafsī), but rather a supportive reinforcement. The primary proof is rational, establishing that speech cannot be confined to letters and sounds. Denial of internal speech is clearly false both rationally (ʿaqlan) and by revelation (naqlan). Once internal speech is proven, any scriptural attribution of speech to God must be taken in its apparent meaning, referring to His eternal attribute of speech subsisting in His essence. To reinterpret such verses without a valid reason is an innovation (bidʿa) and violates the consensus of the Companions and their righteous followers. Finally, the author notes that even without the emphatic verbal noun, the natural understanding of the verse 'And Allah spoke to Musa' is that He spoke to him in a real, non-metaphorical manner.

القديم
البدعة
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the verse 'And Allah spoke to Musa' is the primary proof for internal speech.
Correction: The author explicitly states the verse is only supportive; the primary proof is rational.

Checkpoint

A) The verse 'And Allah spoke to Musa'.
B) Rational proof (al-burhān al-qaṭʿī).
C) Consensus of the Companions.
D) The emphatic verbal noun.

Key Takeaway
Denying internal speech (kalām nafsī) is false by reason and revelation; scriptural speech must be taken literally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author says denying internal speech is 'clearly false by reason and revelation.' Have you ever doubted an attribute of God because you could not conceive of it? If that doubt arises again, will you submit to what reason and revelation affirm, or will you reshape God to fit your mental limits?
?
The passage warns against removing words from their apparent meaning without a cause. Are there any verses about Allah's attributes that you have subtly reinterpreted to feel more comfortable? Will you commit to accepting the apparent meaning as taught by the Salaf, even when it challenges your assumptions?

Warning on Studying Divine Attributes
Sanusi cautions that the issue of unity of attributes raises strong investigations and difficult problems.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the study of divine attributes is considered a 'slippery place' in kalam and requires divine guidance.

Core Idea
The study of divine attributes is a spiritual and intellectual minefield that only divine guidance navigates safely.

Sanusi, after discussing the attributes, pauses to give a stark warning. He acknowledges that the topic of unity in attributes is fraught with strong investigations and difficult problems. He admits he has omitted many discussions to avoid tedium, but warns that the subject is vast and perilous. This lesson examines that warning and its implications for the student.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the tone and the words used: 'strong investigations', 'difficult problems', 'slippery places of the feet'.
Sanusi uses vivid imagery to convey the danger.
2
Understand the warning
Identify what Sanusi says about the nature of these discussions. He says they are extensive and can cause one to slip unless God establishes them.
The 'slippery places' metaphor indicates the risk of falling into error.
3
Reflect on the need for divine guidance
Note how Sanusi emphasizes reliance on God. He says the scope is too narrow without God's facilitation and asks God to make us know Him and not to try us.
This shows that kalam study is not purely intellectual but also spiritual.

واعلم : أنَّ مسألةَ الوَحدةِ في الصفاتِ تتعلَّقُ بها أبحاث قويَّةٌ وإشكالات صعبةٌ يضيقُ مجال النظر فيها إلا أَنْ يُوفِّقَ اللهُ تعالى ، وقد تركنا التعرُّضَ لكثير منها خشية السآمةِ ، وفيما ذكرناهُ مِنْ ذلك كفاية وبالجملة : فمباحثُ الصفاتِ المعنوية والمعاني. متسعة جداً ، وهي مِنْ مزال الأقدام ، إلا أن يثبت الله سبحانَهُ ، نسألُهُ جَلَّ وعلا أَنْ يُعرفنا به ، ولا يَفتَنَّا في ديننا بفضلِهِ
And know: the issue of unity (al-wahda) in the attributes involves strong investigations and difficult problems. The scope of examination is too narrow for them unless God facilitates. We have left out discussing many of them for fear of tedium. What we have mentioned of that is sufficient. In summary, the investigations of the attributes (al-sifat al-ma'nawiyya) and the meanings (al-ma'ani) are very extensive, and they are among the slippery places of the feet, unless God establishes one. We ask Him, may He be glorified and exalted, to make us know Him and not to try us in our religion by His grace.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is a cautionary remark. The author notes that the topic of unity (wahda) in the attributes is very complex. He has omitted many discussions to avoid boring the reader. He states that the discussions of the attributes (al-sifat al-ma'nawiyya) and the meanings (al-ma'ani) are vast and can lead one astray. Only God's guidance keeps one firm. This shows the author's humility and the difficulty of this theological domain.

Correction: Sanusi warns that the topic is a 'slippery place' (mazall al-aqdam) and that unless God establishes one, one can slip into error. He also prays for divine guidance, showing that this study requires both intellectual effort and spiritual reliance.
