Proofs Requiring Intellectual Evidence
Sanusi discusses disagreement on scriptural proof for unity and states the first category requiring intellectual evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that certain foundational beliefs about Allah, such as His existence and attributes, can only be proven by intellectual evidence, not by scripture alone, and why scriptural evidence for them would involve circular reasoning.

Core Idea
Some truths require reason; using scripture alone would be circular.

Sanusi begins his classification by addressing a key methodological question: can divine unity be proven by scripture alone? He notes a disagreement, then establishes the first category of creedal matters that must be proven by reason. This lesson focuses on that category and the logical argument against using scripture for foundational beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the disagreement
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Sanusi presents a disagreement before the classification.
The disagreement sets up the need for classification.
2
Understand the circularity argument
Focus on the statement that using scripture for these matters leads to circularity (dawr). Ask why that is.
The circularity arises because scripture depends on the Creator's existence, which is what you are trying to prove.
3
Identify the first category and its contents
List the attributes mentioned: existence, eternity, knowledge, power, will, life. Understand why these are prerequisites for miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخلاف في صحة الاستدلال على ثبوتِ الوحدانية بالدليل السمعي وحده. ويصح إثباتُ هذا العَقْدِ ـ وهو الوحدانية - بالدليل السمعي، ومنعَهُ بعضُ المحققين، وهو رأي؛ لأنَّ ثبوت الصانع لا يتحقق بدونِها، ولا أثر للدليل السمعي في ثبوت الصانع، فكذا ما يتوقَّفُ عليه، والله أعلم. اعلم: أن عقود التوحيد على ثلاثة أقسام: الأول: ما لا يصح الاستدلال عليه إلا بالدليل العقلي: وهو كلُّ ما يتوقَّفُ ثبوتُ المعجزة عليه؛ وذلكَ كوجودِهِ تعالى، وقدمِهِ وبقائه، وعلمه وقدرته وإرادته وحياتِهِ؛ إذْ لَوِ اسْتُدِلَّ بالسمع على هذه الأمور للزم الدور. الثاني: ما لا يصح الاستدلال عليه إلا بالسمع: وهو كل ما يرجعُ إلى وقوع جائز؛ كالبعثِ، وسؤالِ الملكين في القبرِ، والصراط والميزانِ، والثواب والعقابِ، والجنة والنارِ، ورؤيته تعالى، وغيرِ ذلكَ ممَّا لا يُحصى كثرةً؛ لأنَّ غاية ما يدرك العقل وحدَهُ مِنْ هذهِ الأمور جوازها، أما وقوعها فلا طريق له إلا السمع. الثالثُ: ما يصح الاستدلال عليه بالأمرين: أعني: السمع والعقل؛ بحيثُ يستقلُّ كلُّ واحدٍ منهما بالدلالة عليه؛ وهو ما ليس كذلك.
The disagreement concerning the validity of inferring the affirmation of unity (al-waḥdāniyya) by scriptural evidence (samʿī) alone. The affirmation of this creed—that is, unity—is valid by scriptural evidence, but some verifiers prevented it, and it is an opinion; because the existence of the Maker is not realized without it, and scriptural evidence has no effect in establishing the Maker, so too what depends on it. And Allah knows best. Know that the creeds of tawḥīd are of three divisions: First: that which it is not valid to infer except by intellectual evidence (ʿaqlī): that is everything on which the proof of miracles depends; such as His existence, His beginninglessness and His everlastingness, His knowledge, His power, His will, and His life; because if one were to infer by scripture (samʿ) for these matters, there would be circularity. Second: that which it is not valid to infer except by scripture (samʿ): that is everything that pertains to the occurrence of a contingent event; such as the Resurrection, the questioning of the two angels in the grave, the Ṣirāṭ and the Balance, reward and punishment, Paradise and Hell, the vision of Him Most High, and other things too numerous to count; because the utmost that the intellect alone perceives of these matters is their possibility, but their occurrence has no path except scripture. Third: that which it is valid to infer by both means: I mean: scripture and intellect; so that each one of them independently indicates it; and it is that which is not such.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage first discusses a disagreement: can divine unity (waḥdāniyya) be proven by scriptural evidence alone? Some scholars say no, because the existence of the Creator itself cannot be proven by scripture (since scripture presupposes the Creator), so what depends on that (i.e., unity) also cannot. Then the author provides a classification of the 'uqūd al-tawḥīd (creeds of tawḥīd) into three categories based on the type of evidence required. The first category includes matters that can only be proven by intellectual evidence (ʿaqlī), such as the existence of Allah, His attributes of eternity, knowledge, power, will, life, etc. These are required for the validity of miracles, so if one tried to prove them by scripture, it would result in circular reasoning (dawr). The second category includes matters that can only be proven by scripture (samʿī), such as details of the afterlife (resurrection, grave questioning, etc.) and the vision of Allah. Reason can only establish that these are possible, not that they actually occur. The third category includes matters that can be proven by either reason or scripture independently, without circularity. This classification shows the proper methodology for tawḥīd proofs.

Key Terms
الوحدانية
الدليل السمعي
الدليل العقلي
عقود التوحيد
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Scripture alone can prove all Islamic beliefs, including God's existence.
Correction: Some foundational beliefs, like God's existence and essential attributes, cannot be proven by scripture without circularity; they require intellectual evidence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can't Allah's existence be proven by scriptural evidence alone?
A) Because scripture is unreliable.
B) Because it would lead to circular reasoning.
C) Because reason is superior to scripture.
D) Because existence is self-evident.

Key Takeaway
Foundational divine attributes require intellectual proof; scriptural proof would be circular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran or hadith, do you ever assume that their authority is self-evident without reflecting on the rational basis for trusting revelation? How might this lesson challenge you to examine the foundations of your own faith more deeply?
?
The source warns against circular reasoning in proving God's existence. Have you ever defended your belief in God by simply quoting scripture without considering the need for intellectual evidence? What would it mean for you to intentionally integrate both reason and revelation in your understanding of tawḥīd?
?
If someone asked you why you believe in God's existence, would you be able to provide a rational argument independent of scripture? This lesson demands that you be ready to answer with intellectual evidence. Do you have such reasons at hand? Commit today to learning one rational proof for God's existence.

Proofs Requiring Scriptural or Combined Evidence
Sanusi defines the second and third categories of tawhid proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second and third categories of tawhid proofs: matters that require scriptural evidence (like afterlife details) and matters that can be proven by either reason or scripture.

Core Idea
Afterlife details require scripture; some matters can be proven by both.

Having established the first category requiring intellectual evidence, Sanusi now presents the second category: matters that can only be proven by scripture. He then briefly mentions a third category that can be proven by either. This lesson covers the scope of scriptural evidence and where reason alone is insufficient.

Guided Path
1
Read the second category definition
Read the Arabic and translation for the second category. Notice the examples given.
2
Understand why only scripture can prove contingent events
The passage states that reason can only perceive the possibility of these events, not their actual occurrence. Reflect on this limitation.
This is a key principle: reason deals with necessity and possibility, not actuality without revelation.
3
Examine the third category and its condition
The third category includes matters that can be proven by both reason and scripture independently. Note that it is a residual category.
The text says 'that which is not such' – meaning matters not falling into the first two categories.

Exonerating the Imams: Context Matters in Kalam Debates
Sanusi exonerates the imams from weak attributed sayings using al-Sharif's quote.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that statements made by early scholars in dialectical debates should not be taken as their actual doctrinal positions, as illustrated by Sanusi's exoneration of the imams.

Core Idea
Debate tactics are not doctrine.

When studying early kalam, you may encounter controversial statements attributed to great imams. How should we understand these? Sanusi provides a key principle: such statements often occurred in dialectical debates and do not reflect the scholars' true beliefs. This lesson explores that principle through Sanusi's explanation and a supporting quote from al-Sharif al-Jurjani.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the tone of certainty in Sanusi's opening statement.
Sanusi uses the strong phrase 'أقطعُ بِهِ' meaning 'I am certain'.
2
Identify the core claim and its basis
Sanusi claims the imams are exonerated from the weak sayings. He suggests these sayings originated in dialectical debates (مناظرة جدلية). Understand why he says this.
The phrase 'لإفحام خصم' indicates the goal: to silence a stubborn opponent.
3
Analyze al-Sharif's supporting quote
Al-Sharif provides a specific example about al-Qadi and al-Ustadh regarding created power. He argues it was a debating tactic, and cites consensus (إجماع) to show their true belief.
Notice the rhetorical question 'كيف وقد نقل الإجماع...' which strengthens the exoneration.
4
Reflect on the methodological lesson
Consider how this principle applies to other statements attributed to scholars. Always distinguish between dialectical statements and settled doctrine.
This is a key principle in the study of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة : فالذي أقطعُ بِهِ مِنْ غيرِ تردُّدٍ : تنزه هؤلاء الأئمة عمَّا نُقِلَ عنهم ، ولعلَّ ذلكَ إنَّما صدر عنهم في مناظرة جدلية لإفحام خصمِ قويَتْ منافرته للحقِّ ، فاحتالوا لسوقه إلى الحقِّ بتدريج ، ولهذا قال المشايخ : لا يُنقَلُ عنِ العالم ويُجعَلَ مذهباً له ما يصدر عنه على سبيل البحث
وقد قالَ الشريف في شرح الأسرار العقلية نحو هذا ؛ قال : ( ما يُنسَبُ للقاضي والأستاذ - يعني : مِنْ كون القدرة الحادثة تُؤثر في الحالِ - إِنَّما صدرَ منهما ذلك على وجه المناظرة للخصوم ، وإلا فحاشى القاضي والأستاذ أنْ يعتقدا أثراً لغير القدرة القديمة ، كيف وقد نقل الإجماع في مواضعَ مِنْ كُتُبِهِ على كفْرِ مَنْ نسبَ الاختراعَ لغير الله تعالى ، ونقل أيضاً إجماع الأمَّةِ على كفرِ مَنْ لم يقل بعموم صفات الباري تعالى ؟! )
In summary, what I am certain of without hesitation: the exoneration of these imams from what has been narrated about them, and perhaps that only occurred from them in a dialectical debate to silence a strong opponent whose aversion to truth was strong, so they resorted to leading him to the truth gradually. For this reason, the teachers said: What proceeds from a scholar in the course of investigation should not be transmitted and made into his doctrine. And al-Sharif indeed said in (Sharh al-Asrar al-'Aqliyya) something similar; he said: 'What is attributed to al-Qadi and al-Ustadh—meaning that the created power influences the state—only occurred from them in the manner of debating opponents. Otherwise, far be it from al-Qadi and al-Ustadh to believe in an effect for other than the eternal power. How can they, when he transmitted consensus in places of his books on the disbelief of those who attribute invention to other than God, and also transmitted the consensus of the community on the disbelief of those who do not affirm the generality of the attributes of the Exalted God?!'
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is an exoneration of the imams (Imam al-Haramayn, al-Qadi, al-Ustadh) from the weak sayings attributed to them regarding the effect of created power. The author states his certainty that these imams are innocent of such views. He suggests that such statements may have been made in dialectical debates (munāẓara jadaliyya) to gradually lead a stubborn opponent to the truth, and therefore should not be taken as their actual doctrine. He quotes al-Sharif al-Jurjani from his Sharh al-Asrar al-'Aqliyya, who similarly argues that what was attributed to al-Qadi and al-Ustadh—that the created power influences the state (al-hāl, meaning the accident)—was only said in the context of debating opponents. Al-Sharif asserts that these imams actually believed that no effect exists for other than the eternal power, citing that they transmitted consensus (ijmāʿ) on the disbelief of those who attribute invention (ikhtirāʿ) to other than God, and on the disbelief of those who do not affirm the generality of God's attributes.

Key Terms
تنزيه (tanzīh)
مناظرة جدلية (munāẓara jadaliyya)
إجماع (ijmāʿ)
اختراع (ikhtirāʿ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Whatever a scholar says in a debate is automatically his doctrine.
Correction: The passage clarifies that debate statements may be tactical and not represent actual belief; they should not be taken as the scholar's permanent position.

Checkpoint
According to al-Sharif's quote, why did al-Qadi and al-Ustadh say that the created power influences the state?
A) Because they actually believed it
B) As a concession in a debate to lead opponents gradually
C) Because they changed their minds
D) Because of ignorance

Key Takeaway
Statements made in dialectical debates should not be attributed as the permanent doctrine of a scholar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend a truth, do you ever use arguments that you yourself do not fully embrace? How does this lesson challenge you to examine the integrity of your own reasoning in debates?
?
Think of someone you have dismissed because of something they said in a heated argument. Could you have been unjust? What would it look like to apply Sanusi's principle—separating debate statements from settled belief—to that person today?
?
This lesson reveals that the great imams used gradual persuasion to lead opponents to truth. In your own da'wah or conversations about religion, are you willing to adopt a similar patient strategy, or are you tempted to force the truth bluntly? What concrete step can you take this week to practice such gradual guidance?

Warning Against False Attributions to Sunni Imams
Author laments false attributions and warns transmitters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of verifying statements attributed to Sunni imams and the responsibility of scholars in transmitting them.

Core Idea
Not every attributed statement is authentic; scholars must expose corrupt transmissions.

In this passage, the author addresses a serious methodological issue: the transmission of false statements attributed to revered Sunni imams. He laments that even shocking statements are circulated without verification, and warns that those who transmit them without clarification bear responsibility before Allah. This lesson explores his caution and its implications for scholarly integrity.

Guided Path
1
Contextualize the passage
The author references a previous discussion about al-Qāḍī and al-Ustādh. Understand that these are reference scholars and the statements attributed to them are relatively mild compared to what follows.
2
Identify the author's lament
Notice that he describes being 'afflicted' with false attributions. He emphasizes that only Allah knows the truth of what the imams actually said and in what context.
3
Grasp the warning
The author warns that transmitters of corrupt statements must clarify the corruption or defend the scholar (if possible). Failure to do so brings accountability before Allah.
This is a reminder of the weight of scholarly transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلتُ : وإذا قال هذا في مقالة القاضي والأستاذ معَ خفَّتِها بالنسبة إلى ما نقل عن إمام الحرمين .. فكيف بتلك المقالة الشنيعة التي نُقِلَتْ عن الإمام ممَّا لا يرضى أنْ يقولَها مَنْ هو أدنى منه علماً وديناً بمراتب كثيرة ؟! ولقد ابتلينا بأقوال باطلةٍ تُنسَبُ لأئمَّةِ السنَّةِ ، والله تعالى يعلم هل صدرَتْ منهم أم لا ، وعلى تقدير صدورها فعلى أي وجه صدرَتْ (١) ، وهو سبحانه حسيبُ مَنْ ينقلُ مثل هذهِ الأقوال الفاسدة على وجهِ يتراخي في بيان فسادِها أو دفعِها عمَّنْ لا تليقُ بهِ إِنْ أمكنه ذلكَ ، وبالله التوفيق .
I say: If this is said regarding the statement of al-Qāḍī and al-Ustādh, despite its lightness compared to what was transmitted from Imām al-Ḥaramayn, then how [will it be] regarding that odious statement which was transmitted from the Imām, something that one who is far below him in knowledge and religion would not consent to say? And indeed we have been afflicted with false statements attributed to the Imāms of the Sunnah, and Allah Almighty knows whether they issued from them or not, and on the assumption of their issuance, in what manner did they issue? And He, glorified, is the reckoner of whoever transmits such corrupt statements in a manner that he is negligent in clarifying their corruption or in defending them from [criticism] if he is able to do so. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is the author's personal comment and warning. He begins by invoking a previous discussion about statements attributed to al-Qāḍī and al-Ustādh, which are relatively mild. He then compares them to a more shocking statement attributed to Imām al-Ḥaramayn (likely al-Juwaynī) that even a lesser scholar would not utter. The author laments that false statements are frequently attributed to Sunni imams, and only Allah knows whether they really said them, and if they did, in what context. He warns that whoever transmits such corrupt statements without clarifying their corruption or defending the scholar (if possible) will be held accountable by Allah. The passage concludes with a prayer for success.

Key Terms

إمام الحرمين
الأقوال الفاسدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every statement attributed to a Sunni imam is authentic and accurately represents their position.
Correction: The author warns that many false statements are circulated; verification and context are essential.

Checkpoint
What does the author caution against regarding statements attributed to Sunni imams?
A) Accepting them without question
B) Transmitting them without clarifying their corruption or defending the scholar
C) Rejecting them outright
D) Only transmitting the mild ones

Key Takeaway
Scholars must verify attributions and expose false ones, as transmitting corrupt statements without clarification incurs accountability before Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
How often do you critically examine the religious information you receive, especially when it seems to support your own opinions? Are you willing to reject a convenient narrative if it misrepresents a scholar of the Sunnah?

Demonstrative Proof Using Divine Will
Al-Muqtarih's burhani proof using divine will and his rationale for the shift.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Muqtarih's alternative proof that God alone effects all things, and why this proof is superior to the earlier ad hominem argument.

Core Idea
Divine will must be universally effective, or we admit two gods.

Al-Muqtarih refines his earlier proof. The first was effective only against the Mu'tazila's specific premise. Now he presents a purely demonstrative argument that stands independent of opponents' assumptions. It shows that God's will must be as universal as His power, and any violation implies a second deity.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof itself
Start by reading the central argument: because God's power attaches to every possible act, His will must also attach universally. If He wills an act and another brings it about, that defeats His will – which is impossible.
2
Understand why the shift matters
Notice the author's explanation: the first argument relied on the Mu'tazila denying that human acts are ever within God's power. The new argument does not depend on that premise.
This shows a key principle in kalam: to make arguments that work for all opponents.
3
Connect to the broader doctrine
Reflect on how this proof establishes God's exclusive influence (ta'thir) over all things, refuting views that grant any originated power real effect.
This counters positions attributed to Imām al-Ḥaramayn, al-Qāḍī, and al-Ustādh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقرَّرَ المقترح هذا الدليل على وجه آخرَ ، وذلكَ أنْ قالَ : ( كما عم تعلُّق قدرتِهِ تعالى ؛ بمعنى : أنَّ كلَّ ممكن يتأَنَّى مِنَ الصانع فعله .. فكذلك لا بدَّ أنْ يريد وجودَهُ أو انتفاءه ؛ لعموم تعلقِ الإرادة ، فإذا كانَ الفعلُ معلوم الثبوتِ مثلاً .. وجبَ أنْ يكونَ مراداً ، وإذا قصد إلى إيقاعِهِ وأوقعَهُ غيرُهُ .. كانَ ذلكَ تحقيقاً لعدم نفوذ إرادته ونفوذ إرادة غيرِهِ ، وذلكَ الذي منعناه عندَ إبطال القول بإلهين ((۱) وإنَّما عدلَ المقترَحُ عن التقريرِ الأَوَّلِ إلى هذا التقرير ؛ لأنَّه أرادَ أن يجعلَ الحجَّةَ برهانيَّة لا إلزاميَّةً ؛ لأنَّ التقرير الأَوَّلَ إِنَّما تمَّ على المعتزلة لقولهم : إِنَّ أفعال العبادِ الاختياريَّةَ غير مقدورةٍ لهُ تعالى ، ولو كانوا يقولون بأنَّها لم تزل مقدورةً لهُ تعالى بمعنى تأتي أنْ يفعلها ، وأنَّ تعلق القدرة الحادثة بإيقاعِها إنَّما هو بمشيئتِهِ تعالى .. لم يُرَدَّ عليهم بمثل ذلك ، بخلاف هذا التقرير الذي قرَّرَ بهِ المقترح الدلالة ؛ فإِنَّهُ برهان على انفراده تعالى بالتأثير في جميع الكائناتِ ، وأنَّهُ لا تأثير للقدرة الحادثة في شيءٍ مِنَ الأفعال على كلِّ حالٍ مِنَ الأحوالِ ، حتى يُرَدَّ بِهِ ما حُكِيَ عن إمامِ الحرمين، وما حكي عن القاضي والأستاذ ، والله أعلم.
And al-Muqtarih presented this proof in another way, and that is that he said: (Just as the attachment of His power, exalted be He, is general – meaning: every possible act can be done by the Maker – so likewise He must will its existence or non-existence, due to the generality of the attachment of will. So if the act is known to exist, for example, then it must be willed. And if He intends to bring it about and another brings it about, that would be a realization of the non-penetration of His will and the penetration of another's will, and that is what we denied when nullifying the statement of two gods.) And indeed al-Muqtarih turned from the first formulation to this formulation because he wanted to make the argument demonstrative (burhāniyya) not ad hominem (ilzāmiyya); because the first formulation was only valid against the Mu'tazila due to their saying that the voluntary acts of servants are not within the power of the Exalted, and if they were to say that they never ceased to be within His power in the sense that it is possible for Him to do them, and that the attachment of the originated power to their occurrence is only by His will, exalted be He, then they would not be refuted by such an argument – unlike this formulation by which al-Muqtarih presented the indication; for it is a demonstration of His exclusivity, exalted be He, in effecting all contingent things, and that there is no effect of the originated power in any of the acts in any state whatsoever, such that it refutes what was narrated from Imām al-Ḥaramayn and what was narrated from al-Qāḍī and al-Ustādh. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative formulation of the proof by al-Muqtarih, shifting from an ilzāmi (ad hominem) argument to a burhāni (demonstrative) one. The reasoning is: God's power extends universally to every possible act; similarly, His will must also extend universally—every act that occurs must be willed by God. If someone else brings about an act that God intends, that violates the efficacy of God's will. This is akin to positing two gods, which is impossible. The author then explains why this formulation is superior: the first formulation relied on the Mu'tazila's premise that human acts are not within God's power; if they had held the opposite (that human acts are always within God's power even if not actualized), that argument would not refute them. The new formulation is a pure demonstration that God alone has real influence (ta'thir) over all things, and the originated power has no effect whatsoever. This also counters views attributed to Imām al-Ḥaramayn, al-Qāḍī, and al-Ustādh.

Key Terms
برهاني
إلزامي
إرادة
تأثير
انفراد
مقترح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Both formulations are equally valid and serve the same purpose.
Correction: The first formulation is only valid against the Mu'tazila's premise that human acts are never within God's power. The second is universally demonstrative, working regardless of the opponent's position.

Checkpoint
Why did al-Muqtarih prefer the second formulation over the first?
A) Because the first was too long
B) Because the first only worked against the Mu'tazila's specific premise
C) Because the second uses divine will instead of power
D) Because the second is simpler

Key Takeaway
Al-Muqtarih's burhani argument shows that God's will, like His power, must be universally effective, establishing His exclusive influence over all contingent things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that God's will must be universally effective. Is there an area of your life where you act as if your will, not God's, determines the outcome? What would change if you truly believed that every act you commit is directly willed by Allah?
?
The passage says the proof refutes any attribution of real causality to created powers. Do you live as though your choices have ultimate causal power, or do you live as though Allah is the only true agent? Which belief shapes your decision-making today?
?
Al-Muqtarih shifted from an ad hominem to a demonstrative argument. Are you satisfied with arguments that only defeat opponents, or do you seek proofs that stand on their own? How does that apply to your own understanding of tawheed?

Why the Proof Was Reformulated: From Ilzām to Burhān
Explains why the proof was changed from ilzāmī to burhānī against the Qadariyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proposer changed the proof from an ilzāmī (compelling) argument to a burhānī (demonstrative) one, and how this change makes the argument universally effective against different theological schools.

Core Idea
Demonstrative proofs stand on their own; compelling proofs depend on the opponent's premises.

Sanusi explains a key methodological shift in kalām argumentation: moving from a dialectical (ilzāmī) proof that only refutes those who already hold certain premises, to a demonstrative (burhānī) proof that establishes the truth independently. This passage reveals why the proposer changed the formulation of the cosmological argument, ensuring it refutes not only the Mu'tazila but also later theologians who affirm divine power over human acts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two types of proofs
Learn the difference between an ilzāmī argument (which compels the opponent using their own premises) and a burhānī argument (which demonstrates the truth from first principles).
This distinction is central to kalām methodology.
2
Identify the problem with the first formulation
Notice that the first formulation only worked against the Mu'tazila because they denied God's power over human acts. If an opponent accepted that God has power over acts, the argument would not refute them.
3
See the advantage of the second formulation
The second formulation is a burhān that proves God alone has real effect in all things. This refutes any position that grants efficacy to created power, including those of Imām al-Ḥaramayn, al-Qāḍī, and al-Ustādh.
Thus the proof becomes universally applicable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إرادة غيرِهِ ، وذلكَ الذي منعناه عندَ إبطال القول بإلهين وإنَّما عدلَ المقترَحُ عن التقريرِ الأَوَّلِ إلى هذا التقرير ؛ لأنَّه أرادَ أن يجعلَ الحجَّةَ برهانيَّة لا إلزاميَّةً ؛ لأنَّ التقرير الأَوَّلَ إِنَّما تمَّ على المعتزلة لقولهم : إِنَّ أفعال العبادِ الاختياريَّةَ غير مقدورةٍ لهُ تعالى ، ولو كانوا يقولون بأنَّها لم تزل مقدورةً لهُ تعالى بمعنى تأتي أنْ يفعلها ، وأنَّ تعلق القدرة الحادثة بإيقاعِها إنَّما هو بمشيئتِهِ تعالى .. لم يُرَدَّ عليهم بمثل ذلك ، بخلاف هذا التقرير الذي قرَّرَ بهِ المقترح الدلالة ؛ فإِنَّهُ برهان على انفراده تعالى بالتأثير في جميع الكائناتِ ، وأنَّهُ لا تأثير للقدرة الحادثة في شيءٍ مِنَ الأفعال على كلِّ حالٍ مِنَ الأحوالِ ، حتى يُرَدَّ بِهِ ما حُكِيَ عن إمامِ الحرمين، وما حكي عن القاضي والأستاذ ، والله أعلم.
...the will of another, and that is what we denied when refuting the statement that there are two gods. The proposer only changed from the first formulation to this one because he wanted to make the proof demonstrative (burhāniyya) rather than compelling (ilzāmiyya). For the first formulation only succeeded against the Mu‘tazila because they say that the voluntary actions of servants are not within God’s power. But if they were to say that He has always had power over them in the sense that He can create them, and that the connection of the originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) to performing them is only by His will—then they would not have been refuted by such argument. This formulation, however, which the proposer established as the proof, is a demonstration that He alone has effect in all things, and that the originated power has no effect in any action in any state whatsoever, so as to refute by it what was reported from Imām al-Ḥaramayn and what was reported from al-Qāḍī and al-Ustādh. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage is a methodological explanation clarifying why the proposer (al-muqtaḥ) changed the formulation of the proof from an ilzāmī (compelling by the opponent's premises) to a burhānī (demonstrative) argument. The author states that the original argument only worked against the Mu‘tazila because they deny that God has power over human voluntary acts (they assert that humans create their own actions). If the Mu‘tazila were to instead affirm that God has always had power over these acts, i.e., He can create them, and that the human's originated power (qudra ḥāditha) only connects to the act by God's will, then that first proof would not refute them. So the proposer presents a second, demonstrative formulation: it proves that God alone has real effect (ta’thīr) in all created things, and that the originated power has no effect in any action at all. This refutes positions attributed to Imām al-Ḥaramayn (al-Juwaynī), al-Qāḍī (al-Bāqillānī), and al-Ustādh (Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāyīnī), who held some form of human power. The phrase 'wa Allāhu a‘lam' indicates submission to God's knowledge.

Key Terms
برهاني
إلزامي
قدرة حادثة
تأثير
كائنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the change of argument shows inconsistency or that the first formulation was invalid.
Correction: The change reflects a different level of dialectical engagement: the first argument is compelling only against those who share specific premises (Mu'tazila), while the second is demonstrative and universally valid.

Checkpoint
Why did the proposer change the first formulation of the proof to a second one?
A) Because the first formulation was invalid.
B) Because he wanted to make it a demonstrative (burhānī) argument that works even against those who affirm God's power over human acts.
C) Because the first formulation only refuted the Mu'tazila but not later opponents.
D) Because the second formulation is easier to understand.

Key Takeaway
The shift from ilzāmī to burhānī marks a methodological maturation in kalām, ensuring proofs are universally valid rather than dialectically contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every single action you take—from the smallest gesture to the biggest decision—is under God's sole power, or do you still feel a sense of independent ownership? How does the burhānī proof challenge the way you think about your own choices today?
?
The proposer changed his argument to make it universal, not depending on what others already accept. In your own faith, do you rely on demonstrations from reason and revelation that stand on their own, or have you been leaning on premises that others might not share? What would it take for your conviction to become like that burhānī proof—unshakeable regardless of the audience?
?
If the ilzāmī proof only works against those who deny God's power, what does it say about you if you still argue as if God's control is limited? Have you ever caught yourself acting or thinking as if your own power is independent, even after knowing this truth? What will you do to remind yourself that God alone has real effect in all things?

Cumulative Certainty from Scriptural Evidence
Sanusi argues that multiple scriptural proofs together yield certainty of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why abundant apparent scriptural proofs can yield certainty of the vision of God, even if individual proofs are not explicit.

Core Idea
Many apparent proofs agreeing on one meaning create certainty.

In the previous lessons, Sanusi presented individual scriptural proofs for the vision of God. Now he addresses a potential objection: how can these proofs produce certainty if each is only apparent (zāhir) and not an explicit text (naṣṣ)? His answer is a key principle in uṣūl al-fiqh: the accumulation of many apparent proofs that converge on the same meaning can reach the level of certainty. This lesson explains that principle and shows how it resolves doubts raised by Fakhr al-Rāzī.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that Sanusi acknowledges that each individual proof is apparent, not explicit.
2
Understand the cumulative certainty principle
Identify the key phrase: 'due to their abundance and agreement on one meaning, they yield certainty.' Reflect on how multiple apparent proofs can together produce certainty.
3
See the refutation and examples
Note how Ibn al-Tilmisani used this argument to refute al-Razi. Also note that some proofs individually are nearly explicit, like Moses' request and the hadith 'You will see your Lord.'
This strengthens the case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذه الأدلَّةُ ونحوها مِنْ أدلَّةِ السمع وإنْ كانَ كلُّ واحدٍ منها ظاهراً ليس بنص . . فهي لكثرتها وتواطئها على معنى واحد تفيد القطع بالرؤية ، وإلى هذا المعنى أشرتُ بقولي : ( والظواهر إذا كثُرَتْ ... ) إلى آخرِه . وقد أشار إلى هذا المعنى شرفُ الدين بنُ التِّلِمْساني رحمهُ اللهُ تعالى ردّاً على الإمام الفخرِ في ميله إلى عدم القطع بجواز الرؤيةِ لمَّا لم يتضح له الدليل العقلي عليها ، والأدلة السمعيَّةُ رآها ليسَتْ بنص ، فردَّ عليهِ بما سبق ؛ وهو ظاهر ، على أنَّ بعض تلك الأدلة على الانفراد - كسؤالِ موسى عليهِ السلامُ للرؤيةِ - يكادُ أنْ يكونَ نصاً في جواز الرؤية ، ويقربُ منه حديث : « سَتَرَوْنَ رَبَّكُمْ ، فَإِنَّهُ نَص
These proofs and similar scriptural proofs — even if each of them is apparent and not explicit — yet due to their abundance and agreement on one meaning, they yield certainty of vision. And it is to this meaning that I alluded in my saying: 'And the apparent proofs when they become abundant...' etc. And Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilmisani, may God have mercy on him, alluded to this meaning in refutation of Imam Fakhr [al-Razi] in his inclination toward not being certain of the permissibility of vision because the rational proof was not clear to him, and he considered the scriptural proofs not explicit. So he [Tilmisani] refuted him with what preceded; and it is evident. Moreover, some of those proofs individually — like Moses' request for vision — almost amounts to an explicit text on the permissibility of vision, and the hadith 'You will see your Lord' is close to it, for it is an explicit text.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage discusses the cumulative strength of the scriptural proofs for the vision of God. The author argues that although each scriptural proof (like the ones mentioned earlier) might be only apparent (zāhir) and not explicit (naṣṣ), their multitude and agreement on the same meaning produce certainty (qaṭʿ). He refers to his own statement elsewhere about apparent proofs when abundant. He then mentions that Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilmisani used this very argument to refute Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, who hesitated to affirm certainty because he found no definitive rational proof and considered the scriptural proofs not explicit. The author adds that some of these proofs individually are nearly explicit, such as Moses' request and the hadith about seeing the Lord. The hadith is considered a clear text on the subject.

Key Terms
الظواهر إذا كثرت
القطع
التواطؤ
النص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that a single non-explicit proof is insufficient, and that if no single proof is explicit, then no certainty is possible.
Correction: The author explains that abundance and agreement of many apparent proofs can produce certainty, even if each individually is not explicit.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what makes the abundant apparent scriptural proofs yield certainty?
A) Each proof is a clear text.
B) Their abundance and agreement on one meaning.
C) They are all rational proofs.
D) The consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
Abundant apparent proofs that agree on one meaning can yield certainty, even if each alone is not explicit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult theological question, do you insist on a single clear verse or rational proof, or do you trust a weight of cumulative evidence from multiple sources? What does your tendency reveal about your relationship with certainty?
?
Moses, a prophet, asked to see God. If you were in his place, would you have been satisfied with the scriptural proofs (even abundant ones) or would you also demand a direct vision? How does your answer reflect your level of reliance on revelation?
?
Sanusi argues that abundant apparent proofs can yield certainty. In your own life, do you treat God's signs (in creation, scripture, and personal experience) as cumulative proof, or do you dismiss them because each alone seems insufficient? What would change if you truly adopted this principle?

Refuting the Claim on Negated Indefinite Nouns
Sanusi refutes a false claim and states the correct rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why indefinite nouns in the scope of negation imply generality, contrary to a false claim attributed to scholars of rhetoric.

Core Idea
Indefinite nouns in negation always imply generality.

Sanusi begins by reporting a claim that indefinite nouns in negation do not imply general negation. He immediately refutes this, stating that no scholar of rhetoric or any proponent of generality has ever said so. This lesson will show you the correct position as established by the scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: a claim is reported, then refuted, then the correct rule is given.
Pay attention to the key terms: عموم النفي and نفي العموم.
2
Identify the false claim
The false claim is that a negated noun (definite or indefinite) does not imply general negation but rather negation of generality. Recognize this as the position that is being refuted.
3
Understand the refutation
Sanusi states that no scholar of rhetoric or anyone who affirms generality has ever said this. Instead, they hold that indefinite nouns in negation imply generality—implicitly with other than لَا الجنسية and explicitly with لَا الجنسية.
This is the core doctrinal point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمنها : حكايتُهُ عن علماء المعاني أنَّهم نصُّوا على أنَّ الجمعَ المنفي مُعرَّفاً أو مُنكَراً لا يفيد عموم النفي ، وإنَّما يفيد نفي العموم ، وهذا شيء لم ينص عليه أحدٌ منهم ولا مِنْ غيرِهم ؛ أعني : مِنْ كُلِّ مَنْ يقولُ بالعموم ، بل نضُوا على ضدّه في النكرة ، وأنَّها إذا كانَتْ في سياق النفْيِ تعمُّ ظاهراً معَ غيرِ ( لا ) الجنسيَّةِ ، ونصّاً معَ ( لا ) الجنسية (١) ، ولا فرق في ذلك بين أن تكون النكرة مفردةً ؛ نحو : لا رجل ، أو مثناةً ؛ نحو: لا رجلين ، أو مجموعة ؛ نحو: لا رجال ، وهذا ممَّا لا يختلفونَ فيهِ ، وإِنَّما نزاعُهم في استغراقِ المفرد : هل هو أشملُ مِن استغراق المثنى والمجموع ؛ وهو الذي نص عليه القزويني تبعاً للسكاكي (٢) ، أم هو في الجميع على حد السواء ؛ وإليهِ ميْلُ التفتازاني ، وبحثه وحججه في ذلك مشهورةٌ في مُطوَّلِهِ على التلخيص (٣).
Among them: his narration from the scholars of rhetoric that they stated that the negated noun, whether definite or indefinite, does not imply general negation, but rather implies negation of generality. This is something none of them stated, nor anyone else; I mean: from everyone who affirms generality. Rather, they stated the opposite regarding the indefinite noun: that when it is in the scope of negation, it implies generality implicitly with other than (lā) of genus, and explicitly with (lā) of genus (1). There is no difference in that between the indefinite being singular: e.g. lā rajul, or dual: e.g. lā rajulayn, or plural: e.g. lā rijāl. This is something they do not differ upon. Their dispute is only about the comprehensiveness of the singular: whether it is more comprehensive than the comprehensiveness of dual and plural – which is what al-Qazwīnī stated following al-Sakkākī (2) – or it is equal in all; and the inclination of al-Taftāzānī is towards that, and his research and proofs on that are well-known in his lengthy commentary on al-Talkhīṣ (3).
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. The author begins by stating a claim attributed to scholars of rhetoric: that a negated noun, whether definite or indefinite, does not imply general negation but rather implies negation of generality. The author firmly rebuts this, asserting that no scholar of rhetoric or anyone who affirms generality has ever stated this. On the contrary, they have stated the opposite for indefinite nouns: an indefinite noun in the scope of negation implies generality. This is obvious (ẓāhir) with other than lā al-jinsiyya, and explicit (naṣṣ) with lā al-jinsiyya. The author then clarifies that this ruling applies uniformly whether the indefinite is singular (e.g., 'no man'), dual (e.g., 'no two men'), or plural (e.g., 'no men'). There is no disagreement on this point. The only dispute among scholars is about the extent of generality: whether the singular is more comprehensive than the dual and plural (the view of al-Qazwīnī following al-Sakkākī), or whether all three are equal in generality (the view favored by al-Taftāzānī). The author notes that al-Taftāzānī’s research and proofs on this are well-known in his lengthy commentary on al-Talkhīṣ.

Key Terms
عموم النفي
نفي العموم
نكرة
لا الجنسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Indefinite nouns in negation do not imply generality; they imply negation of generality.
Correction: The correct position is that indefinite nouns in negation imply generality, as stated by scholars of rhetoric and proponents of generality.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what do scholars of rhetoric actually hold regarding indefinite nouns in the scope of negation?
A) They do not imply generality.
B) They imply generality implicitly with other than lā al-jinsiyya and explicitly with lā al-jinsiyya.
C) They imply negation of generality.
D) They imply generality only when the noun is definite.

Key Takeaway
Indefinite nouns in negation always imply generality, as affirmed by scholars of rhetoric.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'la ilaha illallah', do you truly understand that the negation 'la ilaha' implies generality—meaning you reject every single false deity, not just some? Are there any 'gods' in your life (status, wealth, desires) that you have not fully negated?
?
This lesson teaches that indefinite nouns in negation are general in scope. Is there an area in your life where you think 'I don't do X (a specific sin)' but you allow related minor sins, assuming the negation is partial? How can you apply the rule of generality to your repentance?

Generality Across Numbers and a Scholarly Dispute
Sanusi shows uniform rule and introduces a dispute on comprehensiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the rule of generality applies equally to singular, dual, and plural indefinite nouns, and learn the scholarly dispute about whether the singular is more comprehensive.

Core Idea
Singular, dual, and plural indefinite nouns are equal in implying generality.

After establishing the rule, Sanusi clarifies that it applies regardless of number—singular, dual, or plural—using clear examples. He then mentions a minor dispute among later scholars about whether the singular is more comprehensive than the dual and plural. This lesson explores that nuance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the examples for singular, dual, and plural.
The examples are: لا رجل, لا رجلين, لا رجال.
2
Understand the uniform application
Sanusi emphasizes that there is no difference in the ruling: all indefinite nouns in negation imply generality, regardless of number.
This is agreed upon by all.
3
Learn the dispute about comprehensiveness
The only disagreement is whether the singular is more comprehensive than dual/plural (al-Qazwīnī) or equal (al-Taftāzānī). Know the views and the key figures.
Al-Taftāzānī's arguments are in his commentary on al-Talkhīṣ.

Definite Nouns with Negation Imply Generality
Sanusi argues that definite nouns in negation are general, citing textual evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why definite nouns in negation are considered general in Islamic legal theory and how textual evidence supports this view.

Core Idea
Definite nouns with negation are general, not specific.

This passage tackles a key methodological point in usul al-fiqh and kalam: the scope of definite nouns when negated. Sanusi defends the position that such nouns imply generality, citing Quranic verses and a hadith. We'll examine his reasoning and evidence to see how this principle is grounded in textual usage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Notice the author's claim and the examples he provides.
2
Identify key terms
Look for the terms al-muʿarraf (definite noun), an-nafy (negation), ʿāmm (general), and mujarrad (generic noun). Understand how they relate to the argument.
3
Examine the examples
Go through each example from the Quran and hadith. Ask yourself: How does each example show that a definite noun in negation is general?
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this principle matters for interpreting Islamic legal texts. How would understanding a definite noun as general affect rulings?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا المعرَّفُ الذي تعلَّقَ بهِ النفْيُ فلم ينصُّوا على أنَّهُ ليسَ بعام ، بل ظاهر كلامهم أنَّهُ معَ النفْيِ كالمجرَّدِ ، وأكثر الاستعمال على ذلك ; نحو : ﴿ لَا يُحِبُّ الظَّالِمِينَ ﴾ [آل عمران : ٥٧] ، لَا يُحِبُّ الْمُعْتَدِينَ [البقرة : ١٩٠ ] ، ( وما للظالمين من ولي ولا نصير ) (٤) ، ومَا عَلَى الْمُحْسِنِينَ مِن سبيل ﴾ [التوبة : ٩١] ، وفي الحديثِ : « لَا تَقْتُلُوا النِّسَاءَ وَلا الصَّبْيَانَ » (١) ، ومثل ذلك كثير
As for the definite noun connected to negation, they did not state that it is not general; rather, the apparent meaning of their words is that it is like a generic noun with negation, and most usage confirms that: e.g. {Allah does not love the wrongdoers} [Āl ʿImrān: 57], {Allah does not love the aggressors} [al-Baqara: 190], {And the wrongdoers have no protector nor helper} (4), {And there is no way against the doers of good} [al-Tawba: 91], and in the ḥadīth: 'Do not kill women nor children' (1), and many such examples.
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage is an argument from usage. The author addresses definite nouns (al-muʿarraf) that are connected with negation. He states that scholars did not claim that such nouns are not general; rather, the apparent sense of their words indicates that such a noun with negation is like a generic noun (ka al-mujarrad) – that is, it implies generality. The author supports this by citing numerous examples from the Quran and hadith: verses like 'Allah does not love the wrongdoers' and 'the wrongdoers have no protector nor helper', and the hadith 'Do not kill women nor children'. These examples show that definite nouns in negation are understood as general. The author concludes that such examples are abundant (wa mithlu dhālika kathīr).

Correction: The author shows that scholarly practice and usage indicate they are general, as evidenced by Quran and hadith.

Checkpoint

A) It is not general.
B) It is like a generic noun and implies generality.
C) It implies negation of generality.
D) Its ruling is unknown.

Key Takeaway
Definite nouns in negation are understood as general, as evidenced by Quranic and hadith usage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Rejecting a Faulty Analogy: 'al' vs 'kull' in Negation
Sanusi rejects a linguistic analogy comparing 'al' and 'kull' in negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi rejects the linguistic analogy that treats negation with 'al' and 'kull' as equivalent regarding the negation of generality.

Core Idea
Not all analogies hold: 'al' and 'kull' differ in negation of generality.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a methodological issue: can we infer a linguistic rule from one grammatical construction to another? He strongly rejects an analogy that attempts to treat negation with 'al' (the definite article) the same as negation with 'kull' (every) regarding the negation of generality. Let's read his argument and see his evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to grasp Sanusi's main point.
Focus on the two reasons he gives for rejecting the analogy.
2
Identify the two reasons
Find the two distinct arguments Sanusi provides against the analogy: one about the use of 'al' for essence, and one about the Quranic usage of 'kull' with general negation.
The second reason is supported by three Quranic verses.
3
Reflect on the evidence
Consider how the Quranic verses demonstrate that even with 'kull', negation can imply negation of generality, undermining the analogy.
This shows the importance of actual usage over abstract analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتخريج سلْبِ العموم في تعلُّقِ النفي بـ ( أل ) على سلْبِ العموم في تعلقِهِ بـ ( كلَّ ) .. قياس في اللغة معَ ظهورِ الفارقِ ؛ لاحتمال استعمال ( أل ) مع أداة العموم للحقيقة لا للاستغراق ، على أَنَّهُ قدِ اسْتُعْمِلَ العموم السلب معَ ( كل ( أيضاً كثيراً ، ومنه : ﴿ وَاللهُ لَا يُحِبُّ كُلَّ مُخْتَالٍ كَلَّ فَخُورٍ [الحديد: ۲۳]، ﴿ وَاللهُ لَا يُحِبُّ كُلَّ كَفَّارٍ أَثِيمٍ [البقرة: ٢٧٦]، ﴾ وَلَا تُطِعْ كُلَّ حَلافٍ مَّهِينٍ ﴾ [القلم : ١٠ ] .
And deriving the negation of generality regarding the attachment of negation to (al) from the negation of generality regarding its attachment to (kull) is an analogy in language despite the clear difference: because of the possibility that (al) is used with the tool of generality for the essence, not for comprehensiveness, and also because general negation with (kull) has been used frequently, including: {And Allah does not love every conceited boaster} [al-Ḥadīd: 23], {And Allah does not love every sinful disbeliever} [al-Baqara: 276], {And do not obey every worthless swearer} [al-Qalam: 10].
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of a purported analogy. The author notes that someone tried to derive (takhrij) the rule that negation with (al) implies negation of generality from the similar rule with (kull) ('every'). He rejects this analogy as a faulty analogy in language (qiyās fī al-lugha) because there is a clear difference (ẓuhūr al-fāriq). The difference is that (al) when attached to a general tool like 'kull' may be used to indicate the essence (al-ḥaqīqa) rather than comprehensiveness (al-istighrāq). Furthermore, the author points out that general negation (al-ʿumūm al-salab) with (kull) itself has been used frequently in the Quran, as in the verses cited: 'Allah does not love every conceited boaster', 'Allah does not love every sinful disbeliever', and 'Do not obey every worthless swearer'. These examples show that even with (kull), negation can imply negation of generality, so the analogy does not prove what the opponent wants.

Key Terms
سلْب العموم
أل
كل
قياس في اللغة
ظهور الفارق
الحقيقة
الاستغراق
عموم سلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negation with 'al' and 'kull' are analogous in implying negation of generality.
Correction: Sanusi shows they differ: 'al' can indicate essence, and 'kull' itself is used with general negation in the Quran.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the analogy comparing negation with 'al' and 'kull'?
A) Because 'al' is never used with negation.
B) Because 'al' may be used for the essence, not comprehensiveness, and 'kull' itself is used with general negation frequently.
C) Because the Quran does not use 'kull' with negation.
D) Because the scholars agreed on the difference.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi rejects the analogy between negation with 'al' and 'kull' because 'al' can indicate essence, not comprehensiveness, and Quranic usage shows 'kull' itself negates generality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that 'Allah does not love every conceited boaster', do you feel secure or unsettled? Does this verse make you examine your own heart for hidden pride? What would it mean for you to take this verse as a personal warning, not just a general statement?
?
Sanusi rejected an analogy because of a 'clear difference'. Is there an assumption you have held in your faith—perhaps about God's mercy or judgment—that is based on an analogy you have never questioned? Are you willing to test that assumption against the Quran and sound reasoning, even if it means revising a comfortable belief?

Refuting the Misinterpretation of 'No Men in the House'
Sanusi refutes a critic's inference using the example of 'lā rijāl fī al-dār'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the statement 'There are no men in the house' can be true even with one or two men without implying negation of generality, and how the scope of singular versus plural generality resolves the apparent objection.

Core Idea
The truth of 'No men in the house' with one man is about scope, not negation of generality.

Sanusi confronts a common objection to his understanding of negation and generality. Critics point out that 'There are no men in the house' is true even when one or two men are present, claiming this shows the negation applies to the whole indefinite plural. Sanusi explains that this inference is flawed: the truth arises from the different scopes of singular and plural generality. Let's examine his careful distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the literal translation. Note the key terms 'استغراق المفرد' and 'الجمع العام'.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the critic's claim
The critic argues that the truth of 'There are no men in the house' when one or two men are present proves that negation on an indefinite plural negates generality. Mark where Sanusi calls this 'istidlāl fāsid' (corrupt inference).
The critic's error is the core of the refutation.
3
Understand Sanusi's explanation of scope
Sanusi says the truth of the example is due to the fact that the generality of the singular (al-mufrad) encompasses one and above, while the general plural only encompasses units of plurals, not singulars or duals. Grasp this distinction.
Compare with the exclusion of 'woman' from 'man'.
4
Recognize why the inference is invalid
The critic wrongly thought the exclusion of singular/dual from plural was due to negation of generality. Sanusi shows it is because singular/dual are not part of the plural's signification at all.
Misunderstanding of Ibn al-Tilimsānī's point.
5
Apply the principle to the example
Test the principle: 'lā rijāl' is true when there are one or two men because 'rijāl' as a plural does not cover singular/dual; the negation thus applies to the plural only, not to singulars/duals as separate items.
This preserves the distinction between negation of generality and general negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : ( بدليل صدقِ قولنا : « لا رجال في الدارِ » إذا كانَ فيها رجلٌ أو رجلان ( استدلال فاسدٌ ; لأنَّ صدق هذا المثالِ بوجودِ رجل أو رجلينِ إِنَّما هو بناءً على أنَّ استغراق المفردِ أشملُ مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّهُ يستغرقُ الواحد فما فوقه ، والجمع العام إنَّما يستغرقُ آحاد الجموع التي كان يصلحُ لها قبل العموم ، لا الواحد والمثنى ; لأنَّهما ليسا مِنَ مدلول الجمعِ ، فخروجُهما عندَ مَنْ يقولُ بِهِ لهذا شِبْهُ خروج المرأة مِنْ عمومِ الرجل مثلاً وبالعكس (۲) ، لا لما فهمه هذا المعترضُ على ابنِ التلمساني أن خروجهما لأجل أنَّ النفي الداخل على الجمع المنكَّرِ لسلب العموم !
And his statement: 'By the proof of the truthfulness of our saying: “There are no men in the house” when there is one man or two men in it' is a corrupt inference. Because the truthfulness of this example with the existence of one man or two men is only based on the fact that the comprehensiveness of the singular is more inclusive in that it encompasses the one and above, while the general plural only encompasses the units of plurals that were applicable before generality, not the singular and dual; because they are not part of the signification of the plural. So their exclusion, according to one who holds that, is like the exclusion of a woman from the generality of 'man' for example, and vice versa (2), not because of what this objector understood from Ibn al-Tilimsānī: that their exclusion is due to the negation entering the indefinite plural being the negation of generality!
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The critic had argued that the statement 'There are no men in the house' is true even when there is one man or two men, claiming this proves that the negation on an indefinite plural implies negation of generality rather than general negation. The author declares this inference corrupt (istidlāl fāsid). He explains the reason: the truth of the statement when one or two men are present is based on the fact that the generality of the singular (al-mufrad) is more comprehensive; it includes the one and above. The general plural, on the other hand, only encompasses the individual items of plurals that were suitable for that plural before generality, not singulars or duals, because singulars and duals are not within the signification (madlūl) of the plural. Therefore, when someone holds that the plural does not include the singular and dual, it is like excluding a woman from the generality of 'man' – a natural consequence of the different scopes, not a proof of negation of generality. The author thus rejects the critic's misinterpretation of Ibn al-Tilimsānī.

Key Terms
صدق
لا رجال في الدار
استغراق المفرد
الجمع العام
مدلول الجمع
سلب العموم
ابن التلمساني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The truth of 'lā rijāl fī al-dār' with one or two men proves that negation on an indefinite plural is negation of generality (salb al-umūm).
Correction: The truth arises because the singular's generality covers one and above, while the plural does not include singular or dual by its signification; thus their exclusion is due to scope, not to negation of generality.

Checkpoint
Why does the example 'There are no men in the house' being true with one man not prove the negation of generality?
A) Because the plural includes singular and dual.
B) Because the generality of singular is more comprehensive and covers one and above, while the plural does not include singular or dual by its signification.
C) Because the statement is false if there is one man.
D) Because the critics have no proof.

Key Takeaway
The truth of 'lā rijāl fī al-dār' with one or two men is due to the scope of singular versus plural generality, not to negation of generality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals how a subtle misunderstanding about language can lead to a corrupted inference. When you read the Quran or hadith, are you quick to assume your first understanding is correct, or do you pause to check your assumptions? What areas of your faith might you be misinterpreting because you assume language works one way when it actually works another? Commit to examining one verse or hadith this week with a trusted scholar or commentary.
?
Sanūsī corrects a mistake that even a student of Ibn al-Tilimsānī made. How does your own pride react when you realize you have been confidently wrong about something in your religion? Do you humbly accept correction, or do you defend your error? What would it cost you to truly surrender your intellectual pride before the truth?

Method of Learning
intermediate
5 min
Refutation of the Qiyas on 'al' and 'kull'
Sanusi refutes the qiyas between 'al' and 'kull' for negation of generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy between the negation of generality with 'al' and with 'kull' is invalid, and how 'al' can be used for haqiqa rather than istighraq.

Core Idea
The analogy fails because 'al' differs from 'kull' in usage and scope.

Sanusi begins by refuting an opponent's attempt to equate the negation of generality when attached to the definite article 'al' with the same when attached to 'kull'. He points out a key difference: 'al' can be used for the essence (al-haqiqa) rather than comprehensive generality (al-istighraq). Moreover, he shows that 'kull' itself is often used with negative generality in the Quran, undermining the opponent's claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument flow through.
Notice the strong language: 'قياس في اللغة مع ظهور الفارق'.
2
Identify the analogy being refuted
The opponent draws an analogy between the negation of generality with 'al' and with 'kull'. Find where Sanusi states this analogy.
The phrase 'وتخريج سلْبِ العموم في تعلُّقِ النفي بـ (أل) على سلْبِ العموم في تعلقِهِ بـ (كلَّ)' shows the analogy.
3
Understand the refutation's two points
First, Sanusi argues that 'al' can be used for haqiqa not istighraq. Second, he provides Quranic examples where 'kull' is used with negative generality.
The examples show that the opponent's claim about 'kull' is not absolute.
4
Reflect on the implication
Why does the possibility of 'al' for haqiqa break the analogy? Consider that analogy requires a shared underlying principle, which is missing here.
This is a fundamental point in usul al-fiqh and kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتخريج سلْبِ العموم في تعلُّقِ النفي بـ ( أل ) على سلْبِ العموم في تعلقِهِ بـ ( كلَّ ) .. قياس في اللغة معَ ظهورِ الفارقِ ; لاحتمال استعمال ( أل ) مع أداة العموم للحقيقة لا للاستغراق ، على أَنَّهُ قدِ اسْتُعْمِلَ العموم السلب معَ ( كل ( أيضاً كثيراً ، ومنه : ﴿ وَاللهُ لَا يُحِبُّ كُلَّ مُخْتَالٍ كَلَّ فَخُورٍ [الحديد: ۲۳]، ﴿ وَاللهُ لَا يُحِبُّ كُلَّ كَفَّارٍ أَثِيمٍ [البقرة: ٢٧٦]، ﴾ وَلَا تُطِعْ كُلَّ حَلافٍ مَّهِينٍ ﴾ [القلم : ١٠ ] . وقوله : ( بدليل صدق قولنا : « لا رجال في الدارِ » إذا كانَ فيها رجلٌ أو رجلان ( استدلال فاسدٌ ; لأنَّ صدق هذا المثالِ بوجودِ رجل أو رجلينِ إِنَّما هو بناءً على أنَّ استغراق المفردِ أشملُ مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّهُ يستغرقُ الواحد فما فوقه ، والجمع العام إنَّما يستغرقُ آحاد الجموع التي كان يصلحُ لها قبل العموم ، لا الواحد والمثنى ; لأنَّهما ليسا مِنَ مدلول الجمعِ ، فخروجُهما عندَ مَنْ يقولُ بِهِ لهذا شِبْهُ خروج المرأة مِنْ عمومِ الرجل مثلاً وبالعكس (۲) ، لا لما فهمه هذا المعترضُ على ابنِ التلمساني أن خروجهما لأجل أنَّ النفي الداخل على الجمع المنكَّرِ لسلب العموم ! وهي غفلةٌ عظيمة لا يرضى بمقالتها أصاغرُ صبيانِ الكُتَابِ ; إذْ يلزمُهُ على هذا صدْقُ النفْيِ في قولنا : ( لا رجال في الدارِ ) وإِنْ وُجِدَ فيها آلاف الآلافِ مِنَ الرجالِ عندَ غَيبةِ رجل واحدٍ منها ; لأنَّ القضيَّةَ عنده جزئية سالبة ، فهي في قوَّةِ قولنا : بعضُ الرجال ليس في الدارِ ، فيصدقُ بغَيبةِ رجل واحدٍ مِنَ الدارِ أَنَّ بعض الرجالِ ـ ونريدُ : ذلكَ الرجل الواحد - لم يكن في الدار ، فتصدقُ إذا القضية وإن وُجِدَ في الدارِ رجالُ الدنيا كلُّهم سوى ذلكَ الرجل ! وقوله : ( نقولُ : هَبْ أَنَّهُ لا ينافيه ، فأينَ ما يقتضيه ؟! ) نقول : يقتضيهِ ما ذُكِرَ مِنْ أنَّ الآيةَ وردَتْ في مَعرِضِ المدحِ فحملها على خلافِ العمومِ يُخِلُّ ببلاغةِ الكلامِ . وقوله : ( إِنَّ تلك القرينة حاليَّةٌ ، فلا يُترَكُ مدلول اللفظ لأجلها ) هذا الكلامُ يقتضي انحصار قرينة المجاز في القرائن اللفظية وفساده ظاهرُ عندَ المحققينَ وإِنْ كانَ فيهِ خلافٌ وقوله : ( سلَّمنا دلالة الصيغة على العموم بهذه القرينة ) . هذا تسليم يُوجِبُ انقطاعَهُ ، وما ذكر بعده ... إلى آخرِهِ (۱) : كلام منصوبٌ في غير محله ، وكأنَّهُ لم يعرفُ مَنْ يخاطِبُ ! هذا
And deriving the negation of generality in the attachment of negation to (al) from the negation of generality in its attachment to (kull) is an analogy in language despite the clear difference; because of the possibility of using (al) with a generality tool for the reality (al-haqiqa) not for comprehensiveness (al-istighraq), and indeed negative generality has been used with (kull) frequently as well, including: {And Allah does not love every proud boaster} [al-Hadid: 23], {And Allah does not love every ungrateful sinner} [al-Baqara: 276], {And do not obey every worthless swearer} [al-Qalam: 10]. And his statement: (As evidence for the truth of our saying: 'No men are in the house' if there is a man or two men in it) is a corrupt inference; because the truth of this example with the existence of a man or two men is only based on the fact that the comprehensiveness of the singular is more inclusive in that it includes the one and above, while the general plural only includes the individuals of the plurals that were suitable for it before generality, not the singular and dual; because they are not part of the meaning of the plural. So their exclusion, according to those who hold this, is like the exclusion of a woman from the generality of men, for example, and vice versa, not for what this objector understood against Ibn al-Tilmisani that their exclusion is because the negation entering upon the indefinite plural is for the negation of generality! And it is a great heedlessness that even the youngest students of the writing schools would not accept its statement; because it would necessitate for him the truth of the negation in our saying: (No men are in the house) even if there are thousands upon thousands of men in it when a single man is absent from it; because the proposition according to him is a particular negative, so it is equivalent to our saying: Some men are not in the house, so it is true by the absence of one man from the house that some men—and we mean: that single man—was not in the house, so the proposition would be true even if all the men of the world are in the house except that man! And his statement: (We say: Grant that it does not contradict it, but where is what it requires?) We say: What it requires is what was mentioned, that the verse came in the context of praise, so interpreting it contrary to generality would impair the eloquence of the speech. And his statement: (Indeed that contextual clue (al-qarina al-haliyya) is a circumstantial one, so the literal meaning of the wording is not abandoned for its sake) This statement implies the restriction of the clue of metaphor to verbal clues, and its corruption is evident to the verifiers (al-muhaqqiqin), even if there is disagreement. And his statement: (We grant the indication of the form to generality by this clue). This is an admission that necessitates his defeat, and what he mentioned afterward... to its end: speech set up in its wrong place, as if he does not know whom he is addressing! This.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's argument concerning the scope of negation with general terms. The author first attacks the analogy (qiyas) that derives the negation of generality with 'al' from the negation of generality with 'kull', pointing out a clear difference: 'al' can be used for the reality (al-haqiqa) rather than comprehensiveness (al-istighraq). He then cites Quranic examples where 'kull' is used with negative generality. Next, he addresses the opponent's example 'la rijala fi al-dar' (no men are in the house) claimed to be true if one or two men are present. The author explains that the truth of this example is based on a confusion: the comprehensiveness of the singular (istighraq al-mufrad) covers the one and above, while the general plural (al-jam' al-'amm) covers only the individuals of that plural, not the singular or dual. Therefore, the exclusion of singular/dual is like excluding a woman from the generality of men—a structural issue, not a negation of generality. The author then shows the absurd consequence: according to the opponent's view, 'no men are in the house' would be true even if all the world's men are inside except one, because it would be a particular negative equivalent to 'some men are not in the house'. This is a clear reductio ad absurdum. The author then refutes three more objections: (1) the claim that the verse's context of praise does not require generality; the author replies that ignoring generality here disturbs eloquence. (2) The objection that the contextual clue (qarina haliyya) is circumstantial and does not override the literal meaning; the author notes that this implies restricting the clue of metaphor to verbal clues only, which is false according to the verifiers. (3) The opponent's concession that the form indicates generality but not eternally; the author dismisses this as a misplaced argument that leads to the opponent's defeat, showing he does not even know whom he is addressing.

Key Terms
سلْب العموم
الاستغراق
الحقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main reason Sanusi gives for rejecting the analogy between negation with 'al' and negation with 'kull'?
A) 'al' is never used for generality
B) 'al' can be used for the reality (al-haqiqa) not just comprehensiveness
C) 'kull' is never used with negation
D) The Quranic verses prove the opposite

Key Takeaway
The negation of generality with 'al' is not equivalent to that with 'kull' due to different possible uses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse like {Allah does not love every proud boaster}, do you instinctively assume it means 'Allah does not love any proud boaster'? How does this lesson correct a potential hasty interpretation in your own reading of the Quran?
?
The author emphasizes a 'clear difference' (farq) in linguistic usage. Are there areas in your life where you ignore clear differences to make convenient analogies? Commit to identifying one such area today.
?
The opponent made a qiyas based on superficial similarity. In your own learning, have you ever accepted an argument simply because it looked similar to something you already believed? Write down a belief you will re-examine after this lesson.

Method of Learning
intermediate

Correcting the 'La Rijala fi al-Dar' Example
Sanusi corrects the misunderstanding of the example 'لا رجال في الدار'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the example 'لا رجال في الدار' does not prove the opponent's point about negation generality, and how the distinction between general plural and singular comprehensiveness resolves the confusion.

Core Idea
The example is based on a confusion between general plural and singular comprehensiveness.

Sanusi now turns to a specific example the opponent uses as evidence: the statement 'لا رجال في الدار' (no men are in the house) claimed to be true when only one or two men are present. He shows this is a corrupt inference. The truth of the example is due to a different principle: the comprehensiveness of the singular (istighraq al-mufrad) covers the one and above, while the general plural (al-jam' al-'amm) only covers individuals of the plural, not the singular or dual. Therefore, the example does not support the opponent's claim about negation of generality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Follow the author's argument carefully. Pay attention to the technical terms: استغراق المفرد, الجمع العام.
This passage uses a reductio ad absurdum in the latter part.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims that 'لا رجال في الدار' is true when one or two men are inside, using this as evidence for his theory of negation generality.
The opponent's theory: negation entering upon the indefinite plural indicates negation of generality (سلب العموم).
3
Understand Sanusi's explanation
Sanusi explains the truth of the example by contrasting استغراق المفرد and الجمع العام. استغراق المفرد includes the singular and above, while الجمع العام excludes singular and dual. So the presence of one or two men is compatible with the negation because the plural doesn't cover them.
This shows the example does not rely on the negation of generality.
4
See the absurd consequence
Sanusi shows a reductio: if the opponent's view were correct, then 'لا رجال في الدار' would be true even if thousands are present but one is absent, because it becomes a particular negative. This is clearly false.
This strengthens the refutation by showing the opponent's position leads to an unacceptable result.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's argument concerning the scope of negation with general terms. The author first attacks the analogy (qiyas) that derives the negation of generality with 'al' from the negation of generality with 'kull', pointing out a clear difference: 'al' can be used for the reality (al-haqiqa) rather than comprehensiveness (al-istighraq). He then cites Quranic examples where 'kull' is used with negative generality. The author explains that the truth of this example is based on a confusion: the comprehensiveness of the singular (istighraq al-mufrad) covers the one and above, while the general plural (al-jam' al-'amm) covers only the individuals of that plural, not the singular or dual. Therefore, the exclusion of singular/dual is like excluding a woman from the generality of men—a structural issue, not a negation of generality. The author then shows the absurd consequence: according to the opponent's view, 'no men are in the house' would be true even if all the world's men are inside except one, because it would be a particular negative equivalent to 'some men are not in the house'. This is a clear reductio ad absurdum. The author then refutes three more objections: (1) the claim that the verse's context of praise does not require generality; the author replies that ignoring generality here disturbs eloquence. (2) The objection that the contextual clue (qarina haliyya) is circumstantial and does not override the literal meaning; the author notes that this implies restricting the clue of metaphor to verbal clues only, which is false according to the verifiers. (3) The opponent's concession that the form indicates generality but not eternally; the author dismisses this as a misplaced argument that leads to the opponent's defeat, showing he does not even know whom he is addressing.

Key Terms
استغراق المفرد
الجمع العام
سلْب العموم
قضية جزئية سالبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The example 'لا رجال في الدار' being true with one or two men proves that negation of an indefinite plural indicates negation of generality (salb al-'umum).
Correction: The example's truth is based on the fact that the general plural excludes singular and dual, not on any special effect of negation.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is the statement 'لا رجال في الدار' true when one or two men are present?
A) Because the negation of the plural indicates negation of all individuals
B) Because the general plural does not include the singular or dual in its meaning
C) Because the Arabic language does not allow the plural to cover singular cases
D) Because the context of the statement limits its meaning

Key Takeaway
The example 'لا رجال في الدار' is based on the scope of the plural, not on a special rule of negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When has pride in being right prevented you from accepting correction? How can you train yourself to examine your own reasoning as rigorously as Sanusi examines the opponent's?
?
Sanusi calls the opponent's error 'a great heedlessness' that even young students would reject. Are you willing to have your foundational assumptions challenged, or do you avoid such scrutiny?
?
The opponent here mistook a structural exclusion (plural not covering singular) for a special rule of negation. In your spiritual life, are there places where you have misinterpreted a general rule, making it overly narrow or overly broad, to fit your desires? Commit to revisiting one such area this week with an open mind.
