Refutation of Remaining Objections
Sanusi refutes three objections about context, clue, and admission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes three further objections about context of praise, circumstantial clues, and the admission of generality.

Core Idea
Context of praise and circumstantial clues can override literal generality.

After the main refutation, Sanusi addresses three additional objections raised by the opponent. The first concerns whether the context of praise in a verse requires interpreting it contrary to generality. The second argues that circumstantial clues (qarina haliyya) should not override literal meaning. The third is a concession that the form indicates generality but the opponent still disputes. Sanusi responds to each, showing the opponent's inconsistencies and lack of understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
This passage contains three objections and Sanusi's short replies. Identify each objection and its refutation.
The responses are brief but decisive.
2
First objection: context of praise
The opponent questions whether the context of praise requires generality. Sanusi replies that ignoring generality would impair eloquence.
This is a standard argument in usul: madh (praise) often implies comprehensiveness.
3
Second objection: circumstantial clue
The opponent claims that a circumstantial clue (qarina haliyya) should not override literal meaning. Sanusi retorts that this implies only verbal clues can indicate metaphor, which is false according to the verifiers.
This shows the opponent's narrow view of qara'in.
4
Third objection: concession on form
The opponent concedes that the form indicates generality but still argues. Sanusi says this admission defeats him, and his subsequent remarks are misplaced.
This highlights the opponent's inconsistency.

وهي غفلةٌ عظيمة لا يرضى بمقالتها أصاغرُ صبيانِ الكُتَابِ ; إذْ يلزمُهُ على هذا صدْقُ النفْيِ في قولنا : ( لا رجال في الدارِ ) وإِنْ وُجِدَ فيها آلاف الآلافِ مِنَ الرجالِ عندَ غَيبةِ رجل واحدٍ منها ; لأنَّ القضيَّةَ عنده جزئية سالبة ، فهي في قوَّةِ قولنا : بعضُ الرجال ليس في الدارِ ، فيصدقُ بغَيبةِ رجل واحدٍ مِنَ الدارِ أَنَّ بعض الرجالِ ـ ونريدُ : ذلكَ الرجل الواحد - لم يكن في الدار ، فتصدقُ إذا القضية وإن وُجِدَ في الدارِ رجالُ الدنيا كلُّهم سوى ذلكَ الرجل ! وقوله : ( نقولُ : هَبْ أَنَّهُ لا ينافيه ، فأينَ ما يقتضيه ؟! ) نقول : يقتضيهِ ما ذُكِرَ مِنْ أنَّ الآيةَ وردَتْ في مَعرِضِ المدحِ فحملها على خلافِ العمومِ يُخِلُّ ببلاغةِ الكلامِ . وقوله : ( إِنَّ تلك القرينة حاليَّةٌ ، فلا يُترَكُ مدلول اللفظ لأجلها ) هذا الكلامُ يقتضي انحصار قرينة المجاز في القرائن اللفظية وفساده ظاهرُ عندَ المحققينَ وإِنْ كانَ فيهِ خلافٌ وقوله : ( سلَّمنا دلالة الصيغة على العموم بهذه القرينة ) . هذا تسليم يُوجِبُ انقطاعَهُ ، وما ذكر بعده ... إلى آخرِهِ (۱) : كلام منصوبٌ في غير محله ، وكأنَّهُ لم يعرفُ مَنْ يخاطِبُ ! هذا
And deriving the negation of generality in the attachment of negation to (al) from the negation of generality in its attachment to (kull) is an analogy in language despite the clear difference; because of the possibility of using (al) with a generality tool for the reality (al-haqiqa) not for comprehensiveness (al-istighraq), and indeed negative generality has been used with (kull) frequently as well, including: {And Allah does not love every proud boaster} [al-Hadid: 23], {And Allah does not love every ungrateful sinner} [al-Baqara: 276], {And do not obey every worthless swearer} [al-Qalam: 10]. And his statement: (As evidence for the truth of our saying: 'No men are in the house' if there is a man or two men in it) is a corrupt inference; because the truth of this example with the existence of a man or two men is only based on the fact that the comprehensiveness of the singular is more inclusive in that it includes the one and above, while the general plural only includes the individuals of the plurals that were suitable for it before generality, not the singular and dual; because they are not part of the meaning of the plural. So their exclusion, according to those who hold this, is like the exclusion of a woman from the generality of men, for example, and vice versa, not for what this objector understood against Ibn al-Tilmisani that their exclusion is because the negation entering upon the indefinite plural is for the negation of generality! And it is a great heedlessness that even the youngest students of the writing schools would not accept its statement; because it would necessitate for him the truth of the negation in our saying: (No men are in the house) even if there are thousands upon thousands of men in it when a single man is absent from it; because the proposition according to him is a particular negative, so it is equivalent to our saying: Some men are not in the house, so it is true by the absence of one man from the house that some men—and we mean: that single man—was not in the house, so the proposition would be true even if all the men of the world are in the house except that man! And his statement: (We say: Grant that it does not contradict it, but where is what it requires?) We say: What it requires is what was mentioned, that the verse came in the context of praise, so interpreting it contrary to generality would impair the eloquence of the speech. And his statement: (Indeed that contextual clue (al-qarina al-haliyya) is a circumstantial one, so the literal meaning of the wording is not abandoned for its sake) This statement implies the restriction of the clue of metaphor to verbal clues, and its corruption is evident to the verifiers (al-muhaqqiqin), even if there is disagreement. And his statement: (We grant the indication of the form to generality by this clue). This is an admission that necessitates his defeat, and what he mentioned afterward... to its end: speech set up in its wrong place, as if he does not know whom he is addressing! This.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's argument concerning the scope of negation with general terms. The author first attacks the analogy (qiyas) that derives the negation of generality with 'al' from the negation of generality with 'kull', pointing out a clear difference: 'al' can be used for the reality (al-haqiqa) rather than comprehensiveness (al-istighraq). He then cites Quranic examples where 'kull' is used with negative generality. Next, he addresses the opponent's example 'la rijala fi al-dar' (no men are in the house) claimed to be true if one or two men are present. The author explains that the truth of this example is based on a confusion: the comprehensiveness of the singular (istighraq al-mufrad) covers the one and above, while the general plural (al-jam' al-'amm) covers only the individuals of that plural, not the singular or dual. Therefore, the exclusion of singular/dual is like excluding a woman from the generality of men—a structural issue, not a negation of generality. The author then shows the absurd consequence: according to the opponent's view, 'no men are in the house' would be true even if all the world's men are inside except one, because it would be a particular negative equivalent to 'some men are not in the house'. This is a clear reductio ad absurdum. The author then refutes three more objections: (1) the claim that the verse's context of praise does not require generality; the author replies that ignoring generality here disturbs eloquence. (2) The objection that the contextual clue (qarina haliyya) is circumstantial and does not override the literal meaning; the author notes that this implies restricting the clue of metaphor to verbal clues only, which is false according to the verifiers. (3) The opponent's concession that the form indicates generality but not eternally; the author dismisses this as a misplaced argument that leads to the opponent's defeat, showing he does not even know whom he is addressing.

قرينة المجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only verbal clues (qara'in lafziyya) can indicate metaphor and override literal meaning.
Correction: Circumstantial clues (qara'in haliyya) also validly indicate metaphor, as affirmed by verifiers.

Checkpoint

A) He says the objection is irrelevant
B) He states that only verbal clues can indicate metaphor
C) He argues that this implies restricting the clue of metaphor to verbal clues, which is false
D) He agrees and uses another argument

Key Takeaway
Context of praise and circumstantial clues are valid reasons to interpret beyond literal generality.

The Absurdity of Treating Universal Negatives as Particular
Sanusi exposes the absurd consequence of treating a universal negative as a particular negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why treating a universal negative statement as a particular negative leads to absurd consequences in logical reasoning.

Core Idea
A universal negative cannot be reduced to a particular without destroying its meaning.

The author begins by exposing a critical logical mistake: assuming that a general negation like 'There are no men in the house' is actually a particular negative. This error, if accepted, would render the statement true even when almost all men are present. Understanding this fallacy is essential for proper interpretation of Qur'anic verses that involve negation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the example given by Sanusi.
2
Identify the logical error
Notice that the opponent treats 'لا رجال في الدار' as a particular negative (جزئية سالبة), meaning 'some men are not in the house'. The author shows this leads to an absurdity.
The absurdity is that the statement would be true even if all men except one are present.
3
Reflect on the principle
Consider why universal negation cannot be reduced to a particular negation in logical and linguistic contexts. This principle applies to interpreting Qur'anic verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي غفلةٌ عظيمة لا يرضى بمقالتها أصاغرُ صبيانِ الكُتَابِ ؛ إذْ يلزمُهُ على هذا صدْقُ النفْيِ في قولنا : ( لا رجال في الدارِ ) وإِنْ وُجِدَ فيها آلاف الآلافِ مِنَ الرجالِ عندَ غَيبةِ رجل واحدٍ منها ؛ لأنَّ القضيَّةَ عنده جزئية سالبة ، فهي في قوَّةِ قولنا : بعضُ الرجال ليس في الدارِ ، فيصدقُ بغَيبةِ رجل واحدٍ مِنَ الدارِ أَنَّ بعض الرجالِ ـ ونريدُ : ذلكَ الرجل الواحد - لم يكن في الدار ، فتصدقُ إذا القضية وإن وُجِدَ في الدارِ رجالُ الدنيا كلُّهم سوى ذلكَ الرجل ! وقوله : ( نقولُ : هَبْ أَنَّهُ لا ينافيه ، فأينَ ما يقتضيه ؟! ) نقول : يقتضيهِ ما ذُكِرَ مِنْ أنَّ الآيةَ وردَتْ في مَعرِضِ المدحِ فحملها على خلافِ العمومِ يُخِلُّ ببلاغةِ الكلامِ . وقوله : ( إِنَّ تلك القرينة حاليَّةٌ ، فلا يُترَكُ مدلول اللفظ لأجلها ) هذا الكلامُ يقتضي انحصار قرينة المجاز في القرائن اللفظية وفساده ظاهرُ عندَ المحققينَ وإِنْ كانَ فيهِ خلافٌ وقوله : ( سلَّمنا دلالة الصيغة على العموم بهذه القرينة ) . هذا تسليم يُوجِبُ انقطاعَهُ ، وما ذكر بعده ... إلى آخرِهِ : كلام منصوبٌ في غير محله ، وكأنَّهُ لم يعرفُ مَنْ يخاطِبُ ! هذا المخاطب له هو الإمامُ شرفُ الدين بنُ التِّلِمْسَانِي مِنْ أَئمة أهل السنَّةِ ومحققيهم رحمهُ اللهُ تعالى ورضي عنهُ ! وقد قدحَ في بعض الأجوبة عنِ الآية التي استدلَّ بها المعتزلة ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ ذلكَ أَنَّهُ يوافقهم على صحة الاستدلال بها على نفي الرؤية ، كيف وهو قد صرَّحَ بما ارتضاه مِنَ الأجوبة عنها ؛ كحمل الإدراك على ما هو أخص مِنَ الرؤية ، ونحوه ؟! فشأْنُ المُتكلّم معَهُ أنْ يحاول تصحيح الجوابِ الذي اعترضَهُ إِنْ قَدَرَ ، لا أنْ يُسلَّمَ لَهُ ذلك الاعتراض ، ثم يقول : عندنا أجوبة أخرى غيرُ ما اعترضْتَ تُوجِبُ صرْفَ الآية عن الاستدلال بها على نفي الرؤية ؛ لأنَّ ابنَ التِّلِمْساني يوافق على ذلك ، بل صرَّحَ بهِ .
It is a great heedlessness, the small boys of the elementary schools would not be satisfied with its statement; for it would necessitate the truth of the negation in our saying: 'There are no men in the house' even if there were in it thousands of thousands of men when one man is absent from it, because the proposition in his view is a particular negative, so it is equivalent to our saying: 'Some men are not in the house', so it is true by the absence of one man from the house that some men—and we mean that one man—was not in the house. So the proposition would be true even if all the men of the world except that man were in the house! And his saying: 'We say: Grant that it does not contradict it, so where is what requires it?' We say: What requires it is what was mentioned, that the verse came in the context of praise, so interpreting it contrary to generality impairs the eloquence of the speech. And his saying: 'Indeed that contextual indication (qarīna) is a circumstantial one (ḥāliyya), so the literal meaning of the word is not abandoned because of it.' This statement necessitates restricting the indication of metaphor (qarīnat al-majāz) to verbal indications, and its corruption is apparent to the investigators even though there is disagreement about it. And his saying: 'We grant the indication of the form for generality by this contextual indication.' This is a concession that necessitates his defeat, and what he mentioned afterwards... to the end of it: speech set up in an improper place, as if he did not know whom he is addressing! This addressee is the Imām Sharaf al-Dīn Ibn al-Tilimsānī, one of the imams of the Ahl al-Sunna and their investigators, may Allāh have mercy on him and be pleased with him! He criticized some of the answers to the verse that the Mu‘tazila used as evidence, but it does not necessarily follow from that that he agrees with them on the validity of using it as evidence for negating the vision (ru’ya), how could it be when he explicitly stated what he approved of among the answers to it, such as interpreting 'idrāk' (comprehension) as something more specific than vision, and the like? So the duty of the speaker with him is to try to correct the answer that he objected to if he can, not to concede to him that objection, then say: We have other answers besides what you objected to that necessitate turning the verse away from being used as evidence for negating vision, because Ibn al-Tilimsānī agrees with that, indeed he explicitly stated it.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's arguments about negative propositions and the context of a Qur'anic verse. The author first attacks the opponent's handling of the negative judgment in 'There are no men in the house' (lā rijāl fī al-dār). The opponent treats it as a particular negative (qadiyya juz'iyya sāliba), meaning 'some men are not in the house'. The author shows the absurd consequence: even if all the men in the world except one were present, the statement would be true because that one man is absent. This makes the negative proposition trivial and false to its intended meaning. Next, the opponent asks: 'Grant that it does not contradict it, so where is what requires it?' The author answers that the verse came in a context of praise (ma‘riḍ al-madḥ), so interpreting it contrary to generality undermines the eloquence (balāgha) of the speech. Then the opponent claims that the contextual indication (qarīna) is circumstantial (ḥāliyya) and therefore the literal meaning should not be abandoned. The author responds that this would restrict the indication of metaphor (qarīnat al-majāz) to only verbal indications, which is a corrupt view. Finally, the opponent concedes the indication of generality but then argues something else. The author dismisses this as a speech set in the wrong place, as if the opponent does not know whom he is addressing. He identifies the opponent as Imām Sharaf al-Dīn Ibn al-Tilimsānī, a respected Ash‘ari scholar. The author clarifies that Ibn al-Tilimsānī indeed criticized some answers to the verse used by the Mu‘tazila but this does not mean he agreed with them; he explicitly approved of other answers, such as interpreting 'idrāk' as more specific than vision. Therefore, the correct approach is to try to correct the answer that Ibn al-Tilimsānī objected to, not to concede and then claim other answers exist, because Ibn al-Tilimsānī himself agrees that the verse cannot be used to negate vision.

Key Terms
قضية سالبة
عموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a negative statement like 'لا رجال في الدار' is always a particular negative (جزئية سالبة) and not a universal negative.
Correction: The author shows that treating it as particular leads to absurdity; therefore, in its normal usage, it is a universal negative (عموم سالب).

Checkpoint
What absurd consequence does the author show follows from treating the statement 'لا رجال في الدار' as a particular negative?
A) It would mean that no men exist in the house at all.
B) It would be true even if all men except one are present in the house.
C) It would force the interpretation of the verse as a denial of vision.
D) It would require abandoning the literal meaning for a metaphorical one.

Key Takeaway
A universal negative statement cannot be understood as a particular negative without leading to logical absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Will you commit today to studying the logic of aqeedah so that your faith is not built on contradictions?

Context of Praise Requires General Interpretation
Sanusi argues that context of praise requires general interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the context of praise (ma‘riḍ al-madḥ) necessitates interpreting a Qur'anic verse as general, and why deviating from that impairs eloquence.

Core Idea
Context of praise demands general interpretation; otherwise, eloquence is lost.

The opponent asks: 'Grant that it does not contradict it, so where is what requires it?' The author responds that the verse came in a context of praise, which requires general interpretation. This is a key principle in Islamic theology and rhetoric: when a statement is made in praise, interpreting it as specific weakens the intended meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the short Arabic passage and its translation to understand the exchange.
2
Understand the context of praise
The author argues that because the verse is in a context of praise (مدح), it should be interpreted generally. Note how this principle is used in Qur'anic exegesis.
3
Link to balāgha
Consider why interpreting against generality would impair eloquence (بلاغة). This connects to the science of rhetoric.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي غفلةٌ عظيمة لا يرضى بمقالتها أصاغرُ صبيانِ الكُتَابِ ؛ إذْ يلزمُهُ على هذا صدْقُ النفْيِ في قولنا : ( لا رجال في الدارِ ) وإِنْ وُجِدَ فيها آلاف الآلافِ مِنَ الرجالِ عندَ غَيبةِ رجل واحدٍ منها ؛ لأنَّ القضيَّةَ عنده جزئية سالبة ، فهي في قوَّةِ قولنا : بعضُ الرجال ليس في الدارِ ، فيصدقُ بغَيبةِ رجل واحدٍ مِنَ الدارِ أَنَّ بعض الرجالِ ـ ونريدُ : ذلكَ الرجل الواحد - لم يكن في الدار ، فتصدقُ إذا القضية وإن وُجِدَ في الدارِ رجالُ الدنيا كلُّهم سوى ذلكَ الرجل ! وقوله : ( نقولُ : هَبْ أَنَّهُ لا ينافيه ، فأينَ ما يقتضيه ؟! ) نقول : يقتضيهِ ما ذُكِرَ مِنْ أنَّ الآيةَ وردَتْ في مَعرِضِ المدحِ فحملها على خلافِ العمومِ يُخِلُّ ببلاغةِ الكلامِ . وقوله : ( إِنَّ تلك القرينة حاليَّةٌ ، فلا يُترَكُ مدلول اللفظ لأجلها ) هذا الكلامُ يقتضي انحصار قرينة المجاز في القرائن اللفظية وفساده ظاهرُ عندَ المحققينَ وإِنْ كانَ فيهِ خلافٌ وقوله : ( سلَّمنا دلالة الصيغة على العموم بهذه القرينة ) . هذا تسليم يُوجِبُ انقطاعَهُ ، وما ذكر بعده ... إلى آخرِهِ : كلام منصوبٌ في غير محله ، وكأنَّهُ لم يعرفُ مَنْ يخاطِبُ ! هذا المخاطب له هو الإمامُ شرفُ الدين بنُ التِّلِمْسَانِي مِنْ أَئمة أهل السنَّةِ ومحققيهم رحمهُ اللهُ تعالى ورضي عنهُ ! وقد قدحَ في بعض الأجوبة عنِ الآية التي استدلَّ بها المعتزلة ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ ذلكَ أَنَّهُ يوافقهم على صحة الاستدلال بها على نفي الرؤية ، كيف وهو قد صرَّحَ بما ارتضاه مِنَ الأجوبة عنها ؛ كحمل الإدراك على ما هو أخص مِنَ الرؤية ، ونحوه ؟! فشأْنُ المُتكلّم معَهُ أنْ يحاول تصحيح الجوابِ الذي اعترضَهُ إِنْ قَدَرَ ، لا أنْ يُسلَّمَ لَهُ ذلك الاعتراض ، ثم يقول : عندنا أجوبة أخرى غيرُ ما اعترضْتَ تُوجِبُ صرْفَ الآية عن الاستدلال بها على نفي الرؤية ؛ لأنَّ ابنَ التِّلِمْساني يوافق على ذلك ، بل صرَّحَ بهِ .
It is a great heedlessness, the small boys of the elementary schools would not be satisfied with its statement; for it would necessitate the truth of the negation in our saying: 'There are no men in the house' even if there were in it thousands of thousands of men when one man is absent from it, because the proposition in his view is a particular negative, so it is equivalent to our saying: 'Some men are not in the house', so it is true by the absence of one man from the house that some men—and we mean that one man—was not in the house. So the proposition would be true even if all the men of the world except that man were in the house! And his saying: 'We say: Grant that it does not contradict it, so where is what requires it?' We say: What requires it is what was mentioned, that the verse came in the context of praise, so interpreting it contrary to generality impairs the eloquence of the speech. And his saying: 'Indeed that contextual indication (qarīna) is a circumstantial one (ḥāliyya), so the literal meaning of the word is not abandoned because of it.' This statement necessitates restricting the indication of metaphor (qarīnat al-majāz) to verbal indications, and its corruption is apparent to the investigators even though there is disagreement about it. And his saying: 'We grant the indication of the form for generality by this contextual indication.' This is a concession that necessitates his defeat, and what he mentioned afterwards... to the end of it: speech set up in an improper place, as if he did not know whom he is addressing! This addressee is the Imām Sharaf al-Dīn Ibn al-Tilimsānī, one of the imams of the Ahl al-Sunna and their investigators, may Allāh have mercy on him and be pleased with him! He criticized some of the answers to the verse that the Mu‘tazila used as evidence, but it does not necessarily follow from that that he agrees with them on the validity of using it as evidence for negating the vision (ru’ya), how could it be when he explicitly stated what he approved of among the answers to it, such as interpreting 'idrāk' (comprehension) as something more specific than vision, and the like? So the duty of the speaker with him is to try to correct the answer that he objected to if he can, not to concede to him that objection, then say: We have other answers besides what you objected to that necessitate turning the verse away from being used as evidence for negating vision, because Ibn al-Tilimsānī agrees with that, indeed he explicitly stated it.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's arguments about negative propositions and the context of a Qur'anic verse. The author first attacks the opponent's handling of the negative judgment in 'There are no men in the house' (lā rijāl fī al-dār). The opponent treats it as a particular negative (qadiyya juz'iyya sāliba), meaning 'some men are not in the house'. The author shows the absurd consequence: even if all the men in the world except one were present, the statement would be true because that one man is absent. This makes the negative proposition trivial and false to its intended meaning. Next, the opponent asks: 'Grant that it does not contradict it, so where is what requires it?' The author answers that the verse came in a context of praise (ma‘riḍ al-madḥ), so interpreting it contrary to generality undermines the eloquence (balāgha) of the speech. Then the opponent claims that the contextual indication (qarīna) is circumstantial (ḥāliyya) and therefore the literal meaning should not be abandoned. The author responds that this would restrict the indication of metaphor (qarīnat al-majāz) to only verbal indications, which is a corrupt view. Finally, the opponent concedes the indication of generality but then argues something else. The author dismisses this as a speech set in the wrong place, as if the opponent does not know whom he is addressing. He identifies the opponent as Imām Sharaf al-Dīn Ibn al-Tilimsānī, a respected Ash‘ari scholar. The author clarifies that Ibn al-Tilimsānī indeed criticized some answers to the verse used by the Mu‘tazila but this does not mean he agreed with them; he explicitly approved of other answers, such as interpreting 'idrāk' as more specific than vision. Therefore, the correct approach is to try to correct the answer that Ibn al-Tilimsānī objected to, not to concede and then claim other answers exist, because Ibn al-Tilimsānī himself agrees that the verse cannot be used to negate vision.

Key Terms
عموم
بلاغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author say requires the general interpretation of the verse?
A) The literal meaning of the words.
B) The context of praise in which the verse was revealed.
C) The consensus of scholars.
D) The opponent's own concession.

Key Takeaway
The context of praise in a Qur'anic verse demands a general interpretation to preserve eloquence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a Qur'anic verse that could be interpreted generally or specifically, do you ever ask yourself, ‘Which interpretation better preserves the eloquence of Allah’s speech?’ Or do you default to a narrow reading that suits your preconceptions? How will this lesson change that?
?
Consider your own habit of interpreting difficult verses. Do you realise that a context of praise demands a general meaning to fully magnify Allah? If you restrict such a verse, are you not diminishing the praise? What will you do to align your understanding with divine eloquence?
?
If you are quick to say ‘this verse is specific to a situation’ to avoid its general implication, reflect: are you more concerned with protecting your theological certainty or with preserving the comprehensive praise of Allah? What wrenches you away from comfort?

Method of Learning

Circumstantial Indications Can Signal Metaphor
Sanusi refutes the restriction of metaphor indications to verbal only.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why restricting the indication of metaphor (qarīnat al-majāz) to only verbal indications is a flawed view.

Core Idea
Metaphor can be indicated by circumstantial context, not only by verbal cues.

The opponent argues that the contextual indication is circumstantial (ḥāliyya) and therefore cannot override the literal meaning. The author responds that this view would wrongly restrict metaphor indications to verbal ones only, which is a corruption known to investigators. This is an important point in the theory of metaphor in Arabic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see the opponent's claim and the author's response.
2
Understand the two types of indications
The opponent distinguishes between verbal (لفظية) and circumstantial (حالية) indications. The author rejects the restriction.
3
Consider the implication
If only verbal indications could indicate metaphor, many common metaphorical usages based on context would be invalid. This is why the author says the corruption is apparent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That only verbal cues (قرائن لفظية) can indicate that a phrase is metaphorical.
Correction: The author points out that investigators hold that circumstantial indications (قرائن حالية) can also serve as indications of metaphor.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the view that only verbal indications can signal metaphor?
A) It is the correct view.
B) It is a corrupt view according to investigators.
C) It is supported by consensus.
D) It is a view held by Ibn al-Tilimsānī.

Key Takeaway
Metaphorical interpretation can be indicated by circumstantial context, not only by verbal cues.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of the Qur'ān that seems to describe Allāh in human terms, do you automatically take it literally, or do you pause to consider the circumstantial context that might indicate a metaphorical meaning? How often do you let your own assumptions block a deeper understanding?
?
The lesson shows that even scholars disagree about the role of circumstantial indications. Are you willing to hold your interpretations humbly, knowing that your understanding might be limited by ignoring circumstantial cues? What specific area of your faith—such as the attributes of Allāh—might you need to reconsider?
?
Sanusi argues that restricting metaphor to verbal indications is 'corrupt'. Could it be that your own rigidity in interpreting religious texts is a form of corruption of understanding? What would it take for you to open yourself to the full breadth of meaning that Allāh might intend, beyond your привычных patterns?

Proper Scholarly Engagement with Ibn al-Tilimsānī
Sanusi corrects the opponent's misidentification and misguided concession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proper scholarly approach when engaging with the objections of a respected scholar like Ibn al-Tilimsānī.

Core Idea
Conceding an objection to a respected scholar is a mistake; one should try to correct the answer instead.

The opponent makes a concession, which the author says amounts to defeat. He then criticizes the opponent for not knowing whom he is addressing: the great Ash‘ari scholar Ibn al-Tilimsānī. The author clarifies that Ibn al-Tilimsānī's criticisms were directed at certain answers, not at the overall position that the verse cannot be used to negate vision. This lesson teaches the etiquette of scholarly debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the section where the author identifies the addressee and explains his position.
2
Understand the mistake
The opponent conceded the point and then claimed other answers exist. The author says this is misplaced because Ibn al-Tilimsānī himself agreed that the verse does not negate vision.
3
Learn the proper method
The author says the proper method is to try to correct the answer that Ibn al-Tilimsānī objected to, not to concede and then bring up other answers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And his saying: 'We grant the indication of the form for generality by this contextual indication.' This is a concession that necessitates his defeat, and what he mentioned afterwards... to the end of it: speech set up in an improper place, as if he did not know whom he is addressing! This addressee is the Imām Sharaf al-Dīn Ibn al-Tilimsānī, one of the imams of the Ahl al-Sunna and their investigators, may Allāh have mercy on him and be pleased with him! He criticized some of the answers to the verse that the Mu‘tazila used as evidence, but it does not necessarily follow from that that he agrees with them on the validity of using it as evidence for negating the vision (ru’ya), how could it be when he explicitly stated what he approved of among the answers to it, such as interpreting 'idrāk' (comprehension) as something more specific than vision, and the like? So the duty of the speaker with him is to try to correct the answer that he objected to if he can, not to concede to him that objection, then say: We have other answers besides what you objected to that necessitate turning the verse away from being used as evidence for negating vision, because Ibn al-Tilimsānī agrees with that, indeed he explicitly stated it.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's arguments about negative propositions and the context of a Qur'anic verse. The author first attacks the opponent's handling of the negative judgment in 'There are no men in the house' (lā rijāl fī al-dār). The opponent treats it as a particular negative (qadiyya juz'iyya sāliba), meaning 'some men are not in the house'. The author shows the absurd consequence: even if all the men in the world except one were present, the statement would be true because that one man is absent. This makes the negative proposition trivial and false to its intended meaning. Next, the opponent asks: 'Grant that it does not contradict it, so where is what requires it?' The author answers that the verse came in a context of praise (ma‘riḍ al-madḥ), so interpreting it contrary to generality undermines the eloquence (balāgha) of the speech. Then the opponent claims that the contextual indication (qarīna) is circumstantial (ḥāliyya) and therefore the literal meaning should not be abandoned. The author responds that this would restrict the indication of metaphor (qarīnat al-majāz) to only verbal indications, which is a corrupt view. Finally, the opponent concedes the indication of generality but then argues something else. The author dismisses this as a speech set in the wrong place, as if the opponent does not know whom he is addressing. He identifies the opponent as Imām Sharaf al-Dīn Ibn al-Tilimsānī, a respected Ash‘ari scholar. The author clarifies that Ibn al-Tilimsānī indeed criticized some answers to the verse used by the Mu‘tazila but this does not mean he agreed with them; he explicitly approved of other answers, such as interpreting 'idrāk' as more specific than vision. Therefore, the correct approach is to try to correct the answer that Ibn al-Tilimsānī objected to, not to concede and then claim other answers exist, because Ibn al-Tilimsānī himself agrees that the verse cannot be used to negate vision.

Key Terms
رؤية
إدراك
التلمساني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ibn al-Tilimsānī's criticism of certain answers implies he agrees with the Mu‘tazila's use of the verse to negate vision.
Correction: The author clarifies that Ibn al-Tilimsānī explicitly approved of other answers and agreed that the verse cannot be used to negate vision.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the proper scholarly response to Ibn al-Tilimsānī's objection?
A) Concede the objection and offer other answers.
B) Try to correct the answer that he objected to if possible.
C) Ignore his objection and present new evidence.
D) Accept that the verse negates vision.

Key Takeaway
When engaging with a scholar's objection, the proper method is to defend or correct the answer, not concede and pivot to other arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are corrected by someone you respect, is your first instinct to concede or to try to understand and improve your answer? How can you train yourself to follow the correct method taught here?
?
Are you quick to offer alternative arguments when challenged, instead of defending or correcting your original point? What does this reveal about your trust in your own evidence?
?
Reflect on a time you conceded an objection too easily. Could you have tried to correct your answer instead? What would that have required of you?

Responding to Ibn al-Tilmisani's Objections
Sanusi explains proper response methodology to Ibn al-Tilmisani's objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to properly address objections from fellow Sunni scholars like Ibn al-Tilmisani, distinguishing between debates with Sunnis and Mu'tazilites.

Core Idea
Don't concede to a fellow Sunni; instead, try to defend the answer.

Sanusi provides a methodological note on handling criticism from a respected Sunni scholar. Ibn al-Tilmisani's objections to some Sunni answers do not mean he agrees with the Mu'tazilites; he offered his own solutions. The correct approach is to attempt to correct the answer if possible, not to immediately switch to another answer. However, when debating a Mu'tazilite, switching answers is permissible because their goal is to defend their false doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the contrast between handling a Sunni scholar and a Mu'tazilite.
2
Identify the two contexts
Sanusi describes two scenarios: speaking with Ibn al-Tilmisani (a Sunni) and speaking with a Mu'tazilite. Note how the response strategy differs.
Ibn al-Tilmisani already agrees that the verse does not support the Mu'tazilite position.
3
Understand the principle
With a fellow Sunni who objects to one answer, try to correct that answer. Conceding and jumping to another answer is not required because he shares your overall stance.
4
Apply to a Mu'tazilite debate
When debating a Mu'tazilite, you can move from one answer to another if they object, because their objection aims to uphold their false doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المخاطب له هو الإمامُ شرفُ الدين بنُ التِّلِمْسانِي مِنْ أَئمة أهل السنَّةِ ومحققيهم رحمهُ اللهُ تعالى ورضي عنهُ ! وقد قدحَ في بعض الأجوبة عنِ الآية التي استدلَّ بها المعتزلة ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ ذلكَ أَنَّهُ يوافقهم على صحة الاستدلال بها على نفي الرؤية ، كيف وهو قد صرَّحَ بما ارتضاه مِنَ الأجوبة عنها ؛ كحمل الإدراك على ما هو أخص مِنَ الرؤية ، ونحوه ؟! فشأْنُ المُتكلّم معَهُ أنْ يحاول تصحيح الجوابِ الذي اعترضَهُ إِنْ قَدَرَ ، لا أنْ يُسلَّمَ لَهُ ذلك الاعتراض ، ثم يقول : عندنا أجوبة أخرى غيرُ ما اعترضْتَ تُوجِبُ صرْفَ الآية عن الاستدلال بها على نفي الرؤية ؛ لأنَّ ابنَ التِّلِمْساني يوافق على ذلك ، بل صرَّحَ بهِ . نعم ؛ لو كانَ الكلامُ معَ المعتزليّ المستدل بالآية على مذهبِهِ الفاسد .. لحَسُنَ أنْ ينتقل معَهُ مِنْ جواب يعترضُهُ إلى جواب آخرَ يَسلَمُ مِنَ الاعتراض ؛ لأنَّ مقصوده هو بالاعتراض تصحيحُ مذهبه والدفعُ عنه ، لا خصوصيَّةُ ذلك الجواب الذي يعترضُهُ
The addressee is the imam Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilimsani, one of the imams of Ahl al-Sunna and their verifiers, may Allah have mercy on him and be pleased with him! He objected to some of the answers regarding the verse that the Mu'tazilites used as evidence. It does not necessarily follow from that that he agrees with them on the validity of using it as evidence to negate [the possibility of] seeing [God]. How could that be, when he explicitly stated the answers he approved of, such as interpreting 'idrak' as something more specific than 'ru'ya', and so on? So the duty of the dialectician with him is to try to correct the answer he objected to, if possible, not to concede his objection, then say: we have other answers besides the one you objected to that necessitate turning the verse away from being used as evidence for negating vision, because Ibn al-Tilimsani agrees on that, nay, he explicitly stated it. Indeed, if one were speaking with a Mu'tazilite who uses the verse as evidence for his corrupt doctrine, then it would be good to move with him from an answer he objects to to another answer that is free of objection, because his aim through objection is to validate his doctrine and defend it, not the specificity of that particular answer he objects to.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage is a methodological remark about how to handle an objection from a fellow Sunni scholar like Ibn al-Tilimsani. It emphasizes that Ibn al-Tilimsani's criticism of some answers does not mean he agrees with the Mu'tazilite position; rather, he himself gave better answers. The correct approach is to try to defend the answer if possible, not to concede and jump to another answer, because Ibn al-Tilimsani already agrees that the verse does not support the Mu'tazilites. However, when debating a Mu'tazilite, it is permissible to switch answers because his goal is to defend his false doctrine.

معتزلة

إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a Sunni scholar objects to a particular Sunni answer to a Mu'tazilite argument, that means he agrees with the Mu'tazilite position.
Correction: The passage states that Ibn al-Tilimsani's objection to some answers does not mean he agrees with the Mu'tazilites; he himself provided better answers and agreed that the verse does not support their claim.

Checkpoint
What is the proper response when a Sunni scholar like Ibn al-Tilmisani objects to a particular answer to the Mu'tazilite argument?
A) Concede the objection and present a different answer.
B) Try to correct the answer if possible, since he agrees with the overall position.
C) Ignore his objection.
D) Agree with the Mu'tazilites.

Key Takeaway
When a fellow Sunni scholar objects to a specific answer, the best approach is to attempt to correct that answer, not to concede and switch to another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever abandoned a correct belief or understanding simply because someone you respected objected? What might have happened if you had tried to defend it with humility, as Sanusi advises? Will you commit to re-examining that issue with the intention of seeking the truth, not just avoiding conflict?
?
When a fellow believer raises an objection to your understanding of a religious matter, do you immediately assume they are right and give up your position, or do you first consider their point and try to respond? What does that say about your confidence in what you have learned? How can you practice the balance of defending truth while respecting others?
?
Sanusi distinguishes between a Sunni and a Mu'tazilite opponent. In your own discussions about Islam, do you treat every disagreement the same way? Could this lesson change how you discern whether your interlocutor is sincerely seeking truth or defending a preconceived error? How will that affect your approach starting today?

Ibn al-Tilmisani's Introduction to the Objections
Ibn al-Tilmisani introduces the additional objections and their context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly context and honest admission of difficulties in the proof for ru'ya, and how Ibn al-Tilmisani approaches the objections.

Core Idea
Scholarly integrity means acknowledging when arguments face unanswered objections.

Ibn al-Tilmisani sets the stage for a series of objections to the proof for the vision of Allah. He reveals that the original author of the proof admitted he could not answer some of these objections, showing that even great scholars acknowledge difficulties. This lesson introduces the context and the spirit of scholarly humility that underlies the discussion to come.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice who is speaking and what each person says.
Pay attention to the chain: Imam → Ibn al-Tilmisani → Taqi al-Din.
2
Identify the key figures
Identify the Imam (al-Ghazali), Ibn al-Tilmisani, and Taqi al-Din. Understand their roles.
3
Understand the admission of difficulty
Notice that the author of al-Arba'in admitted he could not answer the objections. Why is this significant?
This shows intellectual honesty in the Islamic scholarly tradition.
4
See the plan
Ibn al-Tilmisani says he will briefly indicate the objections and evaluate them. This gives the structure for the upcoming lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اقتصر الإمامُ في المعالم ) على هذين النقضينِ ، قالَ ابنُ التلمساني : ( وقد أورد عليها في ( الأربعينَ » وغيره أسئلة عديدة ، وأكَّد ورودَها بقولِهِ : « وأنا غير قادر على الجواب عنها ، فمَنْ قَدَرَ على الجواب عنها أمكنه أنْ يتمسَّكَ بهذه الطريقة » (٤) ، وقد تصدى جماعةٌ مِنَ الفضلاء للجواب عنها ، وكان شيخُنا تقي الدين يقولُ : « إِنَّ بعضَها لا يمكنُ الجواب عنه بما يشفي الغليلَ » ((٥) قالَ ابنُ التَّلِمْسانيّ (٦) : ونحنُ نشيرُ إليها على وجه الاختصار ، ونُنبِّه على القويّ منها والضعيف ، وبالله التوفيق
And the Imam in al-Ma'alim limited himself to these two objections. Ibn al-Tilmisani said: 'He has raised against them in al-Arba'in and elsewhere many questions, and he confirmed their occurrence by his saying: "I am unable to answer them, so whoever is able to answer them can adhere to this method." And a group of virtuous people undertook to answer them. Our master Taqi al-Din used to say: "Some of them cannot be answered in a way that quenches the thirst." Ibn al-Tilmisani said: 'We will indicate them briefly and draw attention to the strong and the weak among them, and with Allah is success.'
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage is an editorial introduction by Ibn al-Tilmisani. He notes that the Imam (likely al-Ghazali) only presented two objections in his work al-Ma'alim, but more objections were collected in al-Arba'in. The author of al-Arba'in admitted he could not answer them, which made the method seem weak. However, some scholars did attempt answers. Ibn al-Tilmisani's own teacher, Taqi al-Din, was skeptical about the possibility of fully answering all. Nonetheless, Ibn al-Tilmisani will summarize them and evaluate their strength. This shows the scholarly caution and intellectual honesty of the tradition.

Key Terms
النقضان
أسئلة عديدة
طريقة
تقي الدين
اختصار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objections were easily resolved or that the scholars who raised them could not answer them due to lack of skill.
Correction: The passage shows that even the original author of al-Arba'in, who raised the objections, admitted he could not answer them, and Ibn al-Tilmisani's teacher Taqi al-Din also acknowledged some objections as unanswerable. This indicates that the difficulty is inherent and not due to incompetence.

Checkpoint
What did the author of al-Arba'in say about the objections?
A) He answered them all.
B) He said he was unable to answer them.
C) He attributed them to al-Ash'ari.
D) He said they were all weak.

Key Takeaway
The scholarly tradition values honesty: admitting when objections cannot be answered is part of intellectual integrity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a question about your faith that you cannot answer, do you admit it or try to hide it? What would change if you followed the example of the scholar who said 'I am unable to answer'?
?
Who in your life have you looked up to as a source of knowledge, but realized they might not have all the answers? How does that impact your own pursuit of truth?
?
Are you willing to assess your own beliefs by their strong and weak points, as Ibn al-Tilmisani does with the objections? What is one area of your faith where you need to be more honest about the strength of your conviction?

Refuting the Sabr Argument in Kalam
The commentator refutes an argument based on impossibility of composition in the rational cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why inference by survey (sabr) is insufficient for demonstrative proofs unless its enumeration and refutation are certain.

Core Idea
Inference by survey yields only conjecture, not certainty, in demonstrative arguments.

The commentator now tackles a specific argument that relies on the impossibility of composition in the rational cause. The arguer claims to have investigated and found no evidence, but the commentator points out that such reasoning only produces probability, not certainty. This lesson clarifies the methodological conditions required for sabr to be valid in theological demonstrations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the commentator's refutation.
The passage begins mid-sentence; treat it as a continuous response.
2
Identify the two main criticisms
The commentator makes two main points: first, the arguer's method only yields conjecture; second, the refutation of imkan is flawed. Find where each begins.
The first criticism ends around 'وأين ذلك'.
3
Understand the condition for sabr
The commentator explains that sabr is only valid when the exclusive enumeration is certain and the refutation is certain. Ask yourself why certainty is required here.
Relate this to the distinction between amarat and barahin.
4
Analyze the imkan refutation
The arguer claimed that imkan (possibility) cannot be a cause because not every possible thing can be seen. The commentator corrects: it is the occurrence, not the validity, that is impossible.
Note the distinction between imkan awwal and imkan thani.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باستحالة التركيب في العلَّةِ العقليّةِ
قلتُ : ولا يخفى ضَعفُهُ ؛ فإنَّ قولَ المستدل : ( بحثتُ فلم أجد ) إنَّما يُحصِّلُ الظَّنَّ فقط ، فيصحُ قَبولُهُ في الأماراتِ وما المطلوب منه الظن ، لا في البراهين وما المطلوب منه العلم ؛ كمسألتنا هذهِ وإنَّما يصح الاستدلال بالسبرِ في مثلِ مسألتنا إذا كان الحصر قطعياً ؛ لدورانِهِ بينَ النفْي والإثباتِ ، والإبطال قطعياً ؛ لكونِهِ مِنَ الضرورياتِ أو ما ينتهي إليها ، وأين ذلك ، وما ذكر بعد مبني على هذا الأساس الذي بان انهدامه ؟!(۱) على أنَّ إبطالَهُ عليَّةَ الإمكان منفرداً بعدم صحةِ رؤية كلُّ ممكن .. فاسد ؛ لأنَّا نقول : الممتنع وقوع رؤية كل ممكن ، لا صحته (۲) ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ صحةِ الشيء وقوعه ، والمعلَّلُ بالإمكان الثاني لا الأوَّلِ ، والله تعالى أعلم
With the impossibility of composition in the rational cause. I said: Its weakness is not hidden; for the statement of the arguer: 'I investigated and did not find' only produces conjecture only, so accepting it is valid in signs (amarat) and what is required from it is conjecture, not in proofs (barahin) and what is required from it is knowledge; like this issue of ours. And indeed, inference by survey (sabr) is only valid in such as our issue if the exclusive enumeration (hasr) is certain; because it revolves between negation and affirmation, and the refutation (ibṭāl) is certain; because it is of necessary things or what leads to them. And where is that? And what was mentioned after is built upon this foundation which has become ruined? (1) Moreover, his refutation of the causality (ʿilliyya) of possibility (imkān) alone by the lack of validity of seeing every possible thing is corrupt; because we say: The impossibility is the occurrence of seeing every possible thing, not its validity (2). And the thing's occurrence does not follow from its validity. And what is being caused by possibility is the second (imkān), not the first. And God Almighty knows best.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument that relies on the impossibility of composition in the rational cause. The commentator begins by quoting the arguer's claim that 'I investigated and did not find' — but he points out that this method yields only conjecture (ẓann), not certainty. Such reasoning is acceptable only in matters that require mere probability (amārāt), not in demonstrative proofs (barāhīn) like the present issue, which demand certain knowledge (ʿilm). He then explains that inference by survey (sabr) is only valid if the exclusive enumeration (ḥaṣr) is certain, because it distinguishes between what is negated and what is affirmed. The refutation (ibṭāl) must be certain, derived from necessary truths or what leads to them. He asks rhetorically where such certain enumeration exists, implying that the arguer's foundation is unsound. The commentator further criticizes the arguer's refutation of possibility (imkān) as a cause by claiming that it is not valid that every possible thing be seen. The commentator corrects this: it is impossible that every possible thing actually be seen, but not that it is impossible in itself. Validity (ṣiḥḥa) of something does not necessitate its actual occurrence. The relevant causal capacity (taʿlīl) is by the second kind of possibility (imkān thānī), not the first. The passage ends with acknowledgment that God knows best.

Key Terms
العلة العقلية (al-ʿilla al-ʿaqliyya)
السبر (sabr)
الحصر (ḥasr)
الإبطال (ibṭāl)
الظن (ẓann)
البرهان (burhān)
الإمكان (imkān)
صحة الرؤية (ṣiḥḥat al-ruʾya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inference by survey (sabr) provides certain proof in theological arguments.
Correction: Sabr only yields conjecture; it is valid only if the enumeration and refutation are certain, which is rarely achieved in such matters.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, under what condition is inference by survey (sabr) valid for demonstrative proofs?
A) When the survey is exhaustive and the refutation is certain.
B) When the arguer's investigation is thorough.
C) When the conclusion is based on conjecture.
D) When the possibility is of the first kind.

Key Takeaway
Inference by survey only yields conjecture; for demonstrative proofs, it requires certain enumeration and refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study matters of faith, do you ever rely on a hunch or common opinion rather than seeking certain evidence? Where in your belief about Allah have you accepted something merely because 'it seems right' without rigorous proof? Are you willing to examine your convictions with the same strictness as this commentator examines the arguer's claim?
?
This passage shows that even a well-intentioned investigation only yields conjecture if it lacks certain enumeration. In your own pursuit of truth, have you ever assumed that your personal investigation suffices without checking whether your reasoning is actually demonstrative? What are you willing to abandon if it turns out your foundation is unsound?
?
The arguer thought that because not every possible thing is seen, possibility cannot be a cause. Have you ever rejected a truth because you couldn't observe its every instance? What does that reveal about your trust in Allah's description of Himself? Are you willing to accept what Allah has revealed even when your experience is incomplete?

Negating Purpose in Allah's Rulings
Sanusi applies the negation of purpose to Allah's rulings and addresses an objection to Ibn al-Hajib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the negation of purpose applies to Allah's rulings as well as His actions, and how Sunni jurists use the concept of 'cause' without implying divine motivation.

Core Idea
Allah's rulings are free from external purpose like His actions.

This lesson extends the core principle of tanzih from Allah's acts to His rulings. You will see how Sanusi defends the Sunni position against the Mu'tazila and clarifies a potentially misleading expression by Ibn al-Hajib.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the extension
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how Sanusi states that the same obligation to negate purpose in acts applies to rulings.
The phrase 'كذلك يجب في أحكامه' is key.
2
Understand the distinction of causes
Sanusi explains that when Sunni jurists mention 'causes' of rulings, they mean by sacred designation (ja'l shar'i), not rational causation. Identify this distinction.
Compare with the Mu'tazila position mentioned in the footnote.
3
Analyze the objection to Ibn al-Hajib
Sanusi addresses an objection to Ibn al-Hajib's use of 'motive' (ba'ith). Understand the correction and why the expression is still misleading.
The correct interpretation is that the motive is for the obligated person, not for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكما عرفت وجوب نفي الغرض في أفعالِهِ تعالى كذلك يجب في أحكامه ، وما يذكرُهُ فقهاء أهل السنَّةِ مِنْ علل الأحكامِ إِنَّما هو بالجعْلِ الشرعي ورعيه تفضّل (۱) ، لا بالحكم العقلي وإيجابه الأحكام ، ولهذا اعتُرِضَ على ابن الحاجب قوله في « أصوله » في باب القياس عندما تعرض لذكر شروط العلَّةِ : ( ومنها : أن تكون بمعنى الباعثِ )(۲) ، وتُؤوَّلُ بأنَّ مرادَهُ الباعثةُ للمُكلَّف على الامتثال ، لا الباعثة له تعالى على الحكم ، والحقُّ أنَّها معَ ذلكَ عبارةٌ موهمةٌ ، فيجب تجنبها وكذا ما يُوجَدُ في الكتاب أو السنَّةِ مِنْ أفعال الله تعالى موهماً للتعليل بالأغراض ؛ كقوله تعالى : ﴿ وَمَا خَلَقْتُ الْجِنَّ وَالْإِنسَ إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ ﴾ [الذاريات : ٥٦] ، فإِنَّهُ يجب تأويلُهُ ، فَتُجعَلُ اللام في قوله تعالى : لِيَعْبُدُونِ ( لامَ الصيرورة ، مثلُها في قوله تعالى : ﴿ فَالْتَقَطَهُ وَال فِرْعَوْنَ لِيَكُونَ لَهُمْ عَدُوًّا وَحَزَنًا ﴾ [القصص : ] ، أو هي مِنَ الاستعارة التبعية على ما تقرَّرَ في فنّ البيانِ(۳)
(۱) قوله : ( ورعيه ) بالخفض عطفاً على ( الجعل ) ، وضميره عائد على الشرع المفهوم من ( الشرعي ) ، وكذا قوله الآتي : ( وإيجابه ) عطفاً على ( الحكم ) . أفاده العلامة المنجور في ( حاشيته ) ( ص ٢١٤ ) ، ثم العلة عند فقهائنا شرعية ، لا عقلية كما قال فقهاء المعتزلة
(۲) انظر ( بيان المختصر » ( ٢٥/٣ ) ، وأصل الكلام للآمدي ، وابن الحاجب تبعه . « حامدي » ( ص ٤٢٥ ) ، والتأويل الآتي للإمام تقي الدين السبكي
(۳) وذلك باستعارة اللام التي هي أصالة للتعليل وبيان الغاية .. لأجل بيان الترتب الحاصل من خلق الإنس والجن وحصول المعرفة والعبادة لهم ، وإنما كانت تبعية لأنها وقعت في الحرف ، لا في أسماء الأجناس غير المشتقة
And as you have known the obligation of negating purpose in His acts, exalted, likewise it is obligatory in His rulings. And what the jurists of the Ahl al-Sunnah mention of causes of rulings is only by way of sacred designation and its observance is a favor, not by rational judgment and its necessitating rulings. For this reason, objection was raised against Ibn al-Hajib's statement in his 'Usul' in the chapter on analogy when he discussed the conditions of the cause: 'and among them: that it be in the sense of the motive,' and it is interpreted that he meant the motive for the obligated person to comply, not the motive for Him, exalted, for the ruling. And the truth is that despite that, it is a misleading expression, so it must be avoided. Similarly, what is found in the Book or the Sunnah of Allah's acts that appear to indicate causation by purposes, like His statement: 'And I did not create the jinn and mankind except to worship Me,' then it must be interpreted. So the lam in His statement 'to worship Me' is made the lam of result, like it in His statement: 'So the family of Pharaoh picked him up to become for them an enemy and a sorrow,' or it is from the derivative metaphor as established in the art of eloquence.
(1) His statement: 'and its observance' with kasra as a conjunction to 'the designation,' and its pronoun refers to the law understood from 'sacred,' and likewise his coming statement: 'and its necessitating' is a conjunction to 'the judgment.' This was indicated by the noble al-Manjur in his gloss (p. 214). Then the cause according to our jurists is legislative, not rational as the Mu'tazila jurists said.
(2) See 'Bayan al-Mukhtasar' (3/25), and the origin of the statement is from al-Amidi, and Ibn al-Hajib followed him. 'Hamidi' (p. 425), and the following interpretation is by Imam Taqi al-Din al-Subki.
(3) That is by using the lam metaphorically, which originally indicates causation and statement of purpose, in order to express the resulting order from the creation of humans and jinn and the attainment of knowledge and worship for them. And it was derivative because it occurred in a particle, not in non-derived generic nouns.
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This passage is an application and extension of the previously established principle that Allah's actions are free from external purpose. The author (Sanusi) argues that just as purpose is negated in Allah's acts, it must also be negated in His rulings (ahkam). He clarifies that when Sunni jurists speak of 'causes' (ilal) for rulings, they do not mean rational causes that obligate Allah; rather, they refer to causes by sacred designation (ja'l shar'i) and that Allah's observance of them is a favor (tafaddul), not a rational necessity. This is the Sunni position opposed to the Mu'tazila who hold rational obligation. He then addresses an objection to Ibn al-Hajib's definition of 'cause' as a 'motive' (ba'ith). He explains that Ibn al-Hajib meant a motive for the obligated person to comply, not a motive for Allah to legislate. Even so, the expression is misleading and should be avoided. Finally, Sanusi discusses apparent purposive language in revelation, such as the verse 'I did not create jinn and humans except to worship Me.' He asserts that such verses require interpretation (ta'wil) to avoid attributing purpose to Allah. He gives two possible interpretations: either the lam is the lam of result (lam al-sayrurah), indicating the ultimate outcome rather than purpose, similar to the verse about Pharaoh's family, or it is a derivative metaphor (isti'ara taba'iyya) as explained in rhetoric. The footnotes provide additional grammatical clarifications and source references.

Key Terms
العلة
الباعث
الجعْل الشرعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Sunni jurists believe Allah acts for purposes when they speak of 'causes' for rulings.
Correction: Sunni jurists only use 'cause' by sacred designation and favor, not as rational motive for Allah.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, how do Sunni jurists use the term 'cause' (علة) for rulings?
A) As a rational necessity that obligates Allah
B) By sacred designation and as a favor, not rational
C) As a motive for Allah to legislate
D) As a metaphorical expression without real meaning

Key Takeaway
The negation of purpose in Allah's actions applies equally to His rulings; any mention of causes is by sacred designation, not rational necessity.

Interpreting Purposive Language in the Quran
Sanusi provides two interpretations for the lam in the verse of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to interpret Quranic verses that seem to attribute purpose to Allah, using the examples of lam al-sayrurah and derivative metaphor.

Core Idea
Apparent purposive language in the Quran is interpreted as showing result, not divine motive.

This lesson addresses the second application of the principle: how to handle scriptural passages that seem to indicate Allah acts for a purpose. Sanusi uses the famous verse about creation for worship as an example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the verse
Read the Arabic passage and locate the verse from Surah al-Dhariyat (51:56). Note the problem: the lam seems to indicate purpose.
The phrase 'إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ' appears to suggest a purpose for creation.
2
Understand the first ta'wil: lam al-sayrurah
Sanusi's first interpretation is that the lam is the 'lam of result' indicating the ultimate outcome, not purpose. He gives the example of the Pharaoh family verse.
This interpretation removes the implication of divine purpose.
3
Understand the second ta'wil: derivative metaphor
The second interpretation is that the lam is used metaphorically (isti'ara taba'iyya) as explained in rhetoric. This also avoids attributing purpose.
Metaphorical usage transfers the meaning from purpose to mere consequence.

Checkpoint
What are the two interpretations Sanusi gives for the lam in the verse 'I did not create jinn and humans except to worship Me'?
A) Lam of causation and lam of ownership
B) Lam of result and derivative metaphor
C) Lam of purpose and lam of time
D) Lam of exception and lam of condition

Key Takeaway
Quranic language that seems to indicate divine purpose must be interpreted in ways consistent with Allah's transcendence, such as lam al-sayrurah or derivative metaphor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the verse 'And I did not create the jinn and mankind except to worship Me,' do you feel a sense of pressure that you must fulfill a purpose that God needs from you? After learning that the lam is one of result, not purpose, how does this release you from the burden of thinking you owe God something? Will you now approach worship as a natural consequence of your existence rather than a transaction? Commit to one specific way you will change your intention in worship today.
?
Are you ever guilty of thinking that your prayers, charity, or good deeds add something to God or fulfill a need in Him? How does the second ta'wil—that the lam is a derivative metaphor—help you see that your worship is not for God's benefit but a sign of His creation’s nature? This week, when you begin any act of worship, pause and remind yourself that you are not giving God anything; you are simply fulfilling the consequence of being created. Write down one reminder you can use.
?
Consider the analogy of Pharaoh's family picking up Moses: the result was that he became an enemy, though that was not their intention. In your own life, do you see results of your actions that were not your original intention? How does this parallel help you trust that God's actions are beyond human categories of purpose? What is one area of your life where you can surrender your need to understand God's 'why' and instead accept His transcendence?

Formal Proof of the Prophethood of Muhammad
Sanusi presents the syllogistic proof for Muhammad's prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the prophethood of Muhammad is rationally proven using a syllogism whose minor premise is established by tawātur and major premise by the miracle's indication.

Core Idea
Muhammad's prophethood is proven by a rational syllogism, not blind faith.

Sanusi now presents the structured argument for Muhammad's prophethood. He arranges it as a logical syllogism with clear premises. This proof relies on the fact of the miracle and the universal principle connecting miracles to prophethood. Let's examine how this argument works.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the syllogistic form
Read the first part of the Arabic and translation to see the full syllogism. Notice it has a minor premise, a major premise, and a conclusion.
The argument is presented as 'it is said...' then yields the conclusion.
2
Analyze the minor premise
The minor premise states that Muhammad claimed prophethood, brought a miracle, and his opponents were unable to match it. Understand why these three components are necessary.
It's not just a miracle, but a miracle accompanied by inability to oppose.
3
Understand how the minor premise is known
Sanusi says the minor premise is known by tawātur (mass transmission). Learn what tawātur is and why it gives certain knowledge.
Tawātur involves transmission by many from many, leaving no doubt.
4
Connect to the major premise
The major premise is a universal conditional: anyone who meets the conditions is a true messenger. Sanusi notes the evidence for this has already been given in the discussion of miracles. Understand the logical flow.
The conclusion necessarily follows once both premises are accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نظم الدليل في إثباتِ نبوَّة نبينا ومولانا محمد صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ أنْ يُقالَ : نبينا ومولانا محمد صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ادَّعى الرسالةَ ، وظهر الخارقُ على وَفْقِ دعواهُ معَ العجز عن معارضتِهِ ، وكلُّ مَنِ ادَّعى الرسالة وظهر الخارقُ على وَفْقِ دعواهُ معَ العجز عن معارضته.. فهو رسولُ الله تعالى ؛ ينتج : أنَّ نبينا ومولانا محمداً صلى الله عليهِ وسلَّمَ رسولُ الله جل وعزَّ . أما الصغرى : فهي معلومة بالتواتر الذي ينقلُـه الـمـوافـقُ والمخالف ، والتواتر يفيد العلم ضرورةً على ما تقرَّرَ في أصول الفقه ، وأمَّا دليلُ الكبرى : فقد تقدَّمَ في وجهِ دلالة المعجزة
Arranging the proof for establishing the prophethood of our Prophet and our master Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, is to say: Our Prophet and our master Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, claimed the message, and a miracle appeared in accordance with his claim along with the inability to oppose it; and everyone who claimed the message and a miracle appeared in accordance with his claim along with the inability to oppose it, then he is a messenger of God Almighty. This yields: that our Prophet and our master Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, is the Messenger of God, Mighty and Majestic. As for the minor premise: it is known by tawātur (mass transmission) which is transmitted by both ally and opponent, and tawātur yields certain knowledge by necessity as established in uṣūl al-fiqh. As for the evidence for the major premise: it has preceded in the indication of the miracle.
Source pages p. 576

Commentary
This passage presents the formal proof of the prophethood of Muhammad (صلى الله عليه وسلم) in the form of a logical syllogism. The author states the arrangement (نظم الدليل) of the proof. The syllogism consists of two premises: the minor premise (الصغرى) and the major premise (الكبرى). The minor premise is that Muhammad claimed prophethood and a miracle appeared supporting his claim with no one able to oppose it. This is known through tawatur, the mass transmission of reports, which yields certain knowledge. The major premise is a universal conditional: anyone who claims prophethood and a miracle appears in support while opponents are unable to produce a similar miracle is a true messenger of God. The evidence for this major premise has already been explained in the discussion of the indication of a miracle. The conclusion is that Muhammad is indeed the Messenger of God.

Key Terms
نظم الدليل
صغرى
كبرى
تواتر
معجزة
عجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the proof of prophethood relies on blind faith.
Correction: In fact, it is a rational syllogism based on the observable fact of miracles and the principle that a miracle accompanied by inability to oppose is a divine confirmation.

Checkpoint
What is the minor premise (الصغرى) in the proof of Muhammad's prophethood?
A) Muhammad claimed prophethood and a miracle appeared.
B) Anyone with a miracle is a prophet.
C) The Jews denied him.
D) Miracles prove prophethood.

Key Takeaway
The prophethood of Muhammad is proven through a rational syllogism based on the miracle he brought, with the minor premise established by mass transmission (tawātur).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof that Muhammad is a messenger is presented as a logical certainty. Yet many of us live as if this truth does not demand a response. Have you personally verified this proof for yourself, or are you content with inherited belief?
?
If you truly believe that Muhammad's message is from God, what specific change in your life must happen today that you have been avoiding?
?
Consider the miracle brought by Muhammad—the Quran. Do you treat it as an undeniable sign that requires your full submission, or do you approach it selectively? What would change if you accepted this proof with the same certainty as a mathematical conclusion?

Three Views on the Sufficient Surah for I'jaz
Sanusi enumerates three opinions on sufficient surah length for i'jaz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main opinions regarding the sufficient length of a surah for Quranic miraculousness and the reasoning behind each one.

Core Idea
The Quran's miraculousness is not tied to a fixed surah length but to eloquence recognized by experts.

Sanusi presents three competing views on what constitutes the surah that demonstrates the Quran's inimitability. This passage is crucial because it shows that the miracle is not about arbitrary length but about rhetorical excellence. Let's examine each opinion and its justification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice that Sanusi first mentions a weak view, then the majority view, then the view he favors.
The passage uses technical terms like 'إعجاز' (miraculousness) and 'بلاغة' (eloquence).
2
Identify the three opinions
List the three opinions presented: 1) a weak opinion that the surah must contain verses of incapacitation, 2) the majority view that the shortest surah suffices, 3) the view of al-Qadi and Abu Ishaq that the miracle depends on an amount that reveals eloquence.
The first opinion is rejected because the word 'surah' is indefinite general.
3
Compare the rationales
For each opinion, note the proof or reasoning given. Why is the third view considered superior? It relies on the customary judgment of experts in eloquence.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about why the third view is flexible. How does it avoid the problems of the other two? It does not limit the miracle to a fixed length but ties it to the actual experience of eloquent people.
This view highlights the importance of expert consensus in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السورة هي المشتملة على آي التعجيز، وهذا ضعيف، لأن لفظ سورة فيها منكر مطلق، فلا يقيد بمثلها قدراً. وقال الجمهور من أصحابنا: يكفي أقصر سورة؛ كالعصر والكوثر. والذي ارتضاه القاضي في كتاب النقض وارتضاه أبو إسحاق: أن الإعجاز إنما يتعلق بقدر ما من الكلام؛ بحيث يتبين فيه تفاضل ذوي البلاغة، وهذا لا يتبين إلا فيما طال من السور بعض الطول، وقال: وهذا لا يضبط بحروف وكلم، وإنما يصار في مثله إلى المتعارف من أهل الخبرة والدراية بالبلاغة والنظم
The surah is that which contains verses of incapacitation, but this is weak, because the word 'surah' is indefinite general, so it is not restricted to a specific amount. The majority of our companions said: The shortest surah suffices, like al-Asr and al-Kawthar. And what al-Qadi approved in his book al-Naqd and Abu Ishaq approved is that miraculousness is attached to a certain amount of speech such that the superiority of eloquent people becomes evident, and this is not evident except in surahs of some length, and he said: This is not defined by letters and words, but rather in such matters one refers to the customary understanding of people of experience and knowledge of eloquence and composition.
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This passage discusses the criterion for what constitutes a surah for the purpose of the Quranic miracle. The author first mentions a weak opinion that the surah must contain verses of incapacitation (ây at-ta'jiz), then refutes it because the word 'surah' is indefinite and general, so it cannot be restricted. Then he presents the view of the majority of Ash'ari scholars that the shortest surah (like al-Asr or al-Kawthar) is sufficient. Then he presents the view adopted by al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar (in his book al-Naqd) and Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini: that the miracle pertains to an amount of speech that reveals the superiority of eloquent people, which occurs only in surahs of some length, but this amount cannot be defined by letter or word count; rather it is determined by the customary judgment of experts in eloquence.

Key Terms
إعجاز
سورة
مطلق
الجمهور
القاضي
أبو إسحاق
بلاغة
نظم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Quranic miracle requires a surah of a specific fixed length (e.g., a certain number of verses or letters).
Correction: The passage shows that the third and strongest view rejects a fixed length; it relies on the customary judgment of eloquence experts, which is flexible and context-dependent.

Checkpoint
According to al-Qadi and Abu Ishaq, how is the sufficient amount of Quran for miraculousness determined?
A) By a fixed number of letters.
B) By the shortest surah.
C) By the customary judgment of eloquence experts.
D) By the presence of verses of incapacitation.

Key Takeaway
The sufficient length for Quranic miraculousness is determined by the customary judgment of eloquence experts, not by fixed letter counts or arbitrary surah lengths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you rely on your own estimation of its beauty, or do you defer to the experts who have dedicated their lives studying Arabic eloquence? What does your answer reveal about your approach to knowledge?
?
The passage teaches that the miraculousness becomes evident in speech that reveals the superiority of eloquent people. Have you ever experienced that awe when encountering a verse that strikes you as sublime? What stops you from seeking that experience more earnestly?
?
The author rejects rigid definitions and points to the judgment of experts. Are there areas of your faith where you insist on rigid, easy-to-measure criteria rather than entrusting yourself to the guidance of scholars? Why?
