Ibn al-Tilmisani's Definitions: Fasaha, Jazala, Nazm, and Balagha
Quotes Ibn al-Tilmisani's definitions of four rhetorical terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn al-Tilmisani's definitions of fasaha, jazala, nazm, and balagha and how balagha combines jazala and nazm.

Core Idea
Balagha is the combination of word eloquence (jazala) and stylistic arrangement (nazm).

Before presenting the standard view on fasaha, the author cites Ibn al-Tilmisani's definitions of four key rhetorical terms. This provides a foundation for understanding how balagha scholars conceptualize eloquence. The passage shows that balagha is not a single quality but a composite of word-level and structure-level excellence.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn al-Tilmisani's quote
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'قالَ ابنُ التِّلِمْساني' to 'هو البلاغة'. Focus on grasping each term's definition.
Notice the conditional phrases 'بشرط' which specify the conditions for each term.
2
Identify the four terms and their definitions
List the terms: fasaha, jazala, nazm, balagha. For each, note the key condition that defines it. Fasaha requires clarity of purpose; jazala requires few letters and harmonious articulation; nazm is about arrangement; balagha is their sum.
The condition for fasaha here is different from the later standard view – this is one scholar's opinion.
3
Understand the relationship between the terms
Notice that balagha is defined as the combination of jazala and nazm. This implies that eloquence (balagha) has two components: eloquence of words (jazala) and eloquence of structure (nazm).
This relationship is not universally adopted but is informative for the discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالَ ابنُ التِّلِمْساني : ( الفصاحةُ : عبارة عن دلالة اللفظ على المعنى بشرطِ إيضاح الغرض منه ، والجزالة : عبارة عن دلالته على معناه بشرطِ قلَّةِ حروفِهِ وتناسب مخارجها ، والنظم عبارة عن الأسلوب الخاص في ترتيب الأقوالِ بعضها مع بعض ، ثم الحسْنُ فيهِ بحسَبِ تناسب الكلماتِ في مواردِها ، وذلك أنواع وأصناف ، ومجموع الجزالة والنظم هو البلاغة ) قلتُ : المشهور بين البيانيين : أنَّ الفصاحةَ تُوصَفُ بها الكلمة والكلام والمتكلم : فمعناها في الكلمةِ : أنْ تكونَ خالصةً مِنْ تنافرِ الحروفِ ؛ احترازاً من نحو قوله : غَدَائِرُهُ مُسْتَشْزَرَاتِ إِلَى الْعُلَا . والمحكّم في ذلك الذوق السليم . ومِنَ الغرابة ؛ احترازاً مِنْ قولِهِ : ما لكم تكأكَأْتُم عليَّ تكأْكُوكُم على ذي جِنَّةٍ ؟! افرنقعوا عنِّي . ومِنْ ضعفِ القياس ؛ احترازاً مِنْ قولِهِ : الْحَمْدُ اللهِ الْعَلِيُّ الْأَجْلَل إذْ قياسُهُ : الأجل بالإدغام . وبعضُهم يزيدُ : وأن تكون غير مكروهة في السمع ؛ احترازاً مِنْ نحو قوله : كَرِيمُ الْجِرِشَّى شَرِيفُ النَّسَبْ . والاستشزار : الارتفاع والرفع ؛ فعلى الأولى يكون قوله : مستشزرات بكسر الزاي ، وعلى الثاني بفتحها . والجِرِشَّى : النفس، وهذا اللفظ مما يكرهه السمع وينبو عنه اللسان.
Ibn al-Tilmisani said: 'Fasaha is an expression for the indication of the word to the meaning with the condition of clarifying the intended purpose; Jazala is an expression for its indication to its meaning with the condition of fewness of its letters and harmony of their articulation points; Nazm is an expression for the special style in arranging utterances with each other, then the beauty in it is according to the proportion of words in their contexts, and it has types and kinds, and the combination of Jazala and Nazm is Balagha.' I said: The well-known among the rhetoricians is that Fasaha is attributed to the word, the speech, and the speaker: its meaning in the word is that it be free from harshness of letters, as a precaution against something like his saying: 'Ghada'iruhu mustashziratin ila al-'ula.' And the arbiter in that is sound taste. And from strangeness, as a precaution against his saying: 'Ma lakum taka'ka'tum 'alayya taka'kukakum 'ala dhi jinnatin?! Ifranqa'u 'anni.' And from weakness of analogy, as a precaution against his saying: 'Al-hamdu lillahi al-'aliyyi al-ajlal,' since its analogy is 'al-ajal' with idgham. And some add: that it be not disliked in hearing, as a precaution against something like his saying: 'Karimu al-jirishsha sharifu al-nasab.' And al-istishzar: elevation and raising; according to the first, his word 'mustashzirat' is with kasr of the zay, and according to the second with fatha. And al-jirishsha: the self, and this word is such that hearing dislikes it and the tongue recoils from it.
Source pages p. 588

Commentary
This passage is a definitional passage that first presents Ibn al-Tilmisani's definitions of four key rhetorical terms: fasaha, jazala, nazm, and balagha. It then moves to the author's own clarification of the standard view among balagha scholars regarding fasaha, specifically its application to the word. The author states that fasaha is attributed to three things: the word (kalima), the speech (kalam), and the speaker (mutakallim). The focus is then on fasaha al-kalima, which is defined as the word being free from three defects: first, harshness of letters (tanafur al-huruf), exemplified by the line from Imru' al-Qais containing the word 'mustashzirat' where the combination of letters is somewhat harsh, though sound taste is the arbiter. Second, strangeness (gharaba), exemplified by the saying of a grammarian who used rare words like 'taka'ka'tum' and 'ifranqa'u' which are unfamiliar. Third, weakness of analogy (da'f al-qiyas), exemplified by a line where 'al-ajlal' is used instead of the standard 'al-ajal' with idgham. Some scholars add a fourth condition: that the word not be unpleasant to the ear (makruha fi al-sam'), exemplified by a line containing 'al-jirishsha' which means 'self' but is harsh to hear. The author then explains the word 'mustashzirat' from the first example, noting its two possible readings. This lesson establishes the precise technical definition of fasaha in the word, which is foundational for understanding later discussions of eloquence in kalam and other disciplines.

Key Terms
فصاحة
جزالة
نظم
بلاغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Tilmisani, what is balagha?
A) The indication of the word to its meaning with clarity
B) The combination of jazala and nazm
C) A special style in arranging utterances
D) The word being free from harsh letters

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Tilmisani defines balagha as the combination of word-level eloquence (jazala) and structural arrangement (nazm).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about Islam, do you ensure your words are both clear in purpose (fasaha) and pleasing in sound (jazala)? What is one change you can make today to improve your speech for Allah's sake?
?
Ibn al-Tilmisani defines balagha as the combination of precise word choice and structural harmony. Which aspect do you neglect more in your communication, and how can you strengthen it to better convey the truth?
?
The condition of fasaha is 'clarifying the intended purpose.' Think of a time when your words were misunderstood. How could you have applied this definition to prevent that? Will you commit to being more deliberate in your speech?

Conditions for Fasaha in the Word
Defines fasaha in the word with three essential conditions and examples, plus an optional fourth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three essential conditions for a word to be considered fasih according to the balagha scholars, along with examples and the role of sound taste.

Core Idea
A fasih word must be free from harshness, strangeness, and weak analogy.

After citing Ibn al-Tilmisani, the author presents the well-known view among balagha scholars on fasaha. The focus now narrows to fasaha in the word (kalima). The passage lists the conditions that a word must meet to be considered eloquent, each illustrated with a concrete example from Arabic poetry and prose.

Guided Path
1
Read the main statement on fasaha
Read from 'قلتُ : المشهور بين البيانيين' to 'احترازاً من نحو قوله : كَرِيمُ الْجِرِشَّى شَرِيفُ النَّسَبْ'. Notice that fasaha applies to word, speech, and speaker, but the focus here is on the word.
The author states his own view by saying 'قلتُ' (I said).
2
Identify the three core conditions
The text lists three conditions: freedom from harshness of letters (tanafur al-huruf), freedom from strangeness (gharaba), and freedom from weakness of analogy (da'f al-qiyas). Find the examples for each.
The arbiter for harshness is 'sound taste' (al-dhawq al-salim), not a fixed rule.
3
Examine the examples
For each condition, read the example line and understand why it violates the condition. The first example has harsh consonant clusters; the second uses rare words; the third uses an irregular form against analogy.
The author also explains the word 'mustashzirat' and its possible vowellings.
4
Note the additional fourth condition
Some scholars add a fourth condition: freedom from being disliked in hearing (makruha fi al-sam'). The example 'al-jirishsha' illustrates a word that is unpleasant to the ear.
This condition is not unanimously accepted; it is an addition by some.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The arbiter for harshness is 'sound taste'—an inner sensitivity. When was the last time you ignored your inner discomfort with a word or phrase because it was 'correct' by rule? How can you train your ear to trust your innate sense of what sounds good, even when rules say otherwise?

Faṣāḥa in Speech and Speaker
Defines faṣāḥa for speech and speaker with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how faṣāḥa is defined for speech (with its three requirements) and for the speaker (as a faculty).

Core Idea
Faṣāḥa is the foundation: words must be clear, harmonious, and free from complexity.

The author begins by specifying what it means for speech to be eloquent (faṣīḥ). He lists three conditions: words must be themselves eloquent, free from discord, and free from compositional weakness and semantic complexity. Then he defines faṣāḥa in the speaker as a faculty for producing such speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of faṣāḥa in speech
Focus on the three conditions: words faṣīḥa, no discord among them, and being safe from weak composition and semantic complexity. Note the examples of each flaw.
The examples are poetic and serve as warnings against common errors.
2
Understand the three defects to avoid
Discord (tanāfur) makes words harsh; weak composition (ḍaʿf al-taʾlīf) causes grammatical oddity; semantic complexity (taʿqīd maʿnawī) obscures meaning.
3
Read the definition of faṣāḥa in the speaker
It is a faculty (malaka) enabling the speaker to express intended meanings eloquently. This moves from the speech itself to the speaker's ability.
Notice that faṣāḥa is primarily about linguistic correctness and clarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما معناها في الكلام : فأن تكون كلماته فصيحةً على ما سبق ، لا تنافر بينها ؛ احترازاً مِنْ نحو قوله : [ من الرجز] وَقَبْرُ حَرْبِ بِمَكَانِ قَفْرٍ وَلَيْسَ قُرْبَ قَبْرِ حَرْبٍ قَبْرُ سالماً مِنْ ضَعفِ التأليفِ ؛ احترازاً مِنْ نحو : ضربَ غلامُهُ زيداً ، ومِنَ التعقيد المعنوي ؛ احترازاً مِنْ نحو قوله : [ من الطويل] وَمَا مِثْلُهُ فِي النَّاسِ إِلا مُمَلَكاً أَبُو أُمِّهِ حَيٌّ أَبُوهُ يُقَارِبُهُ وأما معناها في المُتكلّم : فهو أن تكونَ لَهُ مَلَكَةٌ يَقتدِرُ بها على التعبير بالفصيح عما يقصدهُ مِنْ كلمة أو كلام . وأما البلاغة : فلا يُوصَفُ بها إلا الكلام والمتكلم أما معناها في الكلام : فهو أن يكون فصيحاً، جارياً على ما يقتضيه الحالُ ؛ أي : السبب الذي ورد الكلام لأجله ؛ كالكلام الوارد لدفع إنكار مُنكِرٍ ؛ فإنَّهُ يناسبُهُ أنْ يُؤكَّد بحسب مراتب الإنكار والوارد لإفادة خالي الذهنِ مِنَ الحُكْم يناسبُهُ أَنْ يُلقى إليه الكلام غير مُؤكَّد ، والوارد لإفادة مَنْ هو مُشعر بالحكمِ شَاكٌ فِيهِ يُستحسَنُ أَنْ يُؤكَّدَ لهُ الكلامُ مِنْ غيرِ وجوب ، وقد يُعكَسُ الأمر في هذهِ الثلاثةِ لعوارض ذلك ، والأحوال وما يليقُ بها متسعةً جداً ، مُقرَّر قواعدها في فن علم المعاني وأما معناها في المُتكلّم : فهي مَلَكَةٌ يَقتدِرُ بها على التعبير بكلام بليغ . فعُلِمَ مِنْ هذا : أنَّ البلاغة أخصُّ مِنَ الفصاحة ، فكلُّ بليغ فصيح ، وليس كل فصيح بليغاً ، وقد تُطلَقُ إحداهما على الأخرى توشعاً . وللبلاغة طرفان : أعلى ؛ وهو الإعجاز ، والمحكّم فيه الذوق ، وأدنى ؛ وهو ما إذا بُدِّلَ عن حالِهِ التحق عندَ البلغاء بأصواتِ الحيوانات ، وبينهما مراتب لا تكاد تنحصر .
And as for its meaning in speech: it is that its words be eloquent (faṣīḥa) according to what preceded, without discord among them; as a precaution against something like his saying [from rajaz]: 'And the grave of Ḥarb is in a desolate place, and there is no grave near the grave of Ḥarb' — safe from weakness of composition; as a precaution against something like 'ḍaraba ghulāmuhu Zaydan' (his slave struck Zayd), and from semantic complexity; as a precaution against something like his saying [from ṭawīl]: 'And there is none like him among people except a king, the son of his mother whose father is alive, resembling him.' And as for its meaning in the speaker: it is that he have a faculty (malaka) by which he is able to express with eloquent (faṣīḥ) speech what he intends from a word or discourse. And as for eloquence (balāgha): it is described only for speech and the speaker. As for its meaning in speech: it is that it be eloquent (faṣīḥ), conforming to what the situation requires; that is, the cause for which the speech was uttered; like speech given to repel the denial of a denier; for it is appropriate that it be emphasized according to the degrees of denial, and speech given to inform one whose mind is empty of the judgment it is appropriate that the speech be delivered to him unemphasized, and speech given to inform one who has a notion of the judgment [but] is in doubt about it, it is deemed good that the speech be emphasized without obligation, and the matter may be reversed in these three due to attendant circumstances, and the states and what suits them are very extensive, their rules are established in the discipline of the science of meanings (ʿilm al-maʿānī). And as for its meaning in the speaker: it is a faculty (malaka) by which he is able to express with eloquent speech (balīgh). So it is understood from this: that eloquence (balāgha) is more specific than eloquence (faṣāḥa), for every balīgh is faṣīḥ, and not every faṣīḥ is balīgh, and one of them may be used for the other by broadening. And eloquence (balāgha) has two extremes: the highest, which is inimitability (iʿjāz), and the arbiter in it is taste; and the lowest, which is that if it is changed from its state, it joins, according to the eloquent, the sounds of animals; and between them are degrees that are almost endless.
Source pages p. 590

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification passage. The author first defines faṣāḥa (eloquence) in speech: it requires that the words be eloquent (as earlier defined), free from discord (تنافر), weak composition (ضعف التأليف as in the examples), and semantic complexity (تعقيد معنوي). He gives poetic examples of each flaw. Then he defines faṣāḥa in the speaker: a faculty enabling one to express intended meanings eloquently.

Next he turns to balāgha (rhetorical eloquence), which is only attributed to speech and speaker. Balāgha in speech means the speech is faṣīḥ and also conforms to the situation (ما يقتضيه الحال). He gives three typical cases: repelling denial (needs emphasis), informing an ignorant mind (no emphasis), and informing a doubter (emphasis recommended but not obligatory). He notes these can be reversed due to circumstances, and the rules are set in ʿilm al-maʿānī. Balāgha in the speaker is a faculty for producing balīgh speech.

From these definitions he concludes that balāgha is more specific (أخص) than faṣāḥa: every balīgh speech/speaker is faṣīḥ, but not vice versa. However, they are sometimes used interchangeably for broadening. Finally, he mentions the two extremes of balāgha: the highest is iʿjāz (inimitability, like the Qur'an) judged by taste, and the lowest is that which if altered becomes like animal sounds. Between them are countless degrees.

Key Terms
فصاحة
تنافر
ضعف التأليف
تعقيد معنوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Faṣāḥa only applies to individual words, not sentences.
Correction: Faṣāḥa in speech also considers harmony and clarity of composition, as shown by the examples.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a requirement for faṣāḥa in speech according to this passage?
A) Words must be eloquent (faṣīḥa).
B) There must be no discord among words.
C) The speech must be suitable for the situation.
D) There must be no weak composition or semantic complexity.

Key Takeaway
Faṣāḥa in speech requires eloquent words, harmony, and clear structure; in the speaker, it is a faculty for producing such speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you consider your own speech to be free from discord, weak composition, and semantic complexity? When was the last time someone misunderstood you because of unclear wording — and what does that reveal about your need to cultivate faṣāḥa?
?
The passage defines faṣāḥa in the speaker as a malaka — a stable faculty. If you were to examine your daily conversations, would you say you have this faculty? What specific step will you take this week to strengthen it, starting with one of the three faults mentioned?

Balāgha in Speech and Speaker
Defines balāgha for speech and speaker with situational condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how balāgha differs from faṣāḥa by adding suitability to the context, and how it is defined for both speech and speaker.

Core Idea
Balāgha is eloquence tailored to the audience and occasion.

After defining faṣāḥa, the author turns to balāgha. He notes it applies only to speech and speaker, and adds a key condition: the speech must not only be eloquent (faṣīḥ) but also conform to the situation (muqtadā al-ḥāl). He gives examples of how emphasis varies with the listener's state.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of balāgha in speech
Notice that balāgha includes faṣāḥa (the speech must be faṣīḥ) plus the condition of conforming to the situation.
2
Understand the three typical situations
The author lists three cases: repelling a denier (needs emphasis), informing someone ignorant (no emphasis), and informing a doubter (emphasis recommended). These show how context dictates style.
He adds that these can be reversed due to circumstances.
3
Read the definition of balāgha in the speaker
It is a faculty for producing balīgh speech, i.e., speech that is both eloquent and context-appropriate.

وأما معناها في الكلام : فأن تكون كلماته فصيحةً على ما سبق ، لا تنافر بينها ؛ احترازاً مِنْ نحو قوله : [ من الرجز] وَقَبْرُ حَرْبِ بِمَكَانِ قَفْرٍ وَلَيْسَ قُرْبَ قَبْرِ حَرْبٍ قَبْرُ سالماً مِنْ ضَعفِ التأليفِ ؛ احترازاً مِنْ نحو : ضربَ غلامُهُ زيداً ، ومِنَ التعقيد المعنوي ؛ احترازاً مِنْ نحو قوله : [ من الطويل] وَمَا مِثْلُهُ فِي النَّاسِ إِلا مُمَلَكاً أَبُو أُمِّهِ حَيٌّ أَبُوهُ يُقَارِبُهُ وأما معناها في المُتكلّم : فهو أن تكونَ لَهُ مَلَكَةٌ يَقتدِرُ بها على التعبير بالفصيح عما يقصدهُ مِنْ كلمة أو كلام . وأما البلاغة : فلا يُوصَفُ بها إلا الكلام والمتكلم أما معناها في الكلام : فهو أن يكون فصيحاً، جارياً على ما يقتضيه الحالُ ؛ أي : السبب الذي ورد الكلام لأجله ؛ كالكلام الوارد لدفع إنكار مُنكِرٍ ؛ فإنَّهُ يناسبُهُ أنْ يُؤكَّد بحسب مراتب الإنكار والوارد لإفادة خالي الذهنِ مِنَ الحُكْم يناسبُهُ أَنْ يُلقى إليه الكلام غير مُؤكَّد ، والوارد لإفادة مَنْ هو مُشعر بالحكمِ شَاكٌ فِيهِ يُستحسَنُ أَنْ يُؤكَّدَ لهُ الكلامُ مِنْ غيرِ وجوب ، وقد يُعكَسُ الأمر في هذهِ الثلاثةِ لعوارض ذلك ، والأحوال وما يليقُ بها متسعةً جداً ، مُقرَّر قواعدها في فن علم المعاني وأما معناها في المُتكلّم : فهي مَلَكَةٌ يَقتدِرُ بها على التعبير بكلام بليغ . فعُلِمَ مِنْ هذا : أنَّ البلاغة أخصُّ مِنَ الفصاحة ، فكلُّ بليغ فصيح ، وليس كل فصيح بليغاً ، وقد تُطلَقُ إحداهما على الأخرى توشعاً . وللبلاغة طرفان : أعلى ؛ وهو الإعجاز ، والمحكّم فيه الذوق ، وأدنى ؛ وهو ما إذا بُدِّلَ عن حالِهِ التحق عندَ البلغاء بأصواتِ الحيوانات ، وبينهما مراتب لا تكاد تنحصر .

Relationship Between Faṣāḥa and Balāgha
Clarifies that balāgha is more specific and describes its extremes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that balāgha is more specific than faṣāḥa, its two extremes (iʿjāz and non-speech), and the countless degrees between them.

Core Idea
Every balīgh is faṣīḥ, but not every faṣīḥ is balīgh – balāgha is a higher level.

Having defined both terms, the author now clarifies their logical relationship. He states that balāgha is more specific (akhṣṣ) than faṣāḥa: all balīgh speech/speaker is faṣīḥ, but the converse is not true. He then mentions the highest and lowest levels of balāgha, with iʿjāz as the peak and near-animal sounds as the bottom.

Guided Path
1
Read the relationship sentence
Note the clear logical statement: 'al-balāgha akhṣṣ min al-faṣāḥa' (balāgha is more specific). This means every instance of balāgha is also an instance of faṣāḥa, but not vice versa.
2
Understand the two extremes of balāgha
The highest is iʿjāz (inimitability), judged by taste; the lowest is speech that if altered becomes like animal sounds.
3
Reflect on the degrees between extremes
The author says there are almost endless degrees, reminding us that eloquence is a spectrum.

Source Text

According to the passage, what is the relationship between faṣāḥa and balāgha?
A) They are completely separate and unrelated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that balāgha is not just about speaking correctly but speaking appropriately. When was the last time you chose your words purely based on correctness without considering your listener? How might you have changed those words if you had aimed for balāgha? Commit to one conversation this week where you will intentionally adapt your speech to your audience.
?
The highest level of balāgha is iʿjāz, found only in the Qur'an. Have you ever felt that your own words failed to express what you truly felt – a moment you regretted a misstatement or felt misunderstood? How does that inadequacy drive you to appreciate the Qur'an's perfection? Write down one specific way you will turn to the Qur'an for guidance in your speech.
?
Between the extreme of animal-like sounds and the peak of iʿjāz are countless degrees. Honestly assess your own level of eloquence today: are you closer to the lowest or the highest? What is one concrete action you will take this week to move upward – such as memorizing eloquent phrases, avoiding slang, or studying a chapter on balāgha?

The Two Outcomes When Apparent Meaning Is Impossible
States the two outcomes after turning away from impossible apparent meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible outcomes after turning away from an impossible apparent meaning: adopting a single valid interpretation or practicing tafwid with tanzih.

Core Idea
If one tawil exists, adopt it; otherwise, consign the meaning to Allah.

Sanusi now turns to texts whose apparent meaning is impossible for Allah, such as the verse about istiwa. He explains that by consensus we must turn away from that impossible meaning, and then either adopt the only valid interpretation or, if multiple possibilities exist, consign the true meaning to Allah while affirming His transcendence. This lesson focuses on that key distinction and the early scholarly position.

Guided Path
1
Read the rule and condition
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice that after stating we turn away from the impossible apparent meaning by consensus, Sanusi gives two cases: if there is one valid interpretation, it must be adopted; otherwise, tafwid with tanzih is obligatory.
The phrase 'مذهب الأقدمين' refers to the way of the early scholars.
2
Contrast with Imam al-Haramayn
Sanusi notes that this position—tafwid when multiple interpretations are possible—is the way of the early scholars, contrary to Imam al-Haramayn. Consider what this tells you about early vs. later approaches.
3
Reflect on the consensus
Notice the word 'اتفاقاً' (by consensus). Why do all scholars agree that we must turn away from the impossible apparent meaning? This connects to the core principle that revelation cannot contradict reason.
This will be further justified in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان ضوابط التأويل ، ومتى يُصار إليه
أما ما استحالَ ظاهرُهُ ؛ نحو : ﴿ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى * ه : ٥] فإنَّا نصرفُهُ عن ظاهره اتفاقاً ، ثم إنْ كانَ له تأويل واحد تعيَّنَ الحمل عليهِ ، وإلا وجبَ التفويضُ معَ التنزيهِ ، وهو مذهب الأقدمينَ ، خلافاً لإمام الحرمين .
لمَّا ذكرَ أنَّ ما يُجوِّزُهُ العقل إذا أخبر الشرعُ بوقوعِهِ يجبُ أنْ يُؤْمَنَ بهِ على ظاهره ، ولا يجوز تأويلُه ؛ والتعرُّضُ لتأويله بدعة . . ذكرَ أَنَّ
عن
ما أخبر الشرعُ بهِ ، وكانَ ظاهرُهُ مستحيلاً عندَ العقل فإنَّا نصرفُهُ ظاهره المستحيل ؛ لأنَّا نعلم قطعاً أنَّ الشرع لا يخبر بوقوع ما لا يمكنُ وقوعه ، ولو كذبنا العقل في هذا ، وعملنا بظاهرِ النقل المستحيل . . لأدَّى ذلك إلى انهدام النقلِ أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ العقل أصل لثبوتِ النبوَّاتِ التي يتفرّعُ عنها صحة النقل ، فيلزم إذاً مِنْ تكذيب العقل تكذيب النقل .
ثم بعد صرْفِ اللفظ عن ظاهره المستحيل :
إنْ لم يكن له بعد ذلكَ إلا تأويل واحد صحيح .. تعيَّنَ الحمل عليه ؛ لعدم وجودِ غيره ؛ وذلكَ مثل قوله : ( وَهُوَ مَعَكُمْ أَيْنَ مَا كُنتُمْ ) [الحديد : ٤] فَإِنَّهُ يستحيلُ حملُهُ على ظاهرِهِ مِنَ المصاحبة بالذاتِ ، ولم
Clarification of the rules of interpretation and when it is resorted to.
As for that whose apparent meaning is impossible, such as [the verse] 'The Most Merciful is established over the Throne' [TaHa:5], we do turn it away from its apparent meaning by consensus. Then if it has only one valid interpretation, it must be taken to mean that. Otherwise, consignment (tafwid) along with transcendence (tanzih) is obligatory, and this is the way of the early scholars, contrary to Imam al-Haramayn.
When he mentioned that what the intellect deems possible if the Law informs of its occurrence must be believed according to its apparent meaning, and it is not permissible to interpret it, and engaging in its interpretation is innovation... he mentioned that what the Law informs of and whose apparent meaning is impossible according to the intellect, we do turn it away from its impossible apparent meaning; because we know with certainty that the Law does not inform of the occurrence of something that cannot occur. If we were to falsify the intellect in this and act according to the impossible apparent meaning of the transmitted text, that would lead to the collapse of the transmitted text as well; because the intellect is the basis for the establishment of prophethoods upon which the soundness of transmission depends. So from falsifying the intellect, falsification of transmission necessarily follows.
Then after turning the word away from its impossible apparent meaning, if it has only one correct interpretation, it must be taken to mean that, due to the absence of any other. An example of that is His saying: 'And He is with you wherever you are' [al-Hadid:4]; indeed, it is impossible to interpret it according to its apparent meaning of physical companionship, and
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the rules for interpreting revealed texts when their apparent meaning is impossible. The author first states that when a text's apparent meaning is impossible (like 'istiwa' meaning physical sitting), we must turn it away from that impossible meaning by consensus. Then if there is only one possible valid interpretation, we adopt it; otherwise, we consign the true meaning to Allah while affirming His transcendence (tafwid with tanzih). This is the way of the early scholars, contrary to Imam al-Haramayn. Then the author explains the reasoning: just as the intellect tells us that we must accept possible events reported by revelation literally, so too when revelation reports something impossible according to intellect, we must interpret it because revelation cannot report what is impossible. He argues that falsifying the intellect would lead to the collapse of transmission itself, since the intellect is the foundation for proving prophethood. Then he reiterates: after turning away the impossible apparent meaning, if only one interpretation is possible, we adopt it, giving the example of 'He is with you' which cannot mean physical presence.

Key Terms
التأويل
التفويض
التنزيه
ظاهر النص
المستحيل
مذهب الأقدمين
إمام الحرمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the early scholars never practiced any interpretation (ta'wil) at all.
Correction: The passage shows that the early scholars only resorted to tafwid when there was no single valid interpretation; otherwise, they did adopt a ta'wil.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what must be done if after turning away from the impossible apparent meaning, there is only one correct interpretation?
A) Consign the meaning to Allah (tafwid)
B) Adopt that interpretation
C) Reject the text
D) Follow the apparent meaning despite impossibility

Key Takeaway
When a text's apparent meaning is impossible, we must turn away from it and either adopt the unique valid interpretation or practice tafwid with tanzih, which is the early scholarly position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse of the Quran whose apparent meaning seems difficult to accept, do you rush to adopt any interpretation that comes to mind, or do you follow the disciplined method of first recognizing the impossibility of the literal sense and then seeking a sound interpretation or consigning the meaning to Allah? Which of these have you been practicing, and what does that reveal about your approach to divine revelation?
?
The passage highlights that turning away from the impossible apparent meaning is by consensus. Yet, in your own heart, have you ever struggled to let go of a literal understanding that makes Allah seem like a physical being? What does that resistance show about your need for tanzih and your submission to the consensus of the scholars?
?
If you were faced with a verse where no single interpretation is certain, would you be content with tafwid, trusting that Allah knows best? Or would you feel uneasy, craving a definite answer? How does your comfort with not knowing the exact meaning reflect your reliance on Allah's knowledge versus your own?

Method of Learning
intermediate
5 min
Why the Intellect Must Guide Interpretation
Provides the rational justification and an example for interpreting impossible apparent meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational justification for turning away from impossible apparent meanings: that revelation cannot contradict the intellect, and that denying the intellect would undermine revelation itself.

Core Idea
Falsifying reason to follow a text destroys the very basis of revelation.

Sanusi now provides the reasoning behind the rule: just as we accept revelation about possible events literally, we must interpret when the apparent meaning is impossible. He argues that revelation cannot report something impossible, and that if we were to reject reason to uphold a literal reading, we would actually collapse the foundation of transmission itself. This lesson unpacks that argument and illustrates it with the example of divine omnipresence.

Guided Path
1
Read the reasoning passage
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'لما ذكر أن ما يجوزه العقل...'. Follow the argument that revelation cannot report what is impossible, and that the intellect is the foundation for proving prophethood.
Notice the logical structure: if we deny reason to follow an impossible literal meaning, we end up denying revelation itself.
2
Analyze the example
Look at the example of 'He is with you wherever you are'. Why must it be interpreted? Because its apparent meaning—physical companionship—is impossible for Allah. The only valid interpretation is what? The passage implies it has a single ta'wil.
This example illustrates the case where a single interpretation must be adopted.
3
Connect back to the rule
This reasoning underpins the earlier rule: we must turn away from impossible apparent meanings. Understand that this is not arbitrary; it is based on preserving both reason and revelation.

Source Text

?

?

?

The Two Methods: Specifying Ta'wil or Delegating to Allah
Sanusi poses the methodological question of ta'yin vs. tafwid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scholarly approaches to handling multiple sound interpretations of ambiguous Qur'anic verses: specifying one interpretation vs. delegating the true meaning to Allah.

Core Idea
When a verse has many valid interpretations, early scholars preferred tafwid over specifying one.

Before diving into the famous example of istawa, Imam Sanusi first sets up the methodological debate. When a Qur'anic verse has multiple sound interpretations after removing the literal meaning that does not befit Allah, what should a scholar do? Should he pick one to help the common people, or should he leave the intended meaning to Allah? This passage presents both answers and their proponents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once and then the translation. Notice that the passage is a question: two possible approaches are presented side by side.
The author does not yet give his preference here.
2
Identify the two positions
Look for the description of each approach: one from Imam al-Haramayn and one from the early scholars (al-aqdamun). What does each method involve?
The key terms are 'ta'yin' (specification) and 'tafwid'/'waqf' (delegation/stopping).
3
Understand the underlying problem
Why is there confusion? Because after negating a literal meaning that is impossible for Allah, several interpretations remain. The question is how to handle them for the public.
The problem arises from the need to avoid both confusion and arbitrary specification.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why Imam al-Haramayn's method is 'to remove confusion' and why the early scholars' method is 'to prevent arbitrariness'. What values are at stake?
This prepares you for the author's later argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإِنْ كانَ لهُ بعد ذلك تأويلات، كلُّ واحدٍ منها مستقيم؛ فهل يُعيَّنُ واحدٌ منها ليندفعَ اللَّبْسُ عن العوام، وهو مذهب إمام الحرمين عنِ التعيين ويُفوَّضُ الأمرُ فيهِ إلى اللهِ تعالى دفعاً للتحكُّم، أو يوقَفُ وهو مذهب الأقدمينَ؟ وذلك مثل قولِهِ تعالى: ﴿عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى﴾ [طه: ٥]، الاستواء؛ بمعنى: الاستقرار المكاني.. محال في حقه تعالى، وبقي بعد ذلك تأويلات صحيحة: أحدُها: أنْ يكونَ استوى بمعنى: استولى عليه بتصريفه له كيف شاء. الثاني: أن يكون بمعنى: قصد إلى خلق شيء هنالك. الثالثُ: أن تكونَ (على) بمعنى: الباء، و(استوى) بمعنى: كَمَّلَ أي: كَمَّلَ الخَلْقَ بالعرش. الرابع: أنَّ المستقرَّ فوقَ العرشِ مخلوق مِنْ مخلوقاتِهِ يُسمَّى استوى. إلى غير ذلك مما قيل. والأظهرُ: مذهبُ الأقدمينَ؛ في ترْكِ تعيين بعضها، وتفويض المقصودِ منها إلى الله تعالى، مع القطع بتنزيهِهِ جَلَّ وعلا عمَّا لا يليقُ بهِ؛ لأنَّ تعيين أحدِ المحتملاتِ الجائزة بغير دليل بدعة في الدين وتجاسر عظيم، وتعيينُ مَنْ عَيَّنَ شيئاً منها كالإمام إنَّما كان لدليل يُرجحُهُ مِنْ جهةِ اللغة أو غيرها، والله تعالى أعلمُ. عدم التعيين مذهب سُنّي سَنيٌّ، لم يختلف أهل الحق بصحته.
And if there are after that interpretations (ta'wilat), each one of them sound; so is a single one specified to remove confusion from the common people—this is the school of Imam al-Haramayn—and the matter is delegated (yufawwad) to Allah the Exalted to prevent arbitrariness, or is it stopped (yuqaf) and that is the school of the early ones (al-aqdamun)? And that is like His statement the Exalted: {He established Himself above the Throne (istawa)} [Taha: 5]. Establishedness (al-istawa) meaning spatial settlement is impossible with respect to Him the Exalted, and after that remain sound interpretations: one of them: that 'istawa' means He conquered it (istawla) by disposing it as He wills. The second: that it means He intended to create something there. The third: that 'ala' means 'with' (al-ba'), and 'istawa' means 'perfected' (kammala), i.e., He perfected the creation with the Throne. The fourth: that the one settled above the Throne is a created being among His creations called 'istawa'. And so on to other things that have been said. And the most apparent (al-azhar) is the school of the early ones: in leaving off specifying some of them, and delegating the intended meaning from them to Allah the Exalted, while definitively declaring His transcendence (tanzih) above what does not befit Him; because specifying any of the permissible possibilities without evidence is innovation in religion (bid'a) and great audacity, and the specification of whoever specified something from them like the Imam was only due to an evidence that preponderates it from the standpoint of language or otherwise. And Allah the Exalted knows best. Non-specification is a sublime Sunni school, concerning whose soundness the people of truth have not differed.
Source pages p. 626

Commentary
This passage is a methodological argument about how to deal with ambiguous Qur'anic verses (mutashabihat) that have multiple possible interpretations after rejecting the literal meaning that does not befit God. The author presents the issue: when a verse has many sound ta'wil (interpretations), should one specify a single interpretation to clear up confusion for the common people, or should one refrain from specifying and delegate the true meaning to God? He identifies two positions: the first is attributed to Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), who held that one should specify an interpretation to prevent confusion; the second is the way of the early scholars (al-aqdamun), which is to stop (waqf) and leave the intended meaning to God (tafwid). The author then gives the classic example of the verse 'istawa 'ala al-'arsh'. After negating the literal meaning of spatial settlement, he lists four sound interpretations: (1) istawa meaning istawla (He conquered/dominated); (2) istawa meaning qasada (He intended); (3) interpreting 'ala as bi (with) and istawa as kammala (perfected); (4) positing a created entity called 'istawa' settled on the Throne. He then declares that the more apparent (al-azhar) position is that of the early scholars: to refrain from specifying any single interpretation, to delegate the true meaning to God, while firmly affirming God's transcendence above all imperfections. The reason given is that specifying one of several permissible possibilities without evidence is innovation and audacity. He notes that when some scholars, like the Imam, did specify a particular ta'wil, they had a linguistic or contextual evidence to prefer it. The passage concludes that the position of non-specification is a noble Sunni school upon which the people of truth have agreed.

Key Terms
التأويل (ta'wil)
التفويض (tafwid)
الوقف (waqf)
التعيين (ta'yin)
إمام الحرمين (Imam al-Haramayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the purpose of Imam al-Haramayn's method of specifying a single ta'wil?
A) To prove that his interpretation is the only correct one
B) To remove confusion from the common people
C) To prevent innovation in religion
D) To follow the early scholars

Key Takeaway
The two main approaches to multiple sound ta'wil are specification (ta'yin) and delegation (tafwid), each with a different goal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse of the Qur'an whose outward meaning seems difficult or problematic, do you feel a pressing need to settle on a specific interpretation to feel at ease, or can you sincerely leave its full understanding to Allah? What does your immediate reaction reveal about the state of your heart toward divine revelation?
?
The passage tells us that early scholars chose tafwid to avoid 'tahakkum' — imposing one's own opinion without divine warrant. Think of a time you insisted on your own understanding of a religious text to win an argument or prove a point. Are you willing to practice tafwid as a discipline against your own ego, even when it feels uncomfortable?
?
If a loved one comes to you confused by a verse about Allah's attributes, would you be more inclined to specify one interpretation to 'remove their confusion' (as in ta'yin) or to teach them tafwid, trusting that Allah knows best? Which approach truly serves their faith in the long run, and why does your answer reveal your own reliance on certainty?

The Example of Istawa and the Preferability of Tafwid
Sanusi illustrates the debate with istawa and argues for tafwid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse of istawa is used as an example and why the author prefers tafwid over specifying a single interpretation, as well as the condition that specification is allowed only with evidence.

Core Idea
Specifying a ta'wil without evidence is innovation; tafwid is the safer, sunni path.

Having framed the methodological debate, Sanusi now brings the classic example: the verse 'He established Himself above the Throne' (istawa). After negating the literal meaning of spatial settlement, he lists four sound interpretations. Then he declares that the way of the early scholars—tafwid—is preferable, because specifying any one interpretation without evidence is religious innovation. However, he notes that if a scholar does specify with valid evidence, that is acceptable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with the example
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the verse and the list of four ta'wil. Notice the pattern: each interpretation reinterprets either 'istawa' or 'ala'.
The author rejects the literal meaning first.
2
Identify the four ta'wil
List them in order: istawla, qasada, kammala with ala as bi, and a created entity called istawa. Understand the linguistic moves.
They are all grammatically possible.
3
Grasp the author's reasoning
Why does he prefer tafwid? Read his argument: specifying without evidence is bid'a and audacity. But what about when there is evidence, like the Imam?
The author acknowledges that specification with evidence is acceptable.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Note the final statement: non-specification is a sublime Sunni school agreed upon by the people of truth. What does this tell you about the mainstream position?
This is a strong endorsement of tafwid.

وإِنْ كانَ لهُ بعد ذلك تأويلات، كلُّ واحدٍ منها مستقيم؛ فهل يُعيَّنُ واحدٌ منها ليندفعَ اللَّبْسُ عن العوام، وهو مذهب إمام الحرمين عنِ التعيين ويُفوَّضُ الأمرُ فيهِ إلى اللهِ تعالى دفعاً للتحكُّم، أو يوقَفُ وهو مذهب الأقدمينَ؟ وذلك مثل قولِهِ تعالى: ﴿عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى﴾ [طه: ٥]، الاستواء؛ بمعنى: الاستقرار المكاني.. محال في حقه تعالى، وبقي بعد ذلك تأويلات صحيحة: أحدُها: أنْ يكونَ استوى بمعنى: استولى عليه بتصريفه له كيف شاء. الثاني: أن يكون بمعنى: قصد إلى خلق شيء هنالك. الثالثُ: أن تكونَ (على) بمعنى: الباء، و(استوى) بمعنى: كَمَّلَ أي: كَمَّلَ الخَلْقَ بالعرش. الرابع: أنَّ المستقرَّ فوقَ العرشِ مخلوق مِنْ مخلوقاتِهِ يُسمَّى استوى. إلى غير ذلك مما قيل. والأظهرُ: مذهبُ الأقدمينَ؛ في ترْكِ تعيين بعضها، وتفويض المقصودِ منها إلى الله تعالى، مع القطع بتنزيهِهِ جَلَّ وعلا عمَّا لا يليقُ بهِ؛ لأنَّ تعيين أحدِ المحتملاتِ الجائزة بغير دليل بدعة في الدين وتجاسر عظيم، وتعيينُ مَنْ عَيَّنَ شيئاً منها كالإمام إنَّما كان لدليل يُرجحُهُ مِنْ جهةِ اللغة أو غيرها، والله تعالى أعلمُ. عدم التعيين مذهب سُنّي سَنيٌّ، لم يختلف أهل الحق بصحته.
And if there are after that interpretations (ta'wilat), each one of them sound; so is a single one specified to remove confusion from the common people—this is the school of Imam al-Haramayn—and the matter is delegated (yufawwad) to Allah the Exalted to prevent arbitrariness, or is it stopped (yuqaf) and that is the school of the early ones (al-aqdamun)? And that is like His statement the Exalted: {He established Himself above the Throne (istawa)} [Taha: 5]. Establishedness (al-istawa) meaning spatial settlement is impossible with respect to Him the Exalted, and after that remain sound interpretations: one of them: that 'istawa' means He conquered it (istawla) by disposing it as He wills. The second: that it means He intended to create something there. The third: that 'ala' means 'with' (al-ba'), and 'istawa' means 'perfected' (kammala), i.e., He perfected the creation with the Throne. The fourth: that the one settled above the Throne is a created being among His creations called 'istawa'. And so on to other things that have been said. And the most apparent (al-azhar) is the school of the early ones: in leaving off specifying some of them, and delegating the intended meaning from them to Allah the Exalted, while definitively declaring His transcendence (tanzih) above what does not befit Him; because specifying any of the permissible possibilities without evidence is innovation in religion (bid'a) and great audacity, and the specification of whoever specified something from them like the Imam was only due to an evidence that preponderates it from the standpoint of language or otherwise. And Allah the Exalted knows best. Non-specification is a sublime Sunni school, concerning whose soundness the people of truth have not differed.
Source pages p. 626

Commentary
This passage is a methodological argument about how to deal with ambiguous Qur'anic verses (mutashabihat) that have multiple possible interpretations after rejecting the literal meaning that does not befit God. The author presents the issue: when a verse has many sound ta'wil (interpretations), should one specify a single interpretation to clear up confusion for the common people, or should one refrain from specifying and delegate the true meaning to God? He identifies two positions: the first is attributed to Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), who held that one should specify an interpretation to prevent confusion; the second is the way of the early scholars (al-aqdamun), which is to stop (waqf) and leave the intended meaning to God (tafwid). The author then gives the classic example of the verse 'istawa 'ala al-'arsh'. After negating the literal meaning of spatial settlement, he lists four sound interpretations: (1) istawa meaning istawla (He conquered/dominated); (2) istawa meaning qasada (He intended); (3) interpreting 'ala as bi (with) and istawa as kammala (perfected); (4) positing a created entity called 'istawa' settled on the Throne. He then declares that the more apparent (al-azhar) position is that of the early scholars: to refrain from specifying any single interpretation, to delegate the true meaning to God, while firmly affirming God's transcendence above all imperfections. The reason given is that specifying one of several permissible possibilities without evidence is innovation and audacity. He notes that when some scholars, like the Imam, did specify a particular ta'wil, they had a linguistic or contextual evidence to prefer it. The passage concludes that the position of non-specification is a noble Sunni school upon which the people of truth have agreed.

Key Terms
الاستواء (istawa)
الاستقرار المكاني (spatial settlement)
استولى (istawla)
البدعة (bid'a)
التنزيه (tanzih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any specification of a ta'wil is always forbidden and that those who specified (like al-Juwayni) were innovators.
Correction: The author clarifies that specification is acceptable if there is evidence (linguistic or otherwise) that preponderates it. Only specification without evidence is bid'a.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is specifying a ta'wil without evidence considered bad?
A) It causes confusion among the common people
B) It is innovation in religion and great audacity
C) It contradicts the early scholars
D) It is linguistically impossible

Key Takeaway
The early scholars' method of tafwid, combined with firm tanzih, is the safest and most agreed-upon approach to ambiguous verses, but specification with evidence is not rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'istawa' in the Quran, do you feel a tension in your heart? What is your immediate assumption about its meaning? How does this lesson challenge your natural tendency?
?
Are you comfortable with not having a precise mental image of Allah's actions? Does tafwid feel like uncertainty or security to you? Be honest.
?
If you have ever been quick to accept a particular interpretation of a divine attribute without verifying evidence, what does that say about your commitment to the sunni methodology described here?

The Four Pillars of Islamic Jurisprudence
Sanusi enumerates the four sources of rulings and commends following the Salaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four primary sources of Islamic rulings (usul al-ahkam) and the importance of following the righteous predecessors for salvation.

Core Idea
Islamic law rests on four foundations: Quran, Sunna, Consensus, and Analogy.

Sanusi begins by listing the fundamental sources from which Islamic rulings are derived. These four sources—the Book, the Sunna, the consensus of the community, and the analogy of the imams—form the bedrock of Islamic jurisprudence. He then urges believers to follow the path of the righteous predecessors, as clinging to their way leads to salvation.

Guided Path
1
Learn the four sources
Memorize the four items listed: الكتاب (the Quran), السنة (the Prophet's practice), إجماع الأمة (scholarly consensus), and قياس الأئمة (analogical reasoning by qualified imams).
These are known as usul al-ahkam.
2
Understand their order and importance
Notice the sequence: the Quran is first, then Sunna, then consensus, then analogy. This hierarchy reflects the primacy of revelation.
3
Reflect on the role of the Salaf
The passage emphasizes that following the Salaf (righteous predecessors) is a means of salvation. Consider why their example is so important.
The Salaf include the Companions and early generations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم: أن أصول الأحكام التي منها نتلقى: الكتاب، والسنة، وإجماع الأمة، وقياس الأئمة.. واتباع السلف الصالح واقتفاء آثارهم نجاة لمن تمسك به. وأفضل الناس بعد نبينا صلى الله عليه وسلم: أبو بكر، ثم عمر، ومختار مالك: الوقف فيما بين عثمان وعلي رضي الله تعالى عنهما وعمن قبلهما والصحابة كلهم عدول أئمة، بأيهم اقتديتم اهتديتم، نفعنا الله بمحبتهم، وأماتنا على سنتهم، وحشرنا في زمرتهم، آمين يا رب العالمين
And know: that the foundations (usul) of the rulings (al-ahkam) from which we derive are: the Book, the Sunna, the consensus of the Community, and the analogy of the imams. And following the righteous predecessors and tracing their footsteps is salvation for whoever clings to it. And the most excellent of people after our Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, are Abu Bakr, then ‘Umar. And the chosen position of Malik is to stop (suspend judgment) concerning what happened between ‘Uthman and ‘Ali, may Allah be pleased with them and with those before them. And all the Companions are upright imams; whichever of them you follow, you are guided. May Allah benefit us through love for them, cause us to die upon their sunna, and gather us in their group, Amen, O Lord of the worlds.
Source pages p. 633

Commentary
This passage is a methodological and creedal statement. It begins by stating the four sources of Islamic rulings (usul al-ahkam): the Quran (Kitab), the Sunna, the consensus of the community (Ijma), and the analogy of the imams (Qiyas). Then it emphasizes the importance of following the righteous predecessors (salaf salih) as a means of salvation. Next, it affirms the hierarchy of the Companions: Abu Bakr is the best after the Prophet, then ‘Umar. Regarding the conflict between ‘Uthman and ‘Ali, the author states that the chosen position of Imam Malik is to suspend judgment (al-waqf), meaning not to take sides or condemn either. This is important for Sunni creed regarding the Companions. Then it declares that all Companions are upright (udul) and reliable imams; following any of them leads to guidance. The passage ends with a supplication for benefit, death upon their sunna, and being gathered with them.

Key Terms
أصول الأحكام
الكتاب
السنة
إجماع الأمة
قياس الأئمة
السلف الصالح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Quran and Sunna are the only sources of Islamic law, ignoring consensus and analogy.
Correction: This passage explicitly includes ijma and qiyas as legitimate foundations, showing that Islamic law also derives from scholarly consensus and analogical reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, which of the following is NOT one of the foundations of rulings (usul al-ahkam)?
A) The Book (al-Kitab)
B) The Sunna
C) Personal opinion (ra'y)
D) Consensus of the community (ijma)

Key Takeaway
Islamic law is derived from four primary sources: the Quran, Sunna, consensus, and analogy, and following the Salaf is a safeguard for correct understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a new situation in your life, do you actively seek guidance from the Quran and Sunna, or do you rely on your own opinions? What would change if you made them your primary reference?
?
Sanusi says that following the Salaf is 'salvation for those who adhere to it.' Are you truly committed to learning and following their path, or do you pick and choose based on convenience? Ask yourself: what is one area where your practice differs from the Salaf, and what step can you take today to align it?
?
The four sources – Quran, Sunna, consensus, and analogy – are meant to guide your every action. Is there a ruling or practice you follow that is not based on these sources? How can you verify its foundation and bring it in line with the methodology of the Salaf?

The Virtues and Hierarchy of the Companions
Sanusi states the hierarchy of the best Companions, Malik's position on the conflict, and the trustworthiness of all Sahaba.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni creed regarding the hierarchy of the Companions, especially the excellence of Abu Bakr and Umar, the position of suspending judgment on the conflicts, and the reliability of all Companions.

Core Idea
The Companions are all upright; the best are Abu Bakr then Umar, and we remain silent about their disputes.

After laying the methodological foundation, Sanusi turns to the creedal stance on the Companions. He affirms the well-known hierarchy—Abu Bakr is the best after the Prophet, followed by Umar—and shares Imam Malik's prudent position of suspending judgment regarding the conflicts between Uthman and Ali. Finally, he declares that all Companions are trustworthy, and following any of them leads to guidance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hierarchy
The passage states: 'The most excellent of people after our Prophet are Abu Bakr, then Umar.' Note the order. It does not mention Uthman and Ali explicitly but hints at them.
This is the settled Sunni view without mentioning Uthman and Ali's relative rank, which is left as a subject of ikhtilaf.
2
Understand waqf (suspension)
Imam Malik's chosen position is 'al-waqf' regarding what happened between Uthman and Ali. This means we do not take sides or condemn either; we leave the matter to Allah.
This avoids controversy and respects both Companions.
3
Absorb the affirmation of all Companions
The passage says all Companions are 'udul (upright) and 'imams' (leaders). Whichever you follow, you are guided. This affirms their reliability.
This is a standard Sunni position that protects the honor of all Sahaba.

The Four Sources of Islamic Rulings
Sanusi enumerates the four sources of Islamic law and affirms the importance of following the salaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four foundational sources of Islamic law as identified by Sanusi: the Quran, Sunnah, consensus, and analogical reasoning, and the importance of following the righteous predecessors.

Core Idea
The four sources are the Book, Sunnah, consensus, and qiyas; following the salaf is salvation.

Sanusi opens his creedal text by establishing the epistemological foundations of Islamic rulings. Before discussing specific beliefs, he reminds us that all sound doctrine and law derive from these four sources. He then emphasizes that clinging to the path of the righteous predecessors (salaf) is the key to salvation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall flow. Then read the translation to understand the meaning.
Notice the concise style of Sanusi.
2
Identify the four sources
The passage lists four specific sources. Write them down in order: Book, Sunnah, consensus, and qiyas.
These are the classical uṣūl al-fiqh sources.
3
Reflect on the mention of the salaf
After listing the sources, Sanusi immediately mentions following the righteous predecessors. Ask yourself why this connection is made.
The salaf represent the proper application of these sources.
4
Consider the pedagogical purpose
Think about why this passage appears at the beginning of a creedal work. What does it establish before deeper theological discussions?
It sets the methodology before discussing specific beliefs.

Know: that the foundations of rulings from which we receive are: the Book, the Sunnah, the consensus of the Ummah, and the analogy of the imams. And following the righteous predecessors and tracing their traces is salvation for whoever clings to it. And the best of people after our Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, is Abu Bakr, then Umar, and the choice of Malik is to stop regarding what is between Uthman and Ali, may Allah be pleased with them and with those before them. And all the Companions are trustworthy imams; whichever of them you follow, you are guided. We ask Him, glorified, to benefit by it through His grace, and to expand the chest of everyone who strives to acquire it by His bounty. O Allah, bless our master and patron Muhammad as many times as the number of times the rememberers have remembered You and remembered him, and as many times as the number of times the heedless have been heedless of Your remembrance and his remembrance. And be pleased with his family and Companions, and praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds.

The Creed of Ahl al-Tawhid regarding the Companions
Sanusi presents the Sunni creed regarding the Companions, including their ranking and trustworthiness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position on the ranking of the Companions, the stance of Imam Malik on the issue of Uthman and Ali, and the belief that all Companions are trustworthy, as affirmed in the creed of Ahl al-Tawhid.

Core Idea
The best after the Prophet is Abu Bakr, then Umar; all Companions are trustworthy imams.

After establishing the sources, Sanusi turns to the creed of Ahl al-Tawhid concerning the Companions. He affirms the well-known ranking of Abu Bakr and Umar, mentions Imam Malik's position of suspending judgment between Uthman and Ali, and declares all Companions as trustworthy guides. He concludes with supplications and a statement that this creed saves from ignorance and innovation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the ranking
Read the Arabic and translation. Pay attention to the order of the four Khulafā' mentioned.
The ranking is: Abu Bakr, then Umar.
2
Analyze the statement about Uthman and Ali
Sanusi records Imam Malik's position: 'الوقف فيما بين عثمان وعلي' – to stop (suspend judgment) between them. Think about why this is important in Sunni creed.
This avoids partisanship and fosters unity.
3
Reflect on the description of all Companions
The passage says all Companions are 'عدول أئمة' (trustworthy imams) and that following any of them leads to guidance. What does this imply about their reliability?
It affirms their collective integrity.
4
Consider the concluding supplication and creed declaration
The author prays for benefit and declares this the creed of Ahl al-Tawhid that humiliates innovators. Notice how this closes the text.
The prayer emphasizes love and following the Sunnah.

واعلم : أنَّ أصول الأحكام التي منها نتلقى : الكتاب ، والسنة ، وإجماع الأمة ، وقياس الأئمة .. واتباع السلف الصالح واقتفاء آثار هم نجاةٌ لَمَنْ تمسَّكَ به . وأفضلُ الناس بعد نبينا صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ : أبو بكرٍ، ثم عمرُ ، ومختارُ مالكِ : الوقفُ فيما بينَ عثمان وعلي رضي الله تعالى عنهما وعمَّنْ قبلهما والصحابة كلهم عدول أئمة ، بأيهم اقتديتم اهتديتُم ، نفعنا الله بمحبَّتِهم ، وأماتنا على سُنَّتِهم ، وحشرنا في زمرتهم ، آمين يا ربَّ العالمينَ فهذه عقيدة أهل التوحيد » ، المُخرجة بفضْلِ اللهِ « تعالى مِنْ ظلماتِ الجهل والتقليد ، المُرغِمةُ بعونِ اللهِ أَنفَ كل مبتدع عنيد ، نسأله سبحانَهُ أنْ ينفع بها بفضلِهِ ، ويشرح بها صدرَ كلَّ مَنْ يسعى في تحصيلها بطَوْلِهِ . وصل اللهم على سيّدِنا ومولانا محمد عدد ما ذكرَكَ وذكره الذاكرونَ ، وعدد ما غفل عن ذكرِكَ وذكره الغافلونَ ورضي الله عن آلِهِ وصحبه ، والحمدُ للهِ ربِّ العالمينَ .
Know: that the foundations of rulings from which we receive are: the Book, the Sunnah, the consensus of the Ummah, and the analogy of the imams. And following the righteous predecessors and tracing their traces is salvation for whoever clings to it. And the best of people after our Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, is Abu Bakr, then Umar, and the choice of Malik is to stop regarding what is between Uthman and Ali, may Allah be pleased with them and with those before them. And all the Companions are trustworthy imams; whichever of them you follow, you are guided. May Allah benefit us with their love, cause us to die upon their Sunnah, and gather us in their company, Amen, O Lord of the worlds. This is the creed of the people of Tawhid, brought out by the grace of Allah from the darknesses of ignorance and blind following, and by the help of Allah humiliating the nose of every stubborn innovator. We ask Him, glorified, to benefit by it through His grace, and to expand the chest of everyone who strives to acquire it by His bounty. O Allah, bless our master and patron Muhammad as many times as the number of times the rememberers have remembered You and remembered him, and as many times as the number of times the heedless have been heedless of Your remembrance and his remembrance. And be pleased with his family and Companions, and praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds.
Source pages p. 633

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the four fundamental sources of Islamic jurisprudence: the Quran, the Sunnah, the consensus of the Ummah, and the analogy of the imams (Qiyas). It then emphasizes that following the righteous predecessors (Salaf al-Salih) is salvation. Next, it declares the best of people after the Prophet as Abu Bakr, then Umar, and mentions the position of Imam Malik to refrain from preferring either Uthman or Ali one over the other. It affirms that all the Companions are trustworthy and guides. The passage concludes with a supplication for benefit through their love and for death upon their Sunnah, then presents the text as 'the creed of the people of Tawhid' that removes ignorance and blind following, and humiliates innovators, followed by prayers upon the Prophet and praise to Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that not all Companions are trustworthy transmitters.
Correction: The passage states that all Companions are 'trustworthy imams' (عدول أئمة) and that following any of them leads to guidance.

Checkpoint
What is the position of Imam Malik regarding Uthman and Ali according to Sanusi?
A) Uthman is better
B) Ali is better
C) Stop (suspend judgment)
D) They are equal but Uthman is better

Key Takeaway
The creed of Ahl al-Tawhid holds that Abu Bakr and Umar are the best, suspends judgment between Uthman and Ali, and affirms all Companions as trustworthy guides.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that Abu Bakr and Umar are the best after the Prophet, and that all Companions are trustworthy imams. In your heart, do you truly honor them above everyone else? What if you find a thought or a comment from others that diminishes their rank? How will you actively guard your tongue and your heart against belittling any Companion, especially in casual talk? Make a specific pledge to correct yourself when you hear such talk.
?
The passage says: 'بأيهم اقتديتم اهتديتم' — whichever of the Companions you follow, you are guided. Are you actually following any of them in your daily life? Not just their names, but their example in worship, ethics, and dealings? If you are not, what concrete step will you take this week to learn from the life of one Companion (maybe Abu Bakr's courage or Umar's justice) and implement it?
?
Imam Malik's position is to stop — to refrain from ranking Uthman and Ali against each other. Is there a conflict or disagreement among people you know (maybe in your family or community) about such historical figures? How can you, like Malik, suspend judgment and avoid taking sides in a way that divides? Write down one situation where you will consciously practice 'الوقف' this week, even if you have a personal preference.
